## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are **memoized recursion (dynamic programming) on frequency states**. 

- **State representation**: The state is a 6-tuple representing the counts of dice faces 1 through 6 (each 0-6, sum ≤6). This compresses the dice multiset into a compact, hashable form.
- **Transitions**: From each state, enumerate all possible **disjoint combinations** as moves:
  - Single 1 (+100 pts, reduce count[1] by 1).
  - Single 5 (+50 pts, reduce count[5] by 1).
  - n-of-a-kind for face `f` and `n=3..6` ((n-2) × base[f] pts, reduce count[f] by n).
  - Straight (if all counts ≥1, +1000 pts, reduce each by 1).
  - Three pairs (choose 3 distinct faces each ≥2, +750 pts, reduce each by 2).
- **Recurrence**: `dp(state) = max(0, score(move) + dp(state after move))` over all valid moves.
- **Base case**: All counts=0 → 0 pts.
- **Optimization**: `@lru_cache` for memoization (≤7⁶=117k states, but <<1k visited). Exhaustive but efficient due to small depth (≤6) and branching (≤50).
- **Output**: If max score=0, "Zonk"; else the score.
This is a classic **subset/partition DP** for max value with predefined "items" (combinations).

## Tutorial
### Memoized Recursion for Combinatorial Scoring Problems
Many problems (dice games, resource partitioning, games like Nim) involve maximizing score by selecting non-overlapping actions/subsets from limited items (here, dice). With small N (≤6 dice), **top-down DP with memoization** is ideal over bottom-up (fewer states computed).

1. **Define State**: Use minimal tuple (e.g., frequencies) instead of permutations/sets. Here, `(c1,c2,c3,c4,c5,c6)` uniquely represents available dice.
2. **Hashable for Memo**: Use tuple/list slices; Python's `@lru_cache(None)` auto-memos hashable args (ints fine).
3. **Enumerate Transitions**: List all atomic moves (combinations). Each move:
   - Checks feasibility (e.g., `count >= k`).
   - Computes score.
   - Generates new state (copy list, modify).
   - Recurses.
4. **Avoid Cycles/Invalid**: Always subtract positively; base case is zero-state.
5. **Max/Aggregate**: `max_score = max(max_score, score + recurse(new_state))`. Include "do nothing" (0).
6. **Efficiency**: Prune impossible (e.g., `if cf < k: skip`). Order moves high-score first for minor speedup.
7. **Implementation Tips**:
   - Nested func with `@lru_cache` for local memo.
   - Copy lists shallow (`cs[:]`).
   - Clear cache if multiple queries (`dp.cache_clear()`).
   - Debug: Print states or use `sys.setrecursionlimit(10000)`.

**Simpler Example**: Max score with only 1's (100 each or 1000 for ≥3).
```python
@lru_cache(None)
def dp(c1):  # only 1's
    if c1 == 0: return 0
    maxs = 100 + dp(c1-1) if c1 >=1 else 0  # single
    if c1 >=3: maxs = max(maxs, 1000 + dp(c1-3))
    return maxs
```
For c1=5: tries 100+dp(4)=100+(100+dp(3))=...=500 vs 1000+dp(2)=1000+200=1200 vs 1000+100+dp(1)=... max=1200? Wait, but with 5-of better in real.

Scale to multi-dim by unpacking tuple args. Handles overlaps automatically (recursion tries combos).

Common pitfalls: Mutable state mutation (always copy), negative counts (check before), overcounting (max not sum).

## Example Problems
### 1. LeetCode 740: Delete and Earn
**Problem**: Given array `nums` (1≤nums[i]≤10^4, n≤10^4), maximize points: pick `nums[i]`, earn `nums[i]` pts but delete all `nums[i]-1` and `nums[i]+1`. Can't pick adjacent values. Return max points.

**Solution**: Group by frequency: `points[val] = val * freq[val]`. Sort unique vals. DP like House Robber: `dp[i] = max(dp[i-1], points[vals[i]] + dp[i-2])` (skip or take, can't adjacent). O(max_val) space/time optimizable. Freq compression like here; linear DP on values.

```python
from collections import Counter
class Solution:
    def deleteAndEarn(self, nums):
        if not nums: return 0
        cnt = Counter(nums)
        vals = sorted(cnt)
        if len(vals) == 1: return vals[0] * cnt[vals[0]]
        dp = [0] * len(vals)
        dp[0] = vals[0] * cnt[vals[0]]
        dp[1] = max(vals[0] * cnt[vals[0]], vals[1] * cnt[vals[1]])
        for i in range(2, len(vals)):
            prev2, prev1 = dp[i-2], dp[i-1]
            take = vals[i] * cnt[vals[i]] + prev2
            dp[i] = max(prev1, take)
        return dp[-1]
```

### 2. LeetCode 698: Partition to K Equal Sum Subsets
**Problem**: Given `nums` (n≤16, 1≤nums[i]≤10^4), partition into `k` subsets each summing to `total//k` (if possible). Return True/False.

**Solution**: Bitmask DP: state `mask` (used items), `rem` (remaining subsets to fill), `cur_sum` (current subset sum). Recurse: try add unused num to current if fits. Memo `(mask, rem, cur_sum)`. 2^16 * k * (total/k) ≈ 10^7 ok. Like here, recursion on subsets/moves with mask state.

```python
class Solution:
    def canPartitionKSubsets(self, nums, k):
        total = sum(nums)
        if total % k: return False
        target = total // k
        n = len(nums)
        memo = {}

        def dfs(mask, rem, cur_sum):
            if rem == 0: return True
            if cur_sum == target: return dfs(mask, rem - 1, 0)
            key = (mask, rem, cur_sum)
            if key in memo: return memo[key]
            for i in range(n):
                if (mask & (1 << i)) == 0:
                    if cur_sum + nums[i] > target: continue
                    if dfs(mask | (1 << i), rem, cur_sum + nums[i]):
                        memo[key] = True
                        return True
            memo[key] = False
            return False
        return dfs(0, k, 0)
```

### 3. LeetCode 343: Integer Break
**Problem**: Break positive `n` (2≤n≤58) into ≥2 positives summing to n, maximize product.

**Solution**: Memo recursion: `dp(n) = max(i * dp(n-i) for i=1..n-1)` (split i and recurse rest; also i*(n-i) for no further). Base: dp(2)=1, dp(3)=2. Like partitioning dice into "breaks" for max score. O(n^2) fills.

```python
from functools import lru_cache
class Solution:
    def integerBreak(self, n: int) -> int:
        @lru_cache(None)
        def dp(x):
            if x == 2: return 1
            if x == 3: return 2
            max_prod = 0
            for i in range(1, x):
                max_prod = max(max_prod, i * dp(x - i), i * (x - i))
            return max_prod
        return dp(n)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Count frequencies of 1-6. Use memoized recursion `dp(c1,c2,c3,c4,c5,c6)` computing max score from that state. Try all combinations as transitions, recurse on reduced counts, take max. `@lru_cache` memos states. Parse stdin as list via `ast.literal_eval`. Output score or "Zonk". Handles all cases (e.g., four 2's + two 6's: 400, no three pairs since <3 faces ≥2; five 1's +5: 3050).

```python
import sys
import itertools
from functools import lru_cache
import ast

def get_score(dice):
    freq = [0] * 7
    for d in dice:
        if 1 <= d <= 6:
            freq[d] += 1
    bases = [0, 1000, 200, 300, 400, 500, 600]

    @lru_cache(None)
    def dp(c1, c2, c3, c4, c5, c6):
        cs = [c1, c2, c3, c4, c5, c6]
        maxs = 0
        # Single 1
        if c1 > 0:
            maxs = max(maxs, 100 + dp(c1 - 1, c2, c3, c4, c5, c6))
        # Single 5
        if c5 > 0:
            maxs = max(maxs, 50 + dp(c1, c2, c3, c4, c5 - 1, c6))
        # n-of-a-kind
        for f in range(1, 7):
            idx = f - 1
            cf = cs[idx]
            for k in range(3, 7):
                if cf >= k:
                    score = (k - 2) * bases[f]
                    new_cs = cs[:]
                    new_cs[idx] -= k
                    maxs = max(maxs, score + dp(*new_cs))
        # Straight
        if all(c > 0 for c in cs):
            maxs = max(maxs, 1000 + dp(c1 - 1, c2 - 1, c3 - 1, c4 - 1, c5 - 1, c6 - 1))
        # Three pairs (three distinct faces >=2 each)
        for comb in itertools.combinations(range(6), 3):
            if all(cs[i] >= 2 for i in comb):
                new_cs = cs[:]
                for i in comb:
                    new_cs[i] -= 2
                maxs = max(maxs, 750 + dp(*new_cs))
        return maxs

    res = dp(*freq[1:])
    return res if res > 0 else "Zonk"

data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
if data:
    dice = ast.literal_eval(data)
    print(get_score(dice))
```

## Algorithms
- **String splitting on single space delimiter (`split(' ')`)**: Unlike `split()` (which collapses whitespace), `split(' ')` treats each space as a delimiter, producing empty strings for consecutive/multiple spaces, leading/trailing spaces. This preserves exact spacing when re-joining with `' '`.
- **Conditional word transformation**: For each token (word or empty string), check length `> 2`. If true, capitalize first character (`upper()`) and lowercase the rest (`lower()`); otherwise, leave unchanged.
- **Joining with space**: `' '.join()` reconstructs the string, restoring exact original spacing via empty tokens.
- **Edge case handling**: Leading/trailing/multiple spaces, mixed case, short words (≤2 chars), guaranteed at least one word.

## Tutorial
The key challenge is manipulating words individually while **preserving exact whitespace** (leading, trailing, multiple spaces). Standard `split()` collapses whitespace into single gaps, losing information. Instead:

1. **Split precisely**: Use `s.split(' ')` to get a list of tokens where:
   - Non-empty tokens are words.
   - Empty strings `''` represent each space (e.g., `"one   space".split(' ')` → `['one', '', '', 'space']`).

2. **Process conditionally**:
   - Iterate tokens.
   - For `len(token) > 2`: Transform to `token[0].upper() + token[1:].lower()` (DropCaps rule).
   - Empty/short tokens: Keep as-is.
   - This handles mixed case (e.g., `'WALK'` → `'Walk'`).

3. **Reconstruct**: `' '.join(tokens)` restores spacing perfectly (e.g., `['One', '', '', 'Space']` → `"One   Space"`).

**Advantages**: O(n) time (single pass), no regex/import, handles all edges (spaces, case, short words).
**Alternatives**:
- **Regex**: `re.sub(r'(\S{3,})', lambda m: m.group(1)[0].upper() + m.group(1)[1:].lower(), s)` (matches non-spaces ≥3 chars).
- **Manual iteration**: Track in-word state with index/pointers (more code, same effect).
**Pitfalls**: `split()` or `split(None)` loses spaces; always test multiples/edges.

This technique is common in string problems requiring whitespace fidelity (e.g., word reversal, selective edits).

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Reverse Words Preserving Spaces
**Problem Description**: Given a string, reverse the order of words (non-empty sequences of non-spaces) while preserving exact spacing, including multiple spaces, leading, and trailing spaces. Input is a single line from stdin; output the modified string to stdout.

**Examples**:
- Input: `hello   world` → Output: `world   hello`
- Input: `  a b  c  ` → Output: `  c b a  `

**Solution Explanation**: Split on `' '` to get tokens (words + empty strings for spaces). Collect non-empty words in a list, reverse it, then rebuild by placing reversed words back into positions of original non-empty tokens (empty tokens stay). This preserves spacing. O(n) time/space.

```python
s = input()
tokens = s.split(' ')
words = [t for t in tokens if t]
words.reverse()
new_tokens = []
word_idx = 0
for t in tokens:
    if t:
        new_tokens.append(words[word_idx])
        word_idx += 1
    else:
        new_tokens.append('')
print(' '.join(new_tokens))
```

### Example Problem 2: Reverse Letters in Long Words
**Problem Description**: Reverse the letters in every word longer than 2 characters, leave shorter words unchanged, and preserve all spacing (multiple/leading/trailing spaces). Input: single line from stdin; output: modified string to stdout.

**Examples**:
- Input: `apple of banana` → Output: `elppa of ananab`
- Input: `one   space` → Output: `eno   ecaeps`
- Input: `  ab CD   ` → Output: `  ab dC   `

**Solution Explanation**: Split on `' '` for tokens. For each non-empty token with `len > 2`, reverse it (`[::-1]`); else keep as-is. Join with `' '`. Handles case/mixed chars naturally. O(n) time.

```python
s = input()
tokens = s.split(' ')
new_tokens = []
for t in tokens:
    if len(t) > 2:
        new_tokens.append(t[::-1])
    else:
        new_tokens.append(t)
print(' '.join(new_tokens))
```

### Example Problem 3: Replace Long Words with Length
**Problem Description**: Replace every word longer than 2 characters with its length as a string (e.g., "apple" → "5"), leave shorter words unchanged, preserve all spacing. Input: single line from stdin; output: modified string to stdout.

**Examples**:
- Input: `apple of banana` → Output: `5 of 6`
- Input: `one   space hi` → Output: `3   5 hi`
- Input: `   longWORD   ` → Output: `   8   `

**Solution Explanation**: Split on `' '` for tokens. For non-empty `len(t) > 2`, append `str(len(t))`; else append `t`. Join with `' '`. Simple conditional transform. O(n) time.

```python
s = input()
tokens = s.split(' ')
new_tokens = []
for t in tokens:
    if len(t) > 2:
        new_tokens.append(str(len(t)))
    else:
        new_tokens.append(t)
print(' '.join(new_tokens))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read input string `s`. Split on `' '` to preserve spacing via tokens (empty for spaces). For each token: if `len > 2`, apply DropCaps (`[0].upper() + [1:].lower()`); else unchanged. Join with `' '` and print. This correctly handles all cases (spaces, case, short words) in O(n) time without imports.

```python
s = input()
tokens = s.split(' ')
new_tokens = []
for t in tokens:
    if len(t) > 2:
        new_tokens.append(t[0].upper() + t[1:].lower())
    else:
        new_tokens.append(t)
print(' '.join(new_tokens))
```

## Algorithms
- **Palindrome Symmetry**: A string is a palindrome if characters at symmetric positions `s[i]` and `s[n-1-i]` are equal for all `i` from 0 to `n//2 - 1`.
- **Pairwise Possibility Checking**: For each symmetric pair `(i, n-1-i)`, compute the set of possible transformed characters (1 or 2 options per position, handling edge cases for 'a' and 'z'). Check if the intersection of these sets is non-empty.
- **Set Intersection**: Efficiently verifies if there exists at least one common character choice for each pair using Python's set operations (`&`).
- **Two-Pointer Adaptation**: Iterate only up to `n//2`, pairing positions without generating all `2^n` possibilities, achieving O(n) time complexity.

## Tutorial
To solve this problem, recognize that generating all possible transformed strings (up to `2^n` for string length `n`) is inefficient for larger `n`, but the palindrome constraint allows a smarter approach:

1. **Define Possible Transformations**:
   - For a character `c`:
     - If `c == 'a'`: only `'b'`.
     - If `c == 'z'`: only `'y'`.
     - Else: `chr(ord(c)-1)` or `chr(ord(c)+1)`.
   - Store as a set: `possible(c)` returns `{'b'}`, `{'y'}`, or `{prev, next}`.

2. **Palindrome Requirement**:
   - For the transformed string to be a palindrome, `transformed[i] == transformed[n-1-i]` for all `i < n//2`.
   - Middle character (if `n` odd) can be anything (always possible).

3. **Independent Pair Checking**:
   - Pairs `(i, n-1-i)` are disjoint, so check each independently.
   - For each pair, compute `poss_i = possible(s[i])` and `poss_j = possible(s[n-1-i])`.
   - If `poss_i & poss_j` is empty, impossible for this pair → return `False`.
   - If all pairs have non-empty intersection, choose a common character for each pair → `True`.

4. **Edge Cases**:
   - `n == 1`: Always `True`.
   - `n == 0`: Unlikely, but `True`.
   - All 'a's paired: intersect at 'b'.

5. **Complexity**:
   - Time: O(n), since set operations are O(1) (sets size ≤2).
   - Space: O(1).

This reduces exponential search to linear verification.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Rearrange String to Palindrome (Similar to Leetcode 1332. Remove Palindromic Subsequences or standard rearrangement check)
**Problem Description**: Given a string `s` of lowercase English letters, determine if it is possible to rearrange the characters to form a palindrome. Input is a single line string from stdin; output `True` or `False`.

**Solution Explanation**: In a palindrome, all characters except at most one must appear an even number of times (pair symmetric positions). Use a frequency counter; count characters with odd frequencies. If ≤1, `True`; else `False`. Time: O(n), Space: O(1) or O(26).

```python
from collections import Counter
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
s = input().strip()
freq = Counter(s)
odd_count = sum(1 for count in freq.values() if count % 2 == 1)
print(odd_count <= 1)
```

### Example Problem 2: Valid Palindrome II (Leetcode 680)
**Problem Description**: Given a string `s` consisting of lowercase letters, digits, and symbols, return `True` if it can be a palindrome after deleting at most one character (ignoring non-alphanumeric, case-insensitive). Input: single line string from stdin; output `True`/`False`.

**Solution Explanation**: Use two pointers `left` and `right`. Skip non-alphanumeric characters, compare lowercase. On mismatch, check if skipping `left` or `right` makes the rest a palindrome (recursive helper). Memoization optional for efficiency. Handles symmetric mismatches by trying one deletion per pair. Time: O(n), Space: O(n) recursion.

```python
import sys
import re

def is_palindrome(s, left, right):
    while left < right:
        while left < right and not s[left].isalnum():
            left += 1
        while left < right and not s[right].isalnum():
            right -= 1
        if left >= right:
            return True
        if s[left].lower() != s[right].lower():
            return False
        left += 1
        right -= 1
    return True

input = sys.stdin.read
s = input().strip()
n = len(s)
left, right = 0, n - 1
while left < right:
    while left < right and not s[left].isalnum():
        left += 1
    while left < right and not s[right].isalnum():
        right -= 1
    if left >= right:
        print(True)
        sys.exit(0)
    if s[left].lower() != s[right].lower():
        # Try skipping left or right
        print(is_palindrome(s, left + 1, right) or is_palindrome(s, left, right - 1))
        sys.exit(0)
    left += 1
    right -= 1
print(True)
```

### Example Problem 3: Check if Binary String Can Be Palindrome with At Most K Flips
**Problem Description**: Given a binary string `s` (0s and 1s) and integer `k`, return `True` if it can be made a palindrome by flipping at most `k` bits (0↔1). Input: first line `s`, second line `k`. Output `True`/`False`. (Relevant CP style, e.g., similar to Codeforces problems on bit flips for palindrome.)

**Solution Explanation**: For each symmetric pair `(i, n-1-i)`:
- If `s[i] == s[n-1-i]`: 0 flips.
- Else: 1 flip (change one to match the other).
Sum mismatches (each costs 1 flip). Middle ignored. If total ≤ `k`, `True`. Time: O(n).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
s = data[0].strip()
k = int(data[1])
n = len(s)
flips = 0
for i in range(n // 2):
    if s[i] != s[n - 1 - i]:
        flips += 1
        if flips > k:
            print(False)
            sys.exit(0)
print(flips <= k)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem:
- **Explanation of the solution**: Implement `possible(c)` to return the set of valid transformations. Iterate over pairs `(i, n-1-i)` for `i` in `range(n//2)`. For each, check if `possible(s[i]) & possible(s[n-1-i])` is non-empty. If any pair fails, `False`; else `True`. Handles 'a'/'z' edges. Middle position auto-satisfied. Reads input via `sys.stdin.read()` for efficiency. O(n) time.

```python
import sys

def possible(c):
    if c == 'a':
        return {'b'}
    if c == 'z':
        return {'y'}
    o = ord(c)
    return {chr(o - 1), chr(o + 1)}

input = sys.stdin.read
s = input().strip()
n = len(s)
ans = True
for i in range(n // 2):
    poss_i = possible(s[i])
    poss_j = possible(s[n - 1 - i])
    if not (poss_i & poss_j):
        ans = False
        break
print(ans)
```

## Algorithms
- **String conversion for digit extraction**: Convert the input integer to a string to easily access each digit from left to right (most significant to least significant).
- **Place value computation**: For each digit position `i` (0-based from left), compute the power `p = len(s) - 1 - i`. Represent `10^p` as a string (`'1' + '0' * p`) to avoid exponent notation.
- **Conditional term building**: Only include terms for non-zero digits. For `p > 0`, format as `"digit*power_str"`; for `p == 0`, use just `"digit"`.
- **String joining**: Collect terms in a list and join with `'+'` for the final expression.
- **Edge case handling**: Empty string for input `0`.
This approach is O(D) time where D is the number of digits (up to ~10^5 for large inputs, but typically small), efficient for competitive programming constraints.

## Tutorial
Processing numbers digit-by-digit is a fundamental technique in competitive programming, especially for problems involving sums, counts, palindromes, or custom representations (e.g., up to 10^18 has ~19 digits).

1. **Read input as string**: Use `s = input().strip()` to handle the number directly as digits, avoiding integer overflow or modulo operations.
2. **Iterate over digits**: `for i in range(len(s)): d = int(s[i])`. This processes from MSD (most significant digit) to LSD.
3. **Compute place value**: Position from left gives power `p = len(s) - 1 - i`. To get `10^p` as string: `power_str = '1' + '0' * p` (faster than `str(10**p)` for large p).
4. **Build terms conditionally**: Skip zeros to match the problem. Format based on `p`:
   - `p == 0`: `str(d)`
   - `p > 0`: `f"{d}*{power_str}"`
5. **Join and output**: `'+'.join(terms)` for the sum expression.
6. **Edge cases**: `if s == '0': print('')`. Handles leading zeros implicitly (but input is integer, so none).
   
This scales to 10^6 digits if needed (rare). Alternative: modulo/reverse for integers, but string is simpler/cleaner for formatting.

Common pitfalls: Forgetting `p=0` case, using `**` in output (must be flat `10000`), skipping zeros incorrectly.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Digit Sum (Common in CP, e.g., CodeChef "DIGSUM")
**Problem Description**: The first line contains T (1 ≤ T ≤ 10^5), the number of test cases. Each of the next T lines contains an integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^18). For each test case, output the sum of the digits of N.

**Solution Explanation**: Convert N to string for O(log N) digit access. Iterate over each character, convert to int, and sum. Handles large N efficiently without loops/modulo (which are O(log N) too but messier for formatting). Time: O(T * 18) = fine.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    N_str = data[index]
    index += 1
    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in N_str)
    print(digit_sum)
```

### Example Problem 2: Palindrome Number Check (LeetCode 9 / CP variant)
**Problem Description**: The first line contains T (1 ≤ T ≤ 10^5). Each of T lines has N (0 ≤ N ≤ 10^18). Output "YES" if the decimal representation of N is a palindrome (reads same forwards/backwards), else "NO".

**Solution Explanation**: Convert to string `s = str(N)`. Check `s == s[::-1]`. String reversal is O(D), efficient. Handles leading zeros? No, since integer input. Edge: 0 is palindrome.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    s = data[index]
    index += 1
    if s == s[::-1]:
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
```

### Example Problem 3: Count Digit Occurrences (CP style, e.g., "Count of digit K")
**Problem Description**: First line: T (1 ≤ T ≤ 10^5). Each of T lines: two integers N K (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^18, 0 ≤ K ≤ 9). Output the number of times digit K appears in N's decimal representation.

**Solution Explanation**: String conversion. Iterate digits, count matches to K. O(D) per test. Use `str.count(str(K))` for simplicity (since single digit).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    N_str = data[index]
    K = data[index + 1]
    index += 2
    count = N_str.count(K)
    print(count)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution:
Read input as string `s`. If `s == '0'`, output empty string. Else, iterate over each digit position from left. For non-zero digit `d` at position `i`, compute power `p = len(s)-1-i`. If `p == 0`, add `str(d)`; else add `f"{d}*{'1'+'0'*p}"` to list `terms`. Finally, print `'+'.join(terms)`. This exactly matches the format, skips zeros, and handles all cases in O(D) time.

```python
s = input().strip()
if s == '0':
    print('')
else:
    terms = []
    n = len(s)
    for i in range(n):
        d = int(s[i])
        if d != 0:
            p = n - 1 - i
            if p == 0:
                terms.append(str(d))
            else:
                power_str = '1' + '0' * p
                terms.append(f"{d}*{power_str}")
    print('+'.join(terms))
```
completed 4/150, Solved: True, number of success = 1/4, acc = 25.0
## Algorithms
The core concepts revolve around **string isomorphism under substitution ciphers**, which checks if two strings can be transformed into each other via a consistent and injective character mapping (a partial bijection between used characters). Key algorithms/steps:
- **Mapping Consistency**: Ensure every occurrence of a character in `string1` maps to the same character in `string2`.
- **Injectivity Check**: Ensure no two distinct characters in `string1` map to the same character in `string2`.
- **Implementation**: Use a dictionary to track the mapping from `string1` chars to `string2` chars while iterating positions, and a set to track used `string2` chars for injectivity. This is O(n) time, where n ≤ 10, and space O(26) since lowercase letters.
This is equivalent to checking if the strings have the same "pattern" (e.g., both follow "a a b c" structure under relabeling). No need for full permutations or backtracking due to small constraints.

## Tutorial
### Solving Substitution Cipher Isomorphism Problems
Substitution ciphers require a **bijective mapping** (permutation) of the alphabet. For short strings, we only care about the **induced partial mapping** from positions:
1. **Iterate positions** `i` from 0 to `len(s1)-1`:
   - Let `c1 = s1[i]`, `c2 = s2[i]`.
   - If `c1` is already mapped, check if it matches `c2`. If not, inconsistent → return `False`.
   - If `c1` is new:
     - Check if `c2` is already used by another `c1`. If yes, violates injectivity → `False`.
     - Assign `mapping[c1] = c2` and mark `c2` as used.
2. **Why this works**:
   - **Consistency**: Same `c1` always gets same `c2`.
   - **Injectivity**: `used` set ensures distinct `c1` get distinct `c2`.
   - **Bidirectionality**: Automatically satisfied (inverse mapping is well-defined and injective on used chars).
   - **Extendability**: Unused letters can be permuted freely to make full bijection.
3. **Edge Cases**:
   - All identical chars: e.g., "aaa" → "bbb" (yes), "aaa" → "abc" (no, inconsistent).
   - Single char: always yes.
   - More distinct chars in `s1` than possible mappings: caught by injectivity.
   - Same string: yes (identity mapping).
4. **Optimizations/Alternatives**:
   - Use arrays `map1[26]`, `map2[26]` for O(1) checks (ord(c)-ord('a')).
   - Compress strings to patterns (e.g., "aacb" → "0 0 1 2"), check equality.
   - For longer strings: Same algo scales to 10^5.
5. **Common Pitfalls**: Forgetting injectivity (consistency alone allows "ab" → "aa"); not stripping input.

This pattern appears in isomorphism, pattern matching, and graph labeling problems.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Isomorphic Strings (LeetCode 205 style, adapted to CP input/output)
**Problem**: Given two strings `s` and `t` of equal length (1 ≤ n ≤ 1000, lowercase), return `true` if `s` and `t` are isomorphic (characters in `s` can be replaced to get `t`, preserving order, with consistent 1-1 mapping).  
**Sample Input**:
```
egg
add
```
**Sample Output**:
```
True
```
**Another Sample**:
```
foo
bar
```
**Output**: `False`

**Solution Explanation**: Identical to the original problem. Use a dict for `s → t` mapping and a set for used `t` chars. Iterate zip(s,t), check consistency and injectivity. Bidirectional check optional but equivalent. Time: O(n), Space: O(1) (26 letters).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()

s = data[0].strip()
t = data[1].strip()

def is_isomorphic(s, t):
    if len(s) != len(t):
        return False
    mapping = {}
    used = set()
    for c1, c2 in zip(s, t):
        if c1 in mapping:
            if mapping[c1] != c2:
                return False
        else:
            if c2 in used:
                return False
            mapping[c1] = c2
            used.add(c2)
    return True

print(is_isomorphic(s, t))
```

### Example Problem 2: Word Pattern (LeetCode 290 style, adapted to CP input/output)
**Problem**: Given a `pattern` string and a `str` string (split `str` by space into words), return `true` if `str` matches the pattern under a bijection (each pattern char maps to a unique word, consistent). 1 ≤ pattern ≤ 8, words lowercase, 1-10 words.  
**Sample Input**:
```
abba
dog cat cat dog
```
**Sample Output**:
```
True
```
**Another Sample**:
```
abba
dog cat cat fish
```
**Output**: `False`

**Solution Explanation**: Split `str` into `words`. Map pattern chars to words (dict), check consistency. Map words to pattern chars (reverse dict/set) for injectivity. Iterate `zip(pattern, words)`. Handles variable word lengths. Time: O(m), m=words.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()

pattern = data[0].strip()
str_words = data[1].strip().split()

def word_pattern(pattern, words):
    if len(pattern) != len(words):
        return False
    p_to_w = {}
    w_to_p = {}
    for p, w in zip(pattern, words):
        if p in p_to_w:
            if p_to_w[p] != w:
                return False
        else:
            if w in w_to_p:
                return False
            p_to_w[p] = w
            w_to_p[w] = p
    return True

print(word_pattern(pattern, str_words))
```

### Example Problem 3: String Pattern Compression Match (Common CP pattern, e.g., similar to Codeforces 766C or pattern problems)
**Problem**: Compress a string to its pattern (replace consecutive same chars with count, or rank chars: first unique=0, next=1,...). Check if two strings have the same pattern. Input: two strings same length (1 ≤ n ≤ 100).  
**Sample Input**:
```
aacb
aabc
```
**Sample Output**:
```
True
```
**Another Sample**:
```
aa
bc
```
**Output**: `False`

**Solution Explanation**: For a string, build pattern list: dict char→rank (first seen=0, etc.). Replace each char with its rank. Check if patterns equal. Equivalent to isomorphism (ranks capture structure). Time: O(n).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()

s1 = data[0].strip()
s2 = data[1].strip()

def get_pattern(s):
    rank = {}
    next_rank = 0
    pattern = []
    for c in s:
        if c not in rank:
            rank[c] = next_rank
            next_rank += 1
        pattern.append(rank[c])
    return tuple(pattern)  # tuple for equality

pat1 = get_pattern(s1)
pat2 = get_pattern(s2)
print(pat1 == pat2)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read two strings from stdin. Use a dictionary `mapping` to track `string1` char → `string2` char and a set `used` for `string2` chars already mapped to. Iterate over zipped positions: check consistency (existing map matches), and for new mappings, check `string2` char unused (injectivity). If all pass, return `True`. This verifies a valid substitution cipher exists (extendable to full permutation). Symmetric, O(n) time, handles all cases per examples/edges.

```python
s1 = input().strip()
s2 = input().strip()

def can_obtain_from_each_other(string1, string2):
    if len(string1) != len(string2):
        return False
    mapping = {}
    used = set()
    for c1, c2 in zip(string1, string2):
        if c1 in mapping:
            if mapping[c1] != c2:
                return False
        else:
            if c2 in used:
                return False
            mapping[c1] = c2
            used.add(c2)
    return True

print(can_obtain_from_each_other(s1, s2))
```

## Algorithms
The core concept is a **greedy algorithm** using **sorting of digits in descending order**. Convert the integer to a string to access individual digits, sort the digits from largest to smallest (descending order), and concatenate them back into a string. This maximizes the numerical value because placing the largest digits in the highest place values always yields the optimal result. No additional constraints like leading zeros affect the largest arrangement (zeros will naturally go to the end). Time complexity: O(d log d), where d is the number of digits (efficient for typical constraints).

## Tutorial
### Forming the Largest Number by Rearranging Digits (Greedy Sorting)
**Core Idea**: To maximize the numerical value of a number using exactly its digits, arrange them in **descending order**. This is greedy because:
- The leftmost digit has the highest place value (e.g., 100s > 10s > 1s).
- Prioritizing larger digits leftward always gives a larger number than any other permutation.

**Steps**:
1. Read the input as a **string** (preserves zeros and avoids int conversion issues).
2. Sort the characters (digits) in **descending order** using `sorted(s, reverse=True)`.
3. Join the sorted list into a new string.
4. Output the string (represents the largest integer value).

**Python Example**:
```python
num = "123456"
largest = ''.join(sorted(num, reverse=True))  # ['6','5','4','3','2','1'] -> "654321"
```

**Why it works**:
- "123456" → sorted desc: "654321" (654321 > any other like 654312).
- "105" → "510" (510 > 501 > 150 etc.).
- Single digit "5" → "5" (unchanged).
- Already max "654" → "654" (unchanged).

**Edge Cases**:
- Single digit: trivially sorted.
- Contains zeros: e.g., "102" → "210".
- All zeros: "000" → "000" (but input likely positive integer without leading zeros).

**Complexity**:
- Time: O(d log d) due to sorting (d ≤ 10^5 feasible).
- Space: O(d).

**Variations**:
- **Smallest**: Sort ascending (`sorted(s)`), but for true minimal numerical value with no leading zeros, place smallest non-zero first, then zeros, then rest ascending.
- **Constraints** (e.g., even, ÷3): Adjust by selecting/swapping/removing digits post-sort.

This technique appears in CP for "digit rearrangement" problems.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Smallest Number by Rearranging Digits
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer as a string, rearrange its digits to form the **smallest possible number** (as a string of the same length). Leading zeros are allowed in the output string (e.g., "100" → "001").

**Sample**:
- Input: 100 → Output: 001
- Input: 105 → Output: 015

**Solution Explanation**: To minimize, arrange digits in **ascending order**. This places smallest digits leftward, minimizing lexical (and padded numerical) value. Greedy works as smaller left digits always better. Use `sorted(s)` (ascending default).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
n = input().strip()
smallest = ''.join(sorted(n))
print(smallest)
```

### Example Problem 2: Largest Even Number by Rearranging Digits
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer as a string (guaranteed to have at least one even digit), rearrange its digits to form the **largest possible even number** (as a string). An even number ends with 0,2,4,6,8.

**Sample**:
- Input: 132 → Output: 312
- Input: 5321 → Output: 5312

**Solution Explanation**:
1. Sort digits **descending** to get largest candidate: `lst = sorted(n, reverse=True)`.
2. If `lst[-1]` even (int(lst[-1]) % 2 == 0), done.
3. Else, find **rightmost even digit** (largest index i where lst[i] even), swap `lst[i]` and `lst[-1]`. This minimizes disruption to the large prefix (only affects suffix).
4. Join to string.
- Why optimal? Sorted desc is largest odd; swapping smallest even (rightmost) with smallest odd (end) keeps prefix maximal.
- Time: O(d log d).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
n = input().strip()
lst = sorted(n, reverse=True)
if int(lst[-1]) % 2 == 0:
    print(''.join(lst))
else:
    for i in range(len(lst) - 2, -1, -1):
        if int(lst[i]) % 2 == 0:
            lst[i], lst[-1] = lst[-1], lst[i]
            break
    print(''.join(lst))
```

### Example Problem 3: Largest Multiple of 3 by Rearranging Digits (Minimal Removals)
**Problem Description**: Given digits as a string, remove the **minimal number of digits** (0, 1, or 2; prefer fewer) so the remaining digits form a number whose digit sum % 3 == 0. Then rearrange remaining to **largest possible** (output as string, no leading zeros unless "0"). Assume possible.

**Sample**:
- Input: 1234 (sum=10%3=1) → Remove smallest %3==1 (1), remain 432 → 432
- Input: 987 (sum=24%3=0) → 987

**Solution Explanation**:
1. Compute `total = sum(int(d) for d in n) % 3`.
2. If total == 0: sort desc remaining (all).
3. Else:
   - Find candidates: digits %3 == total (remove 1), or %3 == (3-total) (remove 2 smallest).
   - Prefer remove 1 if possible (find smallest such digit, remove one occurrence).
   - Else remove 2 smallest such.
4. Sort remaining desc, join (lstrip '0' or handle "0").
- Greedy: remove smallest to preserve large digits.
- Collect removable: sort digits by value asc, filter by mod.
- Time: O(d log d).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
n = input().strip()
digits = [int(d) for d in n]
total = sum(digits) % 3
removals = []

if total == 0:
    pass
else:
    mods = sorted(digits)
    rem1 = [x for x in mods if x % 3 == total]
    rem2 = [x for x in mods if x % 3 == (3 - total)]
    if rem1:
        removals = [min(rem1)]
    elif len(rem2) >= 2:
        rem2.sort()
        removals = rem2[:2]

for r in removals:
    digits.remove(r)  # remove one occurrence

if not digits:
    print(0)
else:
    remaining = ''.join(map(str, sorted(digits, reverse=True)))
    print(remaining.lstrip('0') or '0')
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read the input string from stdin. Convert to list of chars, sort in descending order (`sorted(reverse=True)`), join back to string. This directly forms the largest permutation. Handles single-digit (unchanged), zeros (pushed right), and already-max cases automatically. Output the string to stdout. O(d log d) time.

```python
def super_size(n: str) -> str:
    return ''.join(sorted(n, reverse=True))

n = input().strip()
print(super_size(n))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts involve:
- **1D Distance Calculation**: Using the absolute difference `|position - target|` to compute the Manhattan distance on a number line (floors 0-2).
- **Greedy Selection**: Directly compare distances from two candidates (elevators) to the target (call floor) and select the minimum.
- **Tie-Breaking Rule**: In case of equal distances, prefer the "right" elevator (a deterministic bias).

No complex data structures or searches are needed due to only 2 candidates; it's O(1) time.

## Tutorial
### Computing Distances on a Number Line
In competitive programming, positions on a line (e.g., floors, coordinates) use `abs(a - b)` for distance, as it's the shortest path without obstacles.

**Steps to solve similar problems**:
1. Read positions of candidates (here, `left` and `right`) and target (`call`).
2. Compute `dist_left = abs(left - call)` and `dist_right = abs(right - call)`.
3. Compare:
   - If `dist_left < dist_right`: Choose left.
   - Else (equal or greater): Choose right (tie-breaker).
4. Output the choice.

**Why greedy?** With fixed small candidates, the closest is optimal; no future states to consider.

**Edge Cases**:
- Same floor: dist=0, whoever is called wins (but tie prefers right).
- Equal distance: Always right.
- Floors at extremes (0 or 2).

**Generalization**:
- For N candidates: Iterate to find `min(dist)`, track index/name. O(N).
- Sorted positions: Binary search for closest. O(log N).
- Ties: Define rule (e.g., lowest index, lexic smallest name).

**Python Tip**: Use `abs()` built-in. For input, `int(input())` per line.

Example computation:
```
left=0, right=1, call=0
dist_left = |0-0| = 0
dist_right = |1-0| = 1
0 < 1 → "left"
```

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Closest Cow (Similar to Elevator with Multiple Candidates)
**Problem Description**: There are K cows (K ≤ 10) at distinct floors 0-10. Farmer calls from floor F (0-10). Find the cow's floor closest to F. If multiple equally close, choose the lowest floor number.  
**Input** (stdin):  
Line 1: Two ints K F  
Line 2: K distinct ints (cow floors)  
**Output**: Single int (chosen cow floor).  

**Solution Explanation**:  
- Read K, F, and list of positions.  
- Initialize `min_dist = float('inf')`, `best_floor = -1`.  
- For each cow floor P: compute `d = abs(P - F)`.  
- If `d < min_dist` or (`d == min_dist` and `P < best_floor`), update.  
- Greedy: Scan all O(K), pick best with tie-break (min floor).  

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
K = int(data[0])
F = int(data[1])
pos = list(map(int, data[2:]))
min_dist = float('inf')
best = -1
for p in pos:
    d = abs(p - F)
    if d < min_dist or (d == min_dist and p < best):
        min_dist = d
        best = p
print(best)
```

### Example Problem 2: Binary Search Closest Value (Efficient for Sorted Large Input)
**Problem Description**: Given a sorted list of N unique integers A[1..N] (-10^9 to 10^9), and target T. Find index (1-based) of value closest to T. If tie, smallest index. N ≤ 10^5.  
**Input** (stdin):  
Line 1: N T  
Line 2: N sorted ints  
**Output**: 1-based index.  

**Solution Explanation**:  
- Binary search to find insertion point.  
- Check closest candidates around mid/endpoints.  
- Compute dists for at most 2-3 points, pick min dist with min index tie-break. O(log N).  
- Fallback: Linear scan if N small, but binary for efficiency.  

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
T = int(data[1])
A = list(map(int, data[2:]))
# Binary search for insertion point
left, right = 0, N
while left < right:
    mid = (left + right) // 2
    if A[mid] < T:
        left = mid + 1
    else:
        right = mid
# Check left-1 and left (boundaries)
candidates = []
if left > 0:
    candidates.append((abs(A[left-1] - T), left))  # 1-based: left
if left < N:
    candidates.append((abs(A[left] - T), left + 1))
# Min dist, then min index
candidates.sort()
print(candidates[0][1])
```

### Example Problem 3: Multiple Queries - Min Distance to Servers
**Problem Description**: M servers at fixed positions S[1..M] (M ≤ 100, positions 0-1000). Q queries (Q ≤ 100), each a client position C. For each query, output server index (1-based, smallest if tie) closest to C.  
**Input** (stdin):  
Line 1: M  
Line 2: M ints (server pos)  
Line 3: Q  
Next Q lines: each a C  
**Output**: Q lines, each server index.  

**Solution Explanation**:  
- Pre-read servers.  
- For each query: compute dists to all servers O(M), track min_dist and min_index.  
- Ties: smallest index. Total O(Q*M), fine for constraints.  
- Optimization: If sorted, binary search per query.  

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
M = int(data[index])
index += 1
servers = [int(data[i]) for i in range(index, index + M)]
index += M
Q = int(data[index])
index += 1
queries = [int(data[i]) for i in range(index, index + Q)]

for C in queries:
    min_dist = float('inf')
    best_idx = -1
    for i in range(M):
        d = abs(servers[i] - C)
        if d < min_dist or (d == min_dist and i < best_idx):
            min_dist = d
            best_idx = i + 1  # 1-based
    print(best_idx)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- Define function `elevator(left, right, call)`: Compute `dist_left = abs(left - call)`, `dist_right = abs(right - call)`.  
- If `dist_left < dist_right`, return `"left"`; else `"right"` (covers tie).  
- Read 3 lines from stdin: `int(input())` for left, right, call.  
- Call function, print result. O(1) time, handles all cases per examples. Matches exact I/O format.

```python
def elevator(left, right, call):
    dist_left = abs(left - call)
    dist_right = abs(right - call)
    if dist_left < dist_right:
        return "left"
    else:
        return "right"

left = int(input())
right = int(input())
call = int(input())
print(elevator(left, right, call))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Scaling transformation**: Multiply the input float `x` by 2 to map multiples of 0.5 to integers, simplifying the rounding to standard integer rounding.
- **Half-up rounding**: For the scaled value `y = x * 2`, compute `int(y + 0.5)` to round to the nearest integer, where halfway cases (e.g., 9.5) round up to 10 due to the addition pushing it over the threshold.
- **Inverse scaling**: Divide the rounded integer by 2 to return to the 0.5 grid.
- **Output formatting**: Use Python's f-string with `:g` format specifier to print the result without trailing `.0` (e.g., `4.0` prints as `4`, `4.5` as `4.5`).

This approach ensures exact handling of ties (round up) and works precisely for floats that are multiples of 0.1 or similar, as 0.5 is exactly representable in IEEE 754 floating-point.

## Tutorial
Rounding to the nearest multiple of a step size (here, 0.5) with custom tie-breaking (half-up) is a common arithmetic technique in competitive programming, especially for scoring, measurements, or grid snapping, where standard `round()` fails due to banker's rounding (half-to-even).

### Step-by-Step Explanation:
1. **Understand the grid**: Multiples of 0.5 are `..., 3.5, 4.0, 4.5, 5.0, ...`. The midpoint between `n*0.5` and `(n+1)*0.5` is `(n+0.5)*0.5 = (n*0.5 + 0.25)`.

2. **Scaling**: `y = x * 2` transforms the problem to rounding to nearest integer `{..., 7, 8, 9, 10, ...}`. Midpoints become `.5` (e.g., 4.25 → 8.5).

3. **Half-up rounding for positive numbers**:
   - `int(y + 0.5)` works because:
     - If `y` is in `[k - 0.5, k + 0.5)` for integer `k`, then `y + 0.5` is in `[k, k + 1)`, and `int()` truncates to `k`.
     - At exactly `k + 0.5`, `y + 0.5 = k + 1.0`, `int(k + 1.0) = k + 1` (rounds up).
   - Example: `y = 8.4` → `8.9` → `8`; `y = 8.5` → `9.0` → `9`; `y = 8.6` → `9.1` → `9`.

4. **De-scale**: `result = rounded / 2`.

5. **Edge cases**:
   - Ties: 4.75 → 9.5 → 10 → 5.0 (up).
   - Exact multiples: 4.5 → 9.0 → 9.0 → 4.5.
   - Integers: 4.0 → 8.0 → 8 → 4.0.
   - Floating-point precision: Safe here as 0.5 and inputs like 4.2 (=42/10) are exact.

6. **Output**: `print(f"{result:g}")` removes `.0`.

7. **Limitations**: Assumes non-negative inputs (as per samples). For negatives, use `math.floor` or adjust. Avoid for very large floats due to precision loss.

This scales to any step `s` (e.g., 0.1): `int((x / s) + 0.5) * s`.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Round Temperature Half-Up
**Problem**: Given a floating-point temperature in Celsius, round it to the nearest integer degree. Round up if exactly halfway (e.g., 20.5 → 21, 21.4 → 21, 21.6 → 22).  
**Sample Input**:  
20.5  
**Sample Output**:  
21  

**Solution Explanation**: Scale to integers by using half-up rounding directly: `int(temp + 0.5)`. This is the base case (step=1.0). Handles ties by pushing halfway over the integer boundary. Single input from stdin.

```python
temp = float(input())
rounded = int(temp + 0.5)
print(rounded)
```

### Example Problem 2: Round to Nearest 10 (Half-Up)
**Problem**: Round a given integer score to the nearest multiple of 10, rounding up on ties (e.g., 123 → 120, 125 → 130, 128 → 130). Multiple test cases: first line T, then T lines of integers.  
**Sample Input**:  
3  
123  
125  
128  
**Sample Output**:  
120  
130  
130  

**Solution Explanation**: Treat as float for generality. Scale by dividing by 10: `y = num / 10`, then `int(y + 0.5) * 10`. Ties like 12.5 → 13.0 → 130. Read T, loop T times.

```python
T = int(input())
for _ in range(T):
    num = float(input())
    scaled = num / 10
    rounded_scaled = int(scaled + 0.5)
    result = rounded_scaled * 10
    print(int(result))  # Since multiples of 10 are integers
```

### Example Problem 3: Snap to Nearest Cent (Money Rounding)
**Problem**: Round a money amount to the nearest cent (0.01), half-up (e.g., 1.234 → 1.23, 1.235 → 1.24). First line T (test cases), then T floats. Print with exactly 2 decimal places.  
**Sample Input**:  
2  
1.234  
1.235  
**Sample Output**:  
1.23  
1.24  

**Solution Explanation**: Scale by 100: `y = amount * 100`, `int(y + 0.5) / 100`. Ties like 1.235 → 123.5 → 124 → 1.24. Use `f"{result:.2f}"` for fixed decimals.

```python
T = int(input())
for _ in range(T):
    amount = float(input())
    scaled = amount * 100
    rounded_scaled = int(scaled + 0.5)
    result = rounded_scaled / 100
    print(f"{result:.2f}")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read the float from stdin. Scale by multiplying by 2. Apply half-up rounding with `int(scaled + 0.5)`. De-scale by dividing by 2. Print with `:g` to match sample (no `.0`). Assumes single positive float input as per problem. Handles all examples precisely: 4.2→4, 4.3→4.5, 4.6→4.5, 4.8→5, 4.75→5.

```python
x = float(input())
scaled = x * 2
rounded_scaled = int(scaled + 0.5)
result = rounded_scaled / 2
print(f"{result:g}")
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts/algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Triangular numbers**: The sum of the first \(k\) natural numbers, given by the formula \(T_k = \frac{k(k+1)}{2}\). This helps compute the total numbers before the \(N\)th row, identifying the first number in that row.
- **Arithmetic series sum**: The sum of an arithmetic progression (the \(N\)th row forms one, with \(N\) consecutive integers). The formula is \(\frac{n}{2} \times (first + last)\), where \(n = N\).

These enable an \(O(1)\) solution by directly computing the first and last numbers of the row without generating the triangle.

## Tutorial
### Triangular Numbers
Triangular numbers represent the cumulative count of elements up to a certain row in such structures. The \(k\)th triangular number is:
\[
T_k = \frac{k(k+1)}{2}
\]
For this problem:
- Numbers before the \(N\)th row: \(T_{N-1}\)
- First number in \(N\)th row: \(T_{N-1} + 1\)
- Last number in \(N\)th row: \(T_{N-1} + N\)

### Arithmetic Series Sum
The \(N\)th row is \(N\) consecutive integers from `first` to `last`. The sum \(S\) is:
\[
S = \frac{N}{2} \times (first + last) = \frac{N}{2} \times (2 \times T_{N-1} + N + 1)
\]
**Implementation Tips**:
- Use integer arithmetic to avoid floating-point issues: compute \(T_{N-1} = \frac{(N-1) \times N}{2}\).
- In Python, `/` is float division, so use `//` for integers, but since Python 3 integers are arbitrary-precision, multiplication is safe.
- For large \(N\) (e.g., 10,000), \(T_N \approx 5 \times 10^7\), sum \(\approx 2.5 \times 10^{11}\), which fits in 64-bit ints (Python handles larger seamlessly).
- Edge cases: \(N=1\) (sum=1), \(N=2\) (sum=5).

**Derivation Example**:
For \(N=4\):
- \(T_3 = \frac{3 \times 4}{2} = 6\)
- First = 7, Last = 10
- Sum = \(\frac{4}{2} \times (7+10) = 2 \times 17 = 34\)

This scales to any \(N\) without loops.

## Example Problems
### Example 1: Find the Row of a Given Number
**Problem**: Given a number \(X\) (1 ≤ X ≤ 10^12), find the smallest row \(R\) such that \(X\) appears in row \(R\) or earlier. Input: single integer \(X\). Output: single integer \(R\).

**Solution Explanation**: \(X\) is in row \(R\) where \(T_{R-1} < X \leq T_R\). Solve \(T_R \geq X\) using the quadratic formula:
\[
R = \lceil \frac{-1 + \sqrt{1 + 8X}}{2} \rceil
\]
Use `math.ceil` and `math.sqrt`. For precision with large \(X\), use integer square root or binary search (shown here for robustness).

```python
import sys
import math

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
X = int(data)

# Binary search for row R where T_{R-1} < X <= T_R
low, high = 1, 2 * 10**6  # Sufficient upper bound for X=10^12
while low < high:
    mid = (low + high) // 2
    if mid * (mid + 1) // 2 < X:
        low = mid + 1
    else:
        high = mid
print(low)
```

### Example 2: Sum of All Numbers Up to Row N
**Problem**: Given \(N\) (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^5), compute the sum of all numbers from row 1 to row N. Input: single integer N. Output: single integer (sum).

**Solution Explanation**: Total numbers up to row N: \(T_N\). Sum of first \(M\) naturals where \(M = T_N\): \(\frac{M(M+1)}{2}\). Substitute \(M = \frac{N(N+1)}{2}\):
\[
Sum = \frac{T_N (T_N + 1)}{2}
\]
Compute in O(1) with careful integer multiplication.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
N = int(data)

T_N = N * (N + 1) // 2
total_sum = T_N * (T_N + 1) // 2
print(total_sum)
```

### Example 3: Position in Row for a Given Number
**Problem**: Given \(X\) (1 ≤ X ≤ 10^9), find its position (1-based) in its row. Input: single integer X. Output: single integer (position).

**Solution Explanation**: First find row \(R\) using quadratic/binary search as in Example 1. Then, position = \(X - T_{R-1}\).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
X = int(data)

# Find row R using quadratic formula (precise for large X)
disc = 1 + 8 * X
R = math.ceil((-1 + math.sqrt(disc)) / 2)

# T_{R-1}
T_prev = (R - 1) * R // 2
position = X - T_prev
print(position)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
Compute the triangular number \(T_{N-1} = \frac{(N-1)N}{2}\) to find the first number: `first = T_{N-1} + 1`. Last number: `last = first + N - 1`. Sum = \(\frac{N \times (first + last)}{2}\), simplified to \(\frac{N \times (2 \times T_{N-1} + N + 1)}{2}\). Read N from stdin, compute in O(1), print result. Handles N=1 to 10,000 efficiently with integer arithmetic.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
N = int(data)

if N == 0:
    print(0)
else:
    prev_tri = (N - 1) * N // 2
    row_sum = N * (2 * prev_tri + N + 1) // 2
    print(row_sum)
```

## Algorithms:
The core concepts for solving this problem are:
- **String length validation**: Using `len(s)` to check if the string length is exactly 4 or 6.
- **Digit-only validation**: Using the built-in `str.isdigit()` method, which returns `True` if all characters in the string are digits (0-9) and there are no other characters (e.g., letters, spaces, or symbols).
- **Boolean logic**: Combining conditions with `and` operator to ensure both length and digit criteria are met, returning a boolean `True` or `False`.

No advanced algorithms like sorting, searching, or dynamic programming are needed; this is pure string validation.

## Tutorial:
String validation is a fundamental skill in competitive programming, especially for problems involving input parsing, format checking, or data sanitization. In Python, strings are immutable sequences of Unicode characters, and Python provides powerful built-in methods for quick validation without manual loops in most cases.

### Key String Methods:
- `len(s)`: Returns the integer length of the string `s`.
- `s.isdigit()`: Returns `True` if **all** characters are digits ('0'-'9') and the string is non-empty. Fails for: letters (`"a123"` → `False`), spaces (`"123 "` → `False`), decimals (`"12.3"` → `False`), signs (`"-123"` → `False`), or empty strings (`""` → `False`).
- `s.isalpha()`: All alphabetic characters (a-z, A-Z).
- `s.isalnum()`: All alphanumeric (digits + letters).
- `s.islower()` / `s.isupper()`: All lowercase/uppercase letters.
- Other useful: `s.strip()` to remove leading/trailing whitespace, `s.replace(old, new)` for cleaning, `s[::-1]` for reversal (palindromes).

### Validation Pattern:
```python
def is_valid(s):
    return len(s) == EXPECTED_LEN and s.isdigit()  # Or combine multiple: len(s) in (4,6)
```
- **Edge cases to test**:
  | Input     | len | isdigit() | Valid? | Reason |
  |-----------|-----|-----------|--------|--------|
  | "1234"   | 4   | True     | Yes   | Matches |
  | "123456" | 6   | True     | Yes   | Matches |
  | "12345"  | 5   | True     | No    | Wrong len |
  | "a234"   | 4   | False    | No    | Non-digit |
  | ""       | 0   | False    | No    | Empty |
  | "1234 "  | 5   | False    | No    | Space |

- **Performance**: O(n) time (scans string once), fine for lengths up to 10^5+.
- **Competitive tips**:
  - Use `input().strip()` for stdin to handle extra spaces/newlines.
  - For multiple tests: `import sys; input = sys.stdin.read; data = input().split()`.
  - Avoid regex (`re.match(r'^\d{4}$')`) unless necessary—built-ins are faster and clearer.
  - Combine with `int(s)` for range checks: `1000 <= int(s) <= 9999`.

Practice by modifying lengths/conditions for variations (e.g., hex digits: `all(c in '0123456789ABCDEF' for c in s.upper())`).

### Example Problem 3: Palindromic Number
**Problem Description**: A palindromic number reads the same forwards and backwards (e.g., "121"). Given a string from standard input, output `True` if it consists only of digits (at least 1) and is a palindrome, else `False`. No leading zeros except for "0".

```python
s = input()
if len(s) == 0 or not s.isdigit():
    print(False)
elif len(s) > 1 and s[0] == '0':
    print(False)
else:
    print(s == s[::-1])
```

```python
pin = input()

```

## Algorithms
- **Dynamic Programming (DP)**: Used to compute terms of a recursive sequence efficiently by storing previously computed values in an array, avoiding redundant recursive calls.
- **Memoization (top-down DP)**: Could be used via recursion with a cache, but bottom-up DP is more efficient and straightforward for linear sequences like Hofstadter Q.
- **1D Array for Sequence Computation**: Fill an array iteratively where each term depends on previous terms, with indices determined dynamically by prior values (chaotic recurrence).

## Tutorial
The Hofstadter Q-sequence is defined as Q(1) = 1, Q(2) = 1, and for n ≥ 3, Q(n) = Q(n - Q(n-1)) + Q(n - Q(n-2)). This is a **chaotic recursive sequence** because the "lookback" distances (Q(n-1) and Q(n-2)) vary unpredictably, unlike fixed recurrences like Fibonacci.

**Core Challenge**: Naive recursion leads to exponential time due to overlapping subproblems. Solution: Use **Dynamic Programming (DP)**.

### Bottom-Up DP Approach (Recommended):
1. Create a 1D array `dp` of size `n+1` (1-based indexing for simplicity).
2. Base cases: `dp[1] = 1`, `dp[2] = 1`.
3. For each `i` from 3 to `n`:
   - Compute `dp[i] = dp[i - dp[i-1]] + dp[i - dp[i-2]]`.
   - This works because `Q(k) < k` for all k ≥ 1 (proven property), ensuring valid indices (1 ≤ i - Q(i-1) < i).
4. Time: O(n), Space: O(n). Optimal for n up to 10^6+.

### Top-Down Memoization Alternative:
Use recursion with a dictionary or array cache:
```python
def q(n, memo={}):
    if n in memo: return memo[n]
    if n <= 2: return 1
    memo[n] = q(n - q(n-1, memo), memo) + q(n - q(n-2, memo), memo)
    return memo[n]
```
But bottom-up is faster and avoids recursion depth issues (Python's limit ~1000).

**Key Insights**:
- Always verify index safety: Here, `1 ≤ n - Q(n-1), n - Q(n-2) ≤ n-1`.
- For large n (e.g., 10^6), O(n) DP is fine; optimize space to O(1) if possible (not here due to chaotic access).
- Modulo if numbers grow large (not needed for Q-sequence up to typical CP limits).
- Debug: Print first few terms: [0,1,1,2,3,3,4,5,5,6,7,8,8,9,10,...]

This pattern applies to **linear recurrences** (Fibonacci), **chaotic sequences** (Hofstadter family), and **index-dependent DP** (e.g., knapsack variants).

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Fibonacci Sequence (Classic Linear Recurrence)
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5), compute the nth Fibonacci number where F(1) = 1, F(2) = 1, F(n) = F(n-1) + F(n-2) for n > 2. Output F(n).

**Solution Explanation**: Standard bottom-up DP. Use array `dp[0..n]`, `dp[1]=1`, `dp[2]=1`, then `dp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2]`. Time O(n), Space O(n). Space-optimizable to O(1) with variables.

```python
n = int(input())
if n <= 2:
    print(1)
else:
    dp = [0] * (n + 1)
    dp[1] = 1
    dp[2] = 1
    for i in range(3, n + 1):
        dp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2]
    print(dp[n])
```

### Example Problem 2: Tribonacci Sequence (Multi-Term Recurrence)
**Problem Description**: Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5), compute the nth Tribonacci number where T(1)=1, T(2)=1, T(3)=2, T(n)=T(n-1)+T(n-2)+T(n-3) for n > 3. Output T(n).

**Solution Explanation**: Extend Fibonacci DP to 3 terms. `dp[1]=1`, `dp[2]=1`, `dp[3]=2`, then `dp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2] + dp[i-3]`. Ensures all dependencies are computed before use. Time O(n).

```python
n = int(input())
if n == 1 or n == 2:
    print(1)
elif n == 3:
    print(2)
else:
    dp = [0] * (n + 1)
    dp[1] = 1
    dp[2] = 1
    dp[3] = 2
    for i in range(4, n + 1):
        dp[i] = dp[i-1] + dp[i-2] + dp[i-3]
    print(dp[n])
```

### Example Problem 3: Hofstadter G-Sequence (Similar Chaotic Recurrence)
**Problem Description**: Compute the nth term of Hofstadter G-sequence: G(0)=0, G(1)=0, G(n)=n - G(G(n-1)) for n ≥ 2 (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5). Output G(n). (Relevant as another Hofstadter chaotic sequence.)

**Solution Explanation**: Bottom-up DP with dynamic index: `dp[0]=0`, `dp[1]=0`, then `dp[i] = i - dp[dp[i-1]]`. Property G(n) ≈ n/2 ensures valid indices (0 ≤ G(k) < k). Time O(n).

```python
n = int(input())
dp = [0] * (n + 1)
for i in range(2, n + 1):
    dp[i] = i - dp[dp[i-1]]
print(dp[n])
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read single integer n from stdin. Use bottom-up DP with a list `Q` of size n+1 (1-based). Set Q[1]=1, Q[2]=1. For i=3 to n, compute Q[i] = Q[i - Q[i-1]] + Q[i - Q[i-2]]. This is O(n) time and space, efficient for typical CP constraints (n ≤ 10^6). Properties ensure i - Q[i-1] and i - Q[i-2] are valid indices between 1 and i-1. Print Q[n]. Matches sample: for n=1, output 1.

```python
n = int(input().strip())
if n == 1 or n == 2:
    print(1)
else:
    Q = [0] * (n + 1)
    Q[1] = 1
    Q[2] = 1
    for i in range(3, n + 1):
        Q[i] = Q[i - Q[i-1]] + Q[i - Q[i-2]]
    print(Q[n])
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts/algorithms for solving this problem are:
- **String Sorting for Anagram Detection**: Convert each word to its sorted character sequence (e.g., 'abba' -> ['a','b','b','a']) and compare these canonical representations. Two words are anagrams if their sorted versions are identical.
- **Character Frequency Counting**: Use a hash map (e.g., Python's `collections.Counter`) to count character frequencies. Two words are anagrams if their frequency maps are equal.
These are O(n log n) for sorting (where n is word length) or O(n) for counting (with fixed alphabet size, e.g., 26 lowercase letters), making them efficient for competitive programming where word lengths are small (typically <100).

## Tutorial
### Anagram Detection Basics
An anagram check ignores order but requires exact character matches with frequencies. Naive pairwise comparison is O(n^2 * m) (m = word length), inefficient for large lists.

#### Method 1: Sorting (Simple, O(k * m log m) for k words)
1. For a word, compute `sorted(word)` → list of chars in order.
2. Do the same for each list word.
3. Compare lists: `sorted(w1) == sorted(w2)`.
   - Example: 'abba' → ['a','b','b','a']; 'aabb' → ['a','a','b','b'] → mismatch? Wait, both ['a','b','b','a'] sorted is ['a','a','b','b']? No:
     - 'abba': a,b,b,a → sorted: a,a,b,b
     - 'aabb': a,a,b,b → a,a,b,b ✓
   - Pros: Easy, no extra data structures.
   - Cons: Slower due to log m; fine for m≤100.

#### Method 2: Frequency Counting (Faster, O(k * m))
1. Use `Counter(word)` → dict like {'a':2, 'b':2}.
2. Compare `Counter(w1) == Counter(w2)`.
   - Or manual array [0]*26 for lowercase.
3. Pros: Linear time, handles any chars.
   - Cons: Extra space O(1) or O(alphabet size).
- **Prime Product Variant** (advanced): Multiply primes for each char freq (hash collisions rare), but sorting/counter safer.
- **Time/Space**: For lists up to 10^4 words, both excel. Prefer counter for speed.
- **Edge Cases**: Different lengths (immediate false), case sensitivity (assume lowercase per samples), empty strings/words.
- **Grouping Extension**: Use sorted tuple or counter tuple as dict key to group anagrams.

Practice: Always normalize (lowercase, sort) first.

## Example Problems
### Example 1: LeetCode 242 - Valid Anagram
**Problem Description**: Given two strings `s` and `t`, return `True` if `t` is an anagram of `s`, `False` otherwise. Constraints: 1 <= s.length, t.length <= 5*10^4; lowercase English letters.

**Solution Explanation**: 
- Quick check: if `len(s) != len(t)` return False.
- Use `Counter` for O(n) freq comparison (optimal).
- Alternative: sort both strings and compare (O(n log n)).
- Why Counter? Linear time, handles freq perfectly. Manual array for lowercase: `freq = [0]*26`.

```python
from collections import Counter

class Solution:
    def isAnagram(self, s: str, t: str) -> bool:
        if len(s) != len(t):
            return False
        return Counter(s) == Counter(t)
```

### Example 2: LeetCode 49 - Group Anagrams
**Problem Description**: Given an array of strings `strs`, group the anagrams together. Return a list of lists where each sublist contains anagrams. Order doesn't matter. Constraints: 1 <= strs.length <= 10^4; 1 <= |strs[i]| <= 100; lowercase.

**Solution Explanation**:
- Create a dict: key = tuple(sorted(word)) (hashable), value = list of words.
- Iterate strs, append each to dict[sorted_tuple].
- Return dict.values().
- Time: O(k * m log m), k=#words, m=max len. Space: O(k*m).
- Counter as key: `tuple(sorted(Counter(word).items()))`, but sorting simpler.

```python
from collections import defaultdict

class Solution:
    def groupAnagrams(self, strs: list[str]) -> list[list[str]]:
        groups = defaultdict(list)
        for word in strs:
            key = tuple(sorted(word))
            groups[key].append(word)
        return list(groups.values())
```

### Example 3: LeetCode 438 - Find All Anagrams in a String
**Problem Description**: Given `s` and `p`, return start indices (0-based) of `p`'s anagrams in `s` as list, sorted. If none, empty list. Constraints: 1 <= |s|,|p| <= 3*10^4; lowercase.

**Solution Explanation**:
- Sliding window: len(window)=len(p).
- Counters: `p_count = Counter(p)`, `window_count = Counter()`.
- Expand window first len(p) chars, slide: remove left, add right.
- Match if `window_count == p_count`.
- Use arrays for O(1) updates: `p_freq=[0]*26`, etc.
- Time: O(n), n=|s|. Space: O(1).

```python
from collections import Counter

class Solution:
    def findAnagrams(self, s: str, p: str) -> list[int]:
        if len(s) < len(p):
            return []
        p_count = Counter(p)
        window_count = Counter(s[:len(p)])
        result = []
        if p_count == window_count:
            result.append(0)
        for i in range(len(p), len(s)):
            window_count[s[i - len(p)]] -= 1
            if window_count[s[i - len(p)]] == 0:
                del window_count[s[i - len(p)]]
            window_count[s[i]] += 1
            if window_count == p_count:
                result.append(i - len(p) + 1)
        return result
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- Read input: First line = `word`, second = list string → use `ast.literal_eval` for safe parsing.
- For each word in list, check if anagram of `word` using sorting: `sorted(candidate) == sorted(word)`.
- Collect matches in list (includes self if present).
- Print the result list directly (Python's `str(list)` matches sample format with single quotes).
- Time: O(k * m log m), efficient. Handles edges: empty list → [], no matches → [], length mismatch auto-fails.
- Sorting chosen for simplicity; Counter equivalent.

```python
import sys
import ast

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()

word = data[0].strip()
words = ast.literal_eval(data[1])

def find_anagrams(target, word_list):
    target_sorted = sorted(target)
    return [w for w in word_list if sorted(w) == target_sorted]

result = find_anagrams(word, words)
print(result)
```

## Algorithms
- **Fibonacci Sequence**: Defined as F(1) = 1, F(2) = 1, F(n) = F(n-1) + F(n-2) for n > 2.
- **Modular Arithmetic for Last Digit**: The last digit of F(n) is F(n) mod 10. Computing Fibonacci modulo 10 avoids large numbers.
- **Pisano Period**: The sequence of Fibonacci numbers modulo m is periodic. For m=10, the period (Pisano period π(10)) is 60, meaning F(n) mod 10 = F(n mod 60) mod 10 (with adjustment: if n mod 60 == 0, use F(60) mod 10 = 0).
- **Efficient Computation**: Reduce n to n mod 60, then compute F(n mod 60) mod 10 iteratively in O(period) = O(1) time since period=60 is constant.

## Tutorial
The Fibonacci sequence grows exponentially, making direct computation of F(n) for large n (e.g., 10^6 or 10^18) infeasible due to time and space limits. However, for the **last digit**, we only need F(n) mod 10.

**Key Insight: Periodicity (Pisano Period)**  
Fibonacci modulo any m repeats in a cycle called the Pisano period π(m). Pairs (F(k) mod m, F(k+1) mod m) eventually repeat (0,1) for standard starts, but here starting from (1,1).  

For m=10:  
- The cycle length is 60. Verify by computing: last digits are 1,1,2,3,5,8,3,1,4,5,9,4,3,7,0,7,7,4,1,5,6,1,7,8,5,3,8,1,9,0,9,9,8,7,5,2,7,9,6,5,1,6,7,3,0,3,3,6,9,5,4,9,3,2,5,7,2,9,1,0, and then repeats with 1,1.  
- F(60) mod 10 = 0, F(61) mod 10 = 1, F(62) mod 10 = 1.  

**Algorithm**:  
1. If n ≤ 2, return 1.  
2. Compute pos = n % 60. If pos == 0, pos = 60.  
3. Iteratively compute F(pos) mod 10 using two variables (a=1, b=1), updating in O(pos) ≤ O(60) steps.  
   - a, b = 1, 1  
   - For i in 3 to pos: c = (a + b) % 10; a=b; b=c  
4. Return b.  

This is O(1) time overall. Alternatives: precompute cycle array (size 60), or matrix exponentiation modulo 10 (O(log n)), but Pisano is simplest for fixed m=10.  

**Verification**: last_fib_digit(1000): 1000%60=40, F(40)%10=5 ✓; 1000000%60=40, same ✓.

## Example Problems

### Example 1: Small N Fibonacci Last Digit (Brute Force Iterative)
**Problem**: Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 1000), compute the last digit of the nth Fibonacci number (F(1)=1, F(2)=1). Input: single integer n. Output: single digit.  
**Solution Explanation**: Use iterative DP with modulo 10. Maintain two variables for previous two Fibonacci numbers. Loop n times, but since n≤1000, O(n) is fine (no need for Pisano).  
```python
n = int(input())
if n <= 2:
    print(1)
else:
    a, b = 1, 1
    for _ in range(3, n+1):
        a, b = b, (a + b) % 10
    print(b)
```

### Example 2: Large N Fibonacci Last Digit Using Pisano Period
**Problem**: Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), compute last digit of F(n). Input: single integer n. Output: single digit. Tests: n=1000000000000 → 5.  
**Solution Explanation**: Use Pisano period 60. Reduce to pos = n % 60 (handle pos=0 → 60). Then iterate O(60) to compute F(pos) % 10. Time: O(1).  
```python
n = int(input())
if n <= 2:
    print(1)
else:
    pos = n % 60
    if pos == 0:
        pos = 60
    a, b = 1, 1
    for _ in range(3, pos + 1):
        a, b = b, (a + b) % 10
    print(b)
```

### Example 3: Fibonacci Modulo m (Generalized, Find Pisano Period)
**Problem**: Given m (2 ≤ m ≤ 100) and n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^9), compute F(n) mod m. But first, find π(m) by simulating until cycle repeats (1,1) here. Input: two integers m n. Output: F(n) mod m.  
**Solution Explanation**: Compute Pisano period by generating pairs until back to (1,1). Then F(n) mod m = F(n % period) mod m. But for efficiency, since m≤100, period≤6*m (known bound), precompute or iterate. Here, compute period first, then reduce. (Note: for m=10, period=60.)  
```python
def pisano_period(m):
    a, b = 1, 1
    period = 0
    while True:
        period += 1
        a, b = b, (a + b) % m
        if a == 1 and b == 1:
            return period

m, n = map(int, input().split())
if n <= 2:
    print(1 % m)
else:
    period = pisano_period(m)
    pos = n % period
    if pos == 0:
        pos = period
    a, b = 1, 1
    for _ in range(3, pos + 1):
        a, b = b, (a + b) % m
    print(b)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read single integer n from stdin. Use Pisano period 60 for mod 10. Compute pos = n % 60 (set to 60 if 0). Iteratively compute F(pos) % 10 in O(60) time using two variables. Handles n=1,2 directly. Efficient for any n (even 10^18), matches samples (1000%60=40 →5, 1000000%60=40→5).

```python
n = int(input().strip())

if n <= 2:
    print(1)
else:
    pos = n % 60
    if pos == 0:
        pos = 60
    a, b = 1, 1
    for _ in range(3, pos + 1):
        a, b = b, (a + b) % 10
    print(b)
```

## Algorithms
- **String simulation with multi-source destruction**: Identify all bomb positions ('*') and mark all affected positions (itself and immediate left/right neighbors) using a boolean array or list to track destroyed positions. This handles overlapping blast radii efficiently since marking is idempotent (set to destroyed if any bomb affects it).
- **Score aggregation**: After destruction, iterate through the string once more, summing powers only for surviving combat letters (left: w,p,b,s; right: m,q,d,z). Neutral letters and bombs are ignored.
- **Decision logic**: Compare total left power vs. right power (can use signed scores for simplicity).
- **Time/Space**: O(n) time (two linear passes), O(n) space for destroyed tracking. Suitable for typical constraints (n ≤ 10^5).

Core concepts: Linear scanning, coverage marking (like union of small intervals), prefix/sum computation.

## Tutorial
The key challenge is simulating simultaneous bomb explosions without modifying the string in place (which could invalidate indices). Use a **destroyed** boolean list of size n:

1. **Initialization**:
   ```python
   n = len(fight)
   destroyed = [False] * n
   ```

2. **Mark destroyed positions**:
   - For each index `i` where `fight[i] == '*'`:
     - Mark `destroyed[i]` = True (bomb itself).
     - If `i-1 >= 0`, mark `destroyed[i-1]` = True.
     - If `i+1 < n`, mark `destroyed[i+1]` = True.
   - This simulates all blasts at once; overlaps are handled automatically (boolean OR).

3. **Compute scores**:
   - Define power dictionary: left positive, right negative (e.g., `'w':4, 'm':-4`).
   - `total = 0`
   - For each `i` in 0 to n-1:
     - If not `destroyed[i]` and `fight[i]` in powers:
       - `total += powers[fight[i]]`

4. **Decide winner**:
   - `total > 0`: "Left side wins!"
   - `total < 0`: "Right side wins!"
   - `total == 0`: "Let's fight again!"

**Example walkthrough**: `fight = "aa*aa"`
- n=5, destroyed=[F,F,F,F,F]
- i=2 ('*'): mark 1,2,3 → [F,T,T,T,F]
- Survivors: pos0='a' (neutral), pos4='a' (neutral) → total=0 → "Let's fight again!"

**Edge cases**:
- All bombs: `***` → all destroyed → tie.
- End bombs: `*ab` → pos0,1 destroyed → 'b' survives.
- No bombs: direct score.
- Single char: if combat letter, scores; else tie.

This approach generalizes to larger blast radii (e.g., mark i-r to i+r) or multi-dimensional grids.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Remove All Adjacent Duplicates in String
**Problem Description**: Given a string `s` consisting of lowercase English letters, repeatedly remove all adjacent duplicate letters (e.g., "abbaca" → remove "bb" → "aaca" → remove "aa" → "ca"). Output the final string after no more removals possible. Input: single line string `s`. Output: the result string.

**Solution Explanation**: Use a stack to simulate the process in O(n) time. Iterate through each character:
- If stack not empty and top == current char, pop the top (remove pair).
- Else, push current char.
Final stack joined is the result. This handles multiple passes efficiently without recursion.

```python
s = input().strip()
stack = []
for char in s:
    if stack and stack[-1] == char:
        stack.pop()
    else:
        stack.append(char)
print(''.join(stack))
```

### Example Problem 2: Remove All Adjacent Duplicates in String II
**Problem Description**: Given a string `s` and integer `k=2` (fixed for this variant), remove `k` adjacent identical letters repeatedly (e.g., "deeedbbcccbdaa" with k=3 → "aa"). Input: first line `s`, second line `k` (but use k=2). Output: final string. (LeetCode 1209 variant.)

**Solution Explanation**: Use a stack of pairs `(char, count)`. For each char:
- If matches top char, increment count; if count == k, pop.
- Else, push new (char,1).
This simulates k-duplicate removal in O(n) time.

```python
s = input().strip()
k = int(input().strip())  # Assume k provided, e.g., 2
stack = []
for char in s:
    if stack and stack[-1][0] == char:
        stack[-1] = (char, stack[-1][1] + 1)
        if stack[-1][1] == k:
            stack.pop()
    else:
        stack.append([char, 1])  # mutable list for count
result = ''.join(char * cnt for char, cnt in stack)
print(result)
```

### Example Problem 3: Make The String Great
**Problem Description**: Given a string `s` with lowercase and uppercase letters, repeatedly remove two adjacent characters that are the same letter but different cases (e.g., "abBA" → "a" after removing "bB" then "BA"? Wait, "abBA" → remove bB → "aA" → remove aA → ""). Output the final string. Input: single line `s`. Output: result or "EMPTY" if empty. (LeetCode 1544 variant.)

**Solution Explanation**: Stack-based: push chars. For each new char, while stack not empty, top.lower() == char.lower() and top != char (diff case), pop. Else push. O(n) time.

```python
s = input().strip()
stack = []
for char in s:
    while stack and stack[-1].lower() == char.lower() and stack[-1] != char:
        stack.pop()
        char = None  # Remove pair, skip push
        break
    if char is not None:
        stack.append(char)
print(''.join(stack) or "EMPTY")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: We define a function `alphabet_war` that takes the fight string. Create a `destroyed` list to mark positions hit by any bomb blast (i-1, i, i+1 for each '*'). Use a power dictionary with right-side negative for easy summing. Iterate to mark destructions (O(n)), then sum powers of surviving combat letters (O(n)). Decide output based on total score sign. Main script reads single line input, calls function, prints result. Handles all edges (empty string ties, bombs at ends, overlaps).

```python
def alphabet_war(fight):
    powers = {
        'w': 4, 'p': 3, 'b': 2, 's': 1,
        'm': -4, 'q': -3, 'd': -2, 'z': -1
    }
    n = len(fight)
    if n == 0:
        return "Let's fight again!"
    destroyed = [False] * n
    for i in range(n):
        if fight[i] == '*':
            if i - 1 >= 0:
                destroyed[i - 1] = True
            destroyed[i] = True
            if i + 1 < n:
                destroyed[i + 1] = True
    total = 0
    for i in range(n):
        if not destroyed[i] and fight[i] in powers:
            total += powers[fight[i]]
    if total > 0:
        return "Left side wins!"
    elif total < 0:
        return "Right side wins!"
    else:
        return "Let's fight again!"

fight = input().strip()
print(alphabet_war(fight))
```
