## Algorithms
The core algorithm is **two-pointer subsequence matching**. This checks if one string (the "name") is a subsequence of another string (the input string), meaning all characters of the name appear in the same order in the input string, but not necessarily consecutively. The process is case-insensitive, so both strings are converted to lowercase. Time complexity: O(n), where n is the length of the input string. Space complexity: O(1).

## Tutorial
A subsequence is derived from another sequence by deleting some or no elements without changing the order of the remaining elements. To check if `name` is a subsequence of `str`:

1. Convert both strings to lowercase for case-insensitivity.
2. Initialize two pointers: `i = 0` for the position in `name`, and iterate `j` through `str`.
3. For each character in `str` (at `j`):
   - If `str[j]` matches `name[i]`, increment `i`.
   - If `i` reaches the length of `name`, return `True` (all characters found in order).
4. If the loop ends without `i` reaching the end, return `False`.

This is efficient because it makes a single pass through `str`. Edge cases:
- Empty `name`: Always `True`.
- `name` longer than `str`: Likely `False` unless exact match.
- Repeated characters (e.g., "Allison" needs two 'l's): Handled naturally as pointers only advance on matches.
- Non-consecutive matches: Allowed (e.g., "c h r i s" spread out).

This technique is widely used in string matching, pattern searching, and dynamic programming problems like longest common subsequence (LCS).

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Is Subsequence (LeetCode 392 style, adapted for competitive programming)
**Problem Description**: You are given two strings on separate lines via standard input: `s` (the subsequence to check) and `t` (the main string). Output "YES" if `s` is a subsequence of `t` (case-sensitive), else "NO". Constraints: lengths up to 10^5.

**Solution Explanation**: Use two pointers. Start `p1 = 0` for `s`, iterate `p2` through `t`. On match `t[p2] == s[p1]`, increment `p1`. If `p1 == len(s)`, output "YES". After full iteration, "NO". Single pass, O(n) time.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip().splitlines()
s = data[0]
t = data[1]

p1 = 0
for p2 in range(len(t)):
    if p1 < len(s) and t[p2] == s[p1]:
        p1 += 1
if p1 == len(s):
    print("YES")
else:
    print("NO")
```

### Example Problem 2: Multiple Pattern Matching
**Problem Description**: First line: integer `q` (number of queries, 1 <= q <= 100). Next `q` pairs of lines: `pattern` and `text`. For each, output "True" if `pattern` is a subsequence of `text` (case-insensitive), else "False". Lengths up to 10^4 per string.

**Solution Explanation**: For each query, lowercase both strings. Use two pointers per query: `i` for pattern, `j` for text. Advance `i` on matches. If `i` reaches end of pattern, "True". Repeat for all queries. Total time O(sum lengths).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip().splitlines()
q = int(data[0])
idx = 1
for _ in range(q):
    pattern = data[idx].lower()
    text = data[idx + 1].lower()
    idx += 2
    i = 0
    for j in range(len(text)):
        if i < len(pattern) and text[j] == pattern[i]:
            i += 1
    print("True" if i == len(pattern) else "False")
```

### Example Problem 3: Compressed Subsequence Check
**Problem Description**: First line: `str`. Second line: `name` (compressed form where letters repeat consecutively, e.g., "a2b3" means "aabb b"). Decompress `name` and check if it's a subsequence of `str` (case-insensitive). Output "True" or "False". Lengths up to 10^5.

**Solution Explanation**: First, decompress `name`: parse digits after letters (e.g., "a2b3" -> "aabbb"). Then apply standard two-pointer subsequence check on decompressed `name` vs `str.lower()`. Decompression is O(m), matching O(n). Handles repeats correctly.

```python
import sys
import re
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip().splitlines()
str_lower = data[0].lower()
name = data[1]

# Decompress name
decompressed = ''
i = 0
while i < len(name):
    char = name[i].lower()
    i += 1
    num = 0
    while i < len(name) and name[i].isdigit():
        num = num * 10 + int(name[i])
        i += 1
    decompressed += char * (num if num > 0 else 1)

# Subsequence check
p1 = 0
for p2 in range(len(str_lower)):
    if p1 < len(decompressed) and str_lower[p2] == decompressed[p1]:
        p1 += 1
print("True" if p1 == len(decompressed) else "False")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read two lines from stdin: first is `str`, second is `name`. Convert both to lowercase. Use two pointers: `name_idx = 0`, iterate `char_idx` through the string. If characters match, increment `name_idx`. If `name_idx` equals `len(name)`, return `True`. After iteration, `False`. Print "True" or "False". Handles all cases: order, repeats, case-insensitivity, non-consecutive. O(n) time.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip().splitlines()

str_lower = data[0].lower()
name_lower = data[1].lower()

name_idx = 0
for char_idx in range(len(str_lower)):
    if name_idx < len(name_lower) and str_lower[char_idx] == name_lower[name_idx]:
        name_idx += 1

print("True" if name_idx == len(name_lower) else "False")
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts revolve around **digit validation** and **iterative digit extraction**:
- **String conversion**: Convert the number to a string and iterate through each character, checking if `int(char)` is in `{0,1,2,3,4,5}`.
- **Mathematical digit extraction**: Use modulo (`% 10`) to get the last digit and integer division (`// 10`) to remove it, repeating until the number is 0.
- **Early termination**: If any digit > 5 is found, immediately classify as "NOT!!".
- Time complexity: O(D), where D is the number of digits (≈ log₁₀ N).
- Space complexity: O(1) for math method, O(D) for string method.

These are fundamental for problems involving digit properties (e.g., sum, count, validity).

## Tutorial
### Checking Digits in Python
To solve digit-based problems, extract and validate each digit efficiently.

#### Method 1: String Conversion (Pythonic, Readable)
```python
def is_special(n):
    return all(int(d) <= 5 for d in str(n))
```
- `str(n)`: Converts integer to string (e.g., 513 → "513").
- `all(...)`: Returns True if every element satisfies the condition.
- Pros: Concise, handles leading zeros implicitly (though irrelevant for integers).
- Cons: Uses extra space O(D); slower for very large N (but negligible in Python).

#### Method 2: Mathematical Extraction (Efficient, No Strings)
```python
def is_special(n):
    if n == 0: return True  # Edge case, though N > 0
    while n > 0:
        digit = n % 10
        if digit > 5: return False
        n //= 10
    return True
```
- `n % 10`: Extracts last digit (e.g., 513 % 10 = 3).
- `n // 10`: Removes last digit (513 // 10 = 51).
- Loop until n == 0.
- Pros: O(1) space, works for arbitrary-sized integers.
- Cons: Slightly more verbose; doesn't handle leading zeros (but integers don't have them).

#### Tips
- For sets: Use `digit in {0,1,2,3,4,5}` or `0 <= digit <= 5`.
- Handle N=0: Special case if needed.
- Large N: Python3 `int` is unlimited; math method preferred.
- Common extensions: Count valid digits, sum digits, digit DP for counting up to N.

Practice both for interviews/competitions.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Lucky Numbers (Digits 4 or 7 only)
**Problem Description**: A "Lucky Number" contains only digits 4 and 7. Given a positive integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^18), output "YES" if it's lucky, else "NO". Single input from stdin.

**Solution Explanation**: Extract each digit using math method (handles large N). Check if every digit is 4 or 7. Use set for O(1) lookup. Time: O(log N).

```python
n = int(input())
is_lucky = True
temp = n
while temp > 0:
    digit = temp % 10
    if digit not in (4, 7):
        is_lucky = False
        break
    temp //= 10
print("YES" if is_lucky else "NO")
```

### Example Problem 2: No Zero Digits
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^9), determine if it contains no zero digits. Output "YES" if true, "NO" otherwise. Single input from stdin.

**Solution Explanation**: Use string method for simplicity. Check if '0' not in str(N). Equivalent math: ensure no digit == 0. Handles quickly.

```python
n = int(input())
print("YES" if '0' not in str(n) else "NO")
```

### Example Problem 3: All Distinct Digits
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^18), check if all its digits are unique. Output "YES" if true, "NO" otherwise. Single input from stdin.

**Solution Explanation**: Use a set to track seen digits while extracting via math. If duplicate found, fail. Time: O(log N), Space: O(1) since digits ≤18 (0-9).

```python
n = int(input())
seen = set()
has_duplicate = False
temp = n
while temp > 0:
    digit = temp % 10
    if digit in seen:
        has_duplicate = True
        break
    seen.add(digit)
    temp //= 10
print("YES" if not has_duplicate else "NO")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
Convert input to int. Use string method for readability: check `all(int(d) <= 5 for d in str(n))`. Handles N>0 perfectly. Output "Special!!" or "NOT!!". Math method alternative shown in comments. Time: O(log N), efficient for any N.

```python
def specialNumber(n):
    # String method (concise)
    if all(int(d) <= 5 for d in str(n)):
        return "Special!!"
    return "NOT!!"

    # Alternative: Math method
    # temp = n
    # while temp > 0:
    #     if (temp % 10) > 5:
    #         return "NOT!!"
    #     temp //= 10
    # return "Special!!"

n = int(input().strip())
print(specialNumber(n))
```

## Algorithms:
- **Pairwise simulation**: Process corresponding elements from two lists in parallel up to the minimum length, simulating independent survival checks for each pair.
- **Conditional survival checks**: For each pair, use simple comparisons (opponent's power >= own toughness) to determine if a creature survives, as damage is dealt simultaneously.
- **List extension for unequal lengths**: Append remaining unblocked creatures from the longer list unchanged.
- **Time complexity**: O(min(N, M)), where N and M are the lengths of the input lists, as it's a single pass.

## Tutorial:
The core challenge is simulating turn-based combat in a trading card game like Magic: The Gathering, where creatures battle pairwise without affecting others. Here's a step-by-step guide:

1. **Understand combat mechanics**:
   - Each creature is [power, toughness], where power = damage dealt, toughness = damage threshold to survive.
   - In a battle: Creature A deals `A_power` damage to B; B deals `B_power` to A simultaneously.
   - A survives if `B_power < A_toughness`; else dies. Same for B. Outcomes are independent.

2. **Handle input lists**:
   - `player1` and `player2` are lists of [power, toughness] sublists.
   - Battles occur strictly in order: index 0 vs 0, 1 vs 1, etc.

3. **Simulation algorithm**:
   - Compute `min_len = min(len(player1), len(player2))`.
   - Initialize empty survivor lists `surv1` and `surv2`.
   - For `i` in 0 to `min_len-1`:
     - Unpack: `p1p, p1t = player1[i]`, `p2p, p2t = player2[i]`.
     - If `p2p < p1t`: append `[p1p, p1t]` to `surv1`.
     - If `p1p < p2t`: append `[p2p, p2t]` to `surv2`.
   - Append extras: `surv1.extend(player1[min_len:])`, `surv2.extend(player2[min_len:])`.

4. **Edge cases**:
   - Empty list: No battles, survivor list empty.
   - Unequal lengths: Extras survive untouched.
   - All die: Empty survivor lists.
   - Self-battle (same creature): Possible but follows rules.

5. **Output**: Dictionary `{"player1": surv1, "player2": surv2}`.

6. **Implementation tips**:
   - Use list comprehensions or loops for clarity.
   - Preserve original [power, toughness] for survivors (no damage tracking needed beyond death check).
   - For stdin: Read lengths, then parse creatures line-by-line.

Example simulation:
```
player1 = [[2,3]], player2 = [[3,3]]
- Battle: 3 >= 3 → p1 dies; 2 < 3 → p2 survives.
Result: {"player1": [], "player2": [[3,3]]}
```

## Example Problems:

### Example Problem 1: Army Clash (Pairwise Strength Battles)
**Problem Description**: In a simplified war game, two armies line up with soldiers of given strengths (single integer each). Soldiers fight pairwise: if army1[i] > army2[i], army1[i] survives; if army2[i] > army1[i], army2[i] survives; if equal, both die. Extras from longer army survive. Input: First line T (test cases), then for each: N M, next line N strengths for army1, next line M for army2. Output: For each test, space-separated surviving strengths for army1 then army2 on one line.

**Solution Explanation**: Similar pairwise simulation. Iterate min(N,M), compare strengths, append winner (or none). Extend extras. O(min(N,M)) per test. Handle multiple tests.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
T = int(data[index])
index += 1
for _ in range(T):
    N = int(data[index])
    M = int(data[index + 1])
    index += 2
    army1 = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(N)]
    index += N
    army2 = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(M)]
    index += M
    surv1, surv2 = [], []
    min_len = min(N, M)
    for i in range(min_len):
        if army1[i] > army2[i]:
            surv1.append(army1[i])
        elif army2[i] > army1[i]:
            surv2.append(army2[i])
    surv1.extend(army1[min_len:])
    surv2.extend(army2[min_len:])
    print(' '.join(map(str, surv1 + surv2)))
```

### Example Problem 2: Monster Duel Queue
**Problem Description**: Monsters have [attack, defense]. They duel front-to-back: monster A kills B if A.attack >= B.defense, and vice versa independently. Survivors stay in order; unchallenged monsters pass. Input: Single test case, first line N M, second line 2N ints (a1 d1 a2 d2...), third line 2M for player2. Output: Surviving monsters as "p1: [a1 d1,...] p2: [a2 d2,...]".

**Solution Explanation**: Exact analog to original—unpack pairs, check survival with `opp_attack < own_defense`. Collect survivors, append extras. Parse flattened lists into [[a,d],...]. Use list slicing.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
M = int(data[1])
player1 = [[int(data[2 + 2*i]), int(data[3 + 2*i])] for i in range(N)]
player2 = [[int(data[2 + 2*N + 2*i]), int(data[3 + 2*N + 2*i])] for i in range(M)]
surv1, surv2 = [], []
min_len = min(N, M)
for i in range(min_len):
    p1a, p1d = player1[i]
    p2a, p2d = player2[i]
    if p2a < p1d:
        surv1.append([p1a, p1d])
    if p1a < p2d:
        surv2.append([p2a, p2d])
surv1.extend(player1[min_len:])
surv2.extend(player2[min_len:])
print(f"p1: {surv1} p2: {surv2}")
```

### Example Problem 3: Gladiator Arena Lineup
**Problem Description**: Gladiators have power/toughness. They fight in assigned pairs; survivors advance with unchanged stats. Unpaired survive. But twist: after battles, reverse survivor order for each side. Input: T tests, each: N M, N lines "p t" for side1, M lines for side2. Output: JSON per test {"side1": [...], "side2": [...]}, one per line.

**Solution Explanation**: Core simulation same as original. After collecting survivors, reverse lists (`surv1.reverse()`). Multi-test with line-by-line input. Demonstrates input parsing variation.

```python
import sys
import json
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()
index = 0
T = int(data[index])
index += 1
for _ in range(T):
    N = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    side1 = []
    for i in range(N):
        p, t = map(int, data[index].split())
        side1.append([p, t])
        index += 1
    M = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    side2 = []
    for i in range(M):
        p, t = map(int, data[index].split())
        side2.append([p, t])
        index += 1
    surv1, surv2 = [], []
    min_len = min(N, M)
    for i in range(min_len):
        p1p, p1t = side1[i]
        p2p, p2t = side2[i]
        if p2p < p1t:
            surv1.append([p1p, p1t])
        if p1p < p2t:
            surv2.append([p2p, p2t])
    surv1.extend(side1[min_len:])
    surv2.extend(side2[min_len:])
    surv1.reverse()
    surv2.reverse()
    print(json.dumps({"side1": surv1, "side2": surv2}))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem:
- **Explanation of the solution**: Implement `battle(player1, player2)` as described: pairwise survival checks via comparisons, collect survivors, append unblocked creatures. For stdin (single test case, as format unspecified but competitive-style): Read N, N lines of "p t", then M, M lines of "p t". Call `battle`, output JSON dict. Uses `json.dumps` for object format. Handles empty lists naturally. Efficient O(min(N,M)).

```python
import sys
import json

def battle(player1, player2):
    surv1 = []
    surv2 = []
    min_len = min(len(player1), len(player2))
    for i in range(min_len):
        p1p, p1t = player1[i]
        p2p, p2t = player2[i]
        if p2p < p1t:
            surv1.append([p1p, p1t])
        if p1p < p2t:
            surv2.append([p2p, p2t])
    surv1.extend(player1[min_len:])
    surv2.extend(player2[min_len:])
    return {"player1": surv1, "player2": surv2}

# Read input from stdin (single test case)
n = int(input().strip())
player1 = []
for _ in range(n):
    line = input().strip()
    p, t = map(int, line.split())
    player1.append([p, t])

m = int(input().strip())
player2 = []
for _ in range(m):
    line = input().strip()
    p, t = map(int, line.split())
    player2.append([p, t])

# Call function and print output
result = battle(player1, player2)
print(json.dumps(result))
```

## Algorithms
The core algorithm used is **Prime Factorization via Trial Division**. This efficiently decomposes a number \( n \) into its prime factors by:
- Handling the factor 2 separately to optimize for even numbers.
- Iterating over odd integers from 3 up to \( \sqrt{n} \), checking for divisibility.
- Appending remaining \( n > 1 \) as a prime factor.
Time complexity: \( O(\sqrt{n}) \), suitable for \( n \) up to \( 10^{12} \) in competitive programming.
The three functions build on this:
- `getAllPrimeFactors`: Collects all factors with multiplicity, sorted ascending.
- `getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount`: Collects unique primes and their exponents.
- `getUniquePrimeFactorsWithProducts`: Computes products of each prime raised to its exponent.

Edge cases (n=0/negative/non-integer: empty results; n=1: special [1] cases) are handled explicitly.

## Tutorial
### Prime Factorization with Trial Division
Prime factorization breaks down \( n \) into primes \( p_1^{e_1} \times p_2^{e_2} \times \cdots \).

**Step-by-Step Algorithm**:
1. **Input Validation**: If \( n \) is not a positive integer (>0), return empty (or special for n=1).
2. **Handle 2**: While \( n \% 2 == 0 \), divide \( n \) by 2, count/append 2.
3. **Odd Factors**: For \( i = 3 \) to \( \sqrt{n} \) step 2:
   - While \( n \% i == 0 \), divide \( n \) by \( i \), count/append \( i \).
4. **Remainder**: If \( n > 1 \), it is prime; append/count it.
5. **Derive Variants**:
   - All factors: List with multiplicity.
   - Unique + counts: Track exponents in parallel lists/dict.
   - Products: Compute \( p^e \) for each.

**Why Efficient?**
- Skips evens after 2.
- Stops at \( \sqrt{n} \) (most factors small).
- In CP, pollard rho for larger n, but trial division suffices here.

**Python Tips**:
- Use `i*i <= n` for sqrt (avoids float).
- `n //= i` for integer division.
- `isinstance(n, int)` for type check.

**Example Trace (n=100)**:
- 100 /2 → append 2, n=50
- 50 /2 → append 2, n=25
- i=3, 25%3≠0
- i=5, 25/5→append 5, n=5; 5/5→append 5, n=1
- Done: [2,2,5,5]

**Common Pitfalls**:
- Forget n=1 (no primes, but special-case).
- Negative/0/float inputs.
- Large n: Ensure 64-bit int (Python handles).

This is foundational for divisor counts, totient, etc.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Number of Divisors
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer \( n \) (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), compute the number of positive divisors d(n).  
**Input**: Single integer n from stdin.  
**Output**: Single integer d(n).  
**Example**: Input `100` → Output `9` (divisors: 1,2,4,5,10,20,25,50,100).

**Solution Explanation**: Factorize n into \( p_1^{c_1} \times \cdots \). Then d(n) = \( (c_1+1) \times (c_2+1) \times \cdots \). Use trial division. Handle n=1 (d=1). O(√n).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
n = int(data)

def num_divisors(n):
    if n <= 0 or n == 1:
        return 1
    count = 1
    # Handle 2
    cnt = 0
    while n % 2 == 0:
        cnt += 1
        n //= 2
    if cnt > 0:
        count *= (cnt + 1)
    # Odd factors
    i = 3
    while i * i <= n:
        cnt = 0
        while n % i == 0:
            cnt += 1
            n //= i
        if cnt > 0:
            count *= (cnt + 1)
        i += 2
    if n > 1:
        count *= 2  # exponent 1 +1
    return count

print(num_divisors(n))
```

### Example Problem 2: Sum of Divisors
**Problem Description**: Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), compute the sum of all positive divisors σ(n).  
**Input**: Single integer n.  
**Output**: Single integer σ(n).  
**Example**: Input `100` → Output `282` (1+2+4+5+10+20+25+50+100).

**Solution Explanation**: From factorization \( p_1^{c_1} \times \cdots \), σ(n) = \( \prod \frac{p_i^{c_i+1} - 1}{p_i - 1} \). Compute during trial division. n=1 → 1. Use pow for large exponents if needed (Python int safe).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
n = int(data)

def sum_divisors(n):
    if n <= 0 or n == 1:
        return 1
    total = 1
    # Handle 2
    cnt = 0
    while n % 2 == 0:
        cnt += 1
        n //= 2
    if cnt > 0:
        total *= (2**(cnt + 1) - 1) // (2 - 1)
    # Odd factors
    i = 3
    while i * i <= n:
        cnt = 0
        while n % i == 0:
            cnt += 1
            n //= i
        if cnt > 0:
            p_pow = pow(i, cnt + 1)
            total *= (p_pow - 1) // (i - 1)
        i += 2
    if n > 1:
        total *= (n * n - 1) // (n - 1)  # n^2 -1 / n-1
    return total

print(sum_divisors(n))
```

### Example Problem 3: Largest Prime Factor
**Problem Description**: Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), find the largest prime factor of n.  
**Input**: Single integer n.  
**Output**: Single integer (largest prime factor).  
**Example**: Input `100` → Output `5`. Input `13` → `13`.

**Solution Explanation**: During trial division, track the maximum factor found (last one or remainder). For primes, return n. n=1 → 1 (arbitrary).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
n = int(data)

def largest_prime_factor(n):
    if n <= 1:
        return 1
    largest = -1
    # Handle 2
    while n % 2 == 0:
        largest = 2
        n //= 2
    # Odd factors
    i = 3
    while i * i <= n:
        while n % i == 0:
            largest = i
            n //= i
        i += 2
    if n > 1:
        largest = n
    return largest

print(largest_prime_factor(n))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
The solution defines the three required functions using trial division for factorization:
- All functions validate input (int, >0) and handle n=1 specially.
- `getAllPrimeFactors`: Builds list with multiplicity.
- `getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount`: Builds parallel lists of sorted unique primes and exponents.
- `getUniquePrimeFactorsWithProducts`: Builds list of \( p^e \) in prime order.
The main program reads stdin, safely parses to int (catches non-int as []), calls `getAllPrimeFactors`, and prints in exact format `[2, 2, 5, 5]`. Handles all edges: 0/neg → `[]`; 1 → `[1]`; primes → `[p]`.

```python
def getAllPrimeFactors(n):
    if not isinstance(n, int) or n <= 0:
        return []
    if n == 1:
        return [1]
    factors = []
    while n % 2 == 0:
        factors.append(2)
        n //= 2
    i = 3
    while i * i <= n:
        while n % i == 0:
            factors.append(i)
            n //= i
        i += 2
    if n > 1:
        factors.append(n)
    return factors

def getUniquePrimeFactorsWithCount(n):
    if not isinstance(n, int) or n <= 0:
        return [[], []]
    if n == 1:
        return [[1], [1]]
    primes = []
    counts = []
    cnt = 0
    while n % 2 == 0:
        cnt += 1
        n //= 2
    if cnt > 0:
        primes.append(2)
        counts.append(cnt)
    i = 3
    while i * i <= n:
        cnt = 0
        while n % i == 0:
            cnt += 1
            n //= i
        if cnt > 0:
            primes.append(i)
            counts.append(cnt)
        i += 2
    if n > 1:
        primes.append(n)
        counts.append(1)
    return [primes, counts]

def getUniquePrimeFactorsWithProducts(n):
    if not isinstance(n, int) or n <= 0:
        return []
    if n == 1:
        return [1]
    products = []
    cnt = 0
    while n % 2 == 0:
        cnt += 1
        n //= 2
    if cnt > 0:
        products.append(2 ** cnt)
    i = 3
    while i * i <= n:
        cnt = 0
        while n % i == 0:
            cnt += 1
            n //= i
        if cnt > 0:
            products.append(i ** cnt)
        i += 2
    if n > 1:
        products.append(n)
    return products

# Main program
import sys
data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
try:
    n = int(data)
    result = getAllPrimeFactors(n)
except ValueError:
    result = []
print(result)
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Character to ASCII conversion**: Using `ord(c)` to get the integer ASCII value of each character, then converting it to string for concatenation.
- **String concatenation and manipulation**: Building a large string from ASCII representations using `str.join()`, then using string methods like `replace()` or `count()` for processing.
- **Digit sum calculation**: Summing integer values of digits in a string using generator expressions and `sum()`.
- **Optimization via counting**: Observing that the only difference between `total1` and `total2` arises from replacing '7' with '1', contributing exactly `6` to the difference per occurrence. Thus, count the frequency of '7' in `total1` and multiply by `6`. This avoids full digit sums, making it O(N) time and space efficient for large inputs (string length up to ~10^5).

These are fundamental string processing techniques common in competitive programming for handling "large numbers" as strings.

## Tutorial
### String Processing in Competitive Programming
In CP, strings often represent large numbers or sequences too big for integer types (e.g., 10^6 chars → 10^6 digits). Key principles:
1. **Avoid int conversion**: `int(s)` fails for >~10^18 digits. Treat as string.
2. **ord() and chr()**: `ord('A')` → 65; `chr(65)` → 'A'. ASCII range: 0-127 (printable ~32-126).
3. **Concatenation**: `''.join(str(ord(c)) for c in s)` builds digit strings efficiently.
4. **Digit sum**: `sum(int(d) for d in s)` is O(N), fast for N≤10^6.
5. **String methods**:
   - `s.count(sub)`: Counts non-overlapping occurrences of substring `sub`.
   - `s.replace(old, new)`: Replaces all `old` with `new`.
   - Both O(N).
6. **Optimization insight**: For differences after replacement, count changes only (e.g., each '7'→'1' saves 6).
7. **Input/Output**: Use `input().strip()` for clean stdin; `print()` for stdout. For speed on large input: `sys.stdin.read()`.
8. **Edge cases**: Empty string (diff=0), no '7's (diff=0), all '7's, non-letter chars (e.g., digits, lowercase).
9. **Time/Space**: O(N) time (N=string len), O(N) space for concatenated string. Fine for N≤10^5.

Practice: Always profile for N=10^5-10^6; prefer counting over full replacement/sum.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Digit Sum (Similar to sum_digits(total1/total2))
**Problem Description**: (Inspired by Codeforces-style beginner problems, e.g., "A. Digit Sum").  
First line: integer T (1≤T≤1000), number of test cases.  
Next T lines: each a string S of digits (1≤|S|≤10^6 across all cases).  
For each test case, output the sum of digits in S.

**Solution Explanation**:  
- Read T, then loop T times.  
- For each S, compute `sum(int(d) for d in S)` – efficient as it avoids loops.  
- Handles large |S| since O(|S|) per case.  
- Edge: |S|=1, all zeros (sum=0).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    S = data[index]
    index += 1
    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in S)
    print(digit_sum)
```

### Example Problem 2: Replace Digits (Similar to total1 → total2 replacement and diff)
**Problem Description**: (Similar to AtCoder beginner contests, e.g., "Replace and Difference").  
Single line: string S of digits (1≤|S|≤10^5).  
Replace every '8' with '0', compute (sum of original digits) - (sum of new digits), output the result.

**Solution Explanation**:  
- Build total1 = S (already digits).  
- Instead of replacing and summing, note each '8' contributes 8-0=8 to diff.  
- Use `S.count('8') * 8` – O(N) count, avoids creating new string.  
- More efficient than `sum1 - sum(int(d) for d in S.replace('8','0'))`.  
- Edge: No '8's (0), all '8's (|S|*8).

```python
S = input().strip()
count8 = S.count('8')
diff = count8 * 8
print(diff)
```

### Example Problem 3: ASCII Frequency (Similar to counting '7' in concatenated ASCII)
**Problem Description**: (Inspired by Codeforces "String Transformation" problems).  
Single line: string S of uppercase letters (1≤|S|≤10^5).  
Concatenate str(ord(c)) for each c in S into a big digit string. Count occurrences of digit '4', output the count * 9.

**Solution Explanation**:  
- Generate concatenated string: `total = ''.join(str(ord(c)) for c in S)`.  
- Count `'4'` using `total.count('4')`.  
- Multiply by 9.  
- O(N) time/space (ord(c) for A-Z is 65-90 → digits '6','5'-'9','0').  
- Insight: Could precompute digit freq per letter (e.g., 'D'=68 → one '6',one '8'), but join+count simpler.  
- Edge: S='A' (65 → no '4', 0), S='D' (68 → no '4').

```python
S = input().strip()
total = ''.join(str(ord(c)) for c in S)
count4 = total.count('4')
result = count4 * 9
print(result)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- Read input string `S` using `input().strip()`.  
- Construct `total1` by concatenating `str(ord(c))` for each char `c` in `S`.  
- Instead of building `total2` and computing full digit sums (which works but is less efficient), observe:  
  - Only '7' digits change (to '1'), each reducing sum by 6.  
  - All other digits identical → diff = 6 * (count of '7' in total1).  
- Use `total1.count('7') * 6` → O(N) time, O(N) space (optimal).  
- Handles large |S|≤10^5 (total1 ~3*10^5 chars).  
- Correct for sample: "ABC" → "656667" → one '7' → 6.  
- Edges: empty S (0), no '7's (0), lowercase/others (ord varies).

```python
S = input().strip()
total1 = ''.join(str(ord(c)) for c in S)
count7 = total1.count('7')
diff = count7 * 6
print(diff)
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts involve **run-length encoding (RLE)** to parse the string into consecutive groups (runs) of identical characters ('!' or '?'), followed by checking **adjacent runs** for valid combinations where one run is entirely '!'s and the next is entirely '?'s (or vice versa). We compute the total length of each valid pair, track the maximum length, and among ties, select the leftmost by position. This uses a **linear scan (O(n))** to build runs and O(k) to check pairs (k << n), handling non-adjacent runs or other characters by verifying position continuity. Key ideas: greedy selection of full runs (partial runs don't maximize length), leftmost tie-breaking, and substring extraction.

## Tutorial
To solve problems like finding the longest adjacent run-pair combinations:

1. **Parse Runs**: Traverse the string with a pointer `i`. For each position where `s[i]` is relevant ('!' or '?'), mark `start = i`, extend `i` while `s[i]` matches, record `(start_pos, length, char)`. Skip irrelevant chars (e.g., others) as they break runs.

2. **Identify Valid Pairs**: Iterate over consecutive runs. For runs `j-1` and `j`, check:
   - Adjacency: `runs[j].start == runs[j-1].start + runs[j-1].length`
   - Validity: Chars differ and are the target pair (e.g., '!'→'?' or '?'→'!')
   - Compute `combo_len = len1 + len2`, `combo_start = prev.start`, `combo_str = s[combo_start:combo_start + combo_len]`

3. **Track Best**: Maintain `best_len = 0`, `best_start = inf`, `best_str = ""`. Update if `combo_len > best_len` or (`combo_len == best_len` and `combo_start < best_start`).

4. **Edge Cases**: Single runs (no pair), empty string, all same chars, non-adjacent runs (others in between), multiple ties (leftmost wins), only one char type.

This scales to O(n) time/space, ideal for strings up to 10^5+. Extend to more types by generalizing pair checks (e.g., regex `r'(!+?+|?+!+)'` with `finditer` for simplicity, but loops are regex-free and efficient).

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Maximum AB Combination Length (Inspired by Codeforces 1690C - inspired run-pair problems)
**Problem Description**: Given a string `s` of 'A's and 'B's only, find the longest substring that is a sequence of one or more 'A's immediately followed by one or more 'B's, or 'B's followed by 'A's. If multiple, choose leftmost. Return the substring. If none, return "0". Single line input `s`.

**Solution Explanation**: Parse into runs of 'A'/'B'. Check consecutive runs for adjacency (auto since no others) and opposite chars. Track max len leftmost combo substring. O(n) scan.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
s = data

runs = []
i = 0
while i < len(s):
    start = i
    ch = s[i]
    while i < len(s) and s[i] == ch:
        i += 1
    runs.append((start, i - start, ch))

best = "0"
best_len = 0
best_start = float('inf')
for j in range(1, len(runs)):
    p_start, p_len, p_ch = runs[j-1]
    c_start, c_len, c_ch = runs[j]
    if c_start == p_start + p_len and p_ch != c_ch:
        combo_len = p_len + c_len
        combo_start = p_start
        combo_str = s[combo_start:combo_start + combo_len]
        if combo_len > best_len or (combo_len == best_len and combo_start < best_start):
            best_len = combo_len
            best_start = combo_start
            best = combo_str

print(best)
```

    while i < len(s):
        start = i
        ch = s[i]
        while i < len(s) and s[i] == ch:
            i += 1
        runs

## Algorithms
- **String Parsing**: Splitting strings by a delimiter ('-') to extract individual numbers and converting them to integers using `split()` and `int()`.
- **Absolute Difference Calculation**: Computing `abs(a - b)` for each pair to find the magnitude of the difference.
- **Linear Scan for Maximum with Stable Selection**: Iterating through the list once (O(N) time), tracking the maximum difference seen so far and the corresponding first pair (update only when a strictly greater difference is found to preserve the first occurrence in case of ties).

## Tutorial
This problem involves processing a list of string-formatted pairs (e.g., "56-23") to find the pair with the maximum absolute difference `|a - b|`. Here's a step-by-step guide:

1. **Input Parsing**: Read the input as a string resembling a Python list (e.g., "['56-23','1-100']"). Use `ast.literal_eval()` for safe evaluation into a list of strings, or manual parsing with `strip()` and `split(',')` followed by quote stripping.

2. **Processing Each Pair**:
   - Split the string by `'-'` to get two parts: `parts = pair.split('-')`.
   - Convert to integers: `a, b = int(parts[0]), int(parts[1])`.
   - Compute difference: `diff = abs(a - b)`.

3. **Tracking the Maximum**:
   - Initialize `max_diff = -1` and `result = None`.
   - For each pair, if `diff > max_diff`, update `max_diff = diff` and `result = pair`.
   - This ensures the first pair with the maximum difference is selected (no update on equal diffs).

4. **Edge Case Handling**:
   - After iteration, if `max_diff == 0` (all pairs have zero difference), output `"false"`.
   - Otherwise, output the `result` string.

5. **Efficiency**: O(N) time where N is the number of pairs (single pass), O(1) extra space besides input storage.

This approach is common in competitive programming for pair-wise computations on parsed inputs, avoiding sorting (which would be O(N log N) and disrupt order).

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Maximum Absolute Difference in Number Pairs (Similar to PairMaxDiff on CodeChef-like platforms)
**Problem Description**:  
You are given N pairs of positive integers. Find the first pair (in input order) with the maximum absolute difference |A - B|. If multiple pairs have the same maximum difference, choose the one appearing first. If the maximum difference is 0, output -1.  
**Input Format**:  
- First line: integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 1000)  
- Next N lines: two integers A B (1 ≤ A, B ≤ 10^9)  
**Output Format**: A-B (as string) or -1  

**Solution Explanation**:  
Read N, then read N pairs. For each pair, compute `diff = abs(A - B)`. Track `max_diff = -1` and the result pair string. Update only if `diff > max_diff` to keep the first occurrence. After loop, if `max_diff == 0`, print -1; else print the result. Time: O(N), Space: O(N) for storing pairs (but can optimize to O(1)).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
index = 1
max_diff = -1
result = None
for i in range(N):
    A = int(data[index])
    B = int(data[index + 1])
    index += 2
    diff = abs(A - B)
    pair_str = f"{A}-{B}"
    if diff > max_diff:
        max_diff = diff
        result = pair_str
if max_diff == 0:
    print(-1)
else:
    print(result)
```

### Example Problem 2: Max Score Difference (Inspired by AtCoder Beginner contests)
**Problem Description**:  
N students have scores on two tests: Math and Science. Find the first student (in order) with the maximum |Math - Science| difference. If max difference is 0, output "ALL_EQUAL". Output as "Math Science".  
**Input Format**:  
- First line: N (1 ≤ N ≤ 500)  
- Next N lines: Math Science (0 ≤ scores ≤ 100)  
**Output**: "Math Science" for the selected student or "ALL_EQUAL"  

**Solution Explanation**:  
Similar linear scan. Read N, process each line's two ints. Compute `diff = abs(math - science)`. Track max_diff (-1 init) and result string `f"{math} {science}"`. Update on `diff > max_diff`. Post-loop check: if max_diff <= 0, "ALL_EQUAL"; else result. Handles ties by first-seen. O(N) time.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
index = 1
max_diff = -1
result = None
for i in range(N):
    math = int(data[index])
    science = int(data[index + 1])
    index += 2
    diff = abs(math - science)
    pair_str = f"{math} {science}"
    if diff > max_diff:
        max_diff = diff
        result = pair_str
if max_diff <= 0:
    print("ALL_EQUAL")
else:
    print(result)
```

### Example Problem 3: Largest Gap in Heights (LeetCode/CP-style: 200. Number of Islands variant but pairs)
**Problem Description**:  
Given N people's heights as strings "H1-H2" (adjacent in queue), find the first pair with max |H1 - H2|. If max gap=0, output False. Heights positive ints.  
**Input Format**:  
- First line: N  
- Next N lines: "H1-H2" strings  
**Output**: the string or False  

**Solution Explanation**:  
Parse each line: `parts = line.split('-')`, `h1, h2 = int(parts[0]), int(parts[1])`, `gap = abs(h1 - h2)`. Track `max_gap = -1`, `result = None`. Update if `gap > max_gap`. After, if `max_gap == 0`: "False"; else `result`. Mirrors original parsing + scan. O(N) time.

```python
N = int(input())
max_gap = -1
result = None
for _ in range(N):
    pair = input().strip()
    parts = pair.split('-')
    h1 = int(parts[0])
    h2 = int(parts[1])
    gap = abs(h1 - h2)
    if gap > max_gap:
        max_gap = gap
        result = pair
if max_gap == 0:
    print("False")
else:
    print(result)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**:  
  Read the entire input using `sys.stdin.read()` to capture the list-like string (e.g., "['56-23','1-100']"). Use `ast.literal_eval()` to safely parse it into a list of strings. Iterate through each couple string: split by '-', convert to ints, compute `abs(a - b)`. Track `max_diff = -1` and `result` (the pair string), updating only on strictly greater diff to select the first in case of ties. After loop, if `max_diff == 0`, print `"false"`; else print `result`. Handles empty list implicitly (no update, but assume valid input). Time: O(N), robust parsing.

```python
import sys
import ast

data = sys.stdin.read().strip()
if data.startswith('[') and data.endswith(']'):
    couples = ast.literal_eval(data)
else:
    couples = []

max_diff = -1
result = None
for couple in couples:
    parts = couple.split('-')
    if len(parts) == 2:
        a = int(parts[0])
        b = int(parts[1])
        diff = abs(a - b)
        if diff > max_diff:
            max_diff = diff
            result = couple

if max_diff == 0:
    print("false")
else:
    print(result)
```

## Algorithms
- **Character classification**: Identify if a character is a vowel ('a', 'e', 'i', 'o', 'u') or consonant using a set for O(1) lookups; handle special cases ('c', 'o', 'd', 'e') with a dictionary.
- **Caesar cipher with wrapping**: Shift letters using modular arithmetic: `new_pos = (ord(c) - ord('a') + shift) % 26 + ord('a')`. Python's modulo operator handles negative shifts correctly (e.g., `-5 % 26 == 21`).
- **Conditional transformations**: Apply different shifts based on type (vowel: -5, consonant: +9, specials: custom negative shifts); post-process to revert if result is in {'c', 'o', 'd', 'e'}.
- **String processing**: Transform each character independently and join results, leveraging generator expressions for efficiency.

## Tutorial
String manipulation in competitive programming often involves processing each character with rules like shifting (Caesar cipher) or classification (vowels/consonants). 

**Caesar Cipher Basics**: To shift a lowercase letter `c` by `shift` (positive forward, negative backward), compute its position `pos = ord(c) - ord('a')` (0 for 'a', 25 for 'z'). New position: `(pos + shift) % 26`. Convert back: `chr(new_pos + ord('a'))`. This wraps 'z' +1 to 'a' and 'a' -1 to 'z'. Python's `%` works for negatives: `(0 - 5) % 26 == 21` ('v').

**Classification**:
- Vowels: Use `vowels = set('aeiou')`; `if c in vowels:` is fast.
- Specials: `specials = {'c': -1, 'o': -1, 'd': -3, 'e': -4}` for mapping.

**Full Logic Flow**:
1. Check specials first (overrides vowel/cons).
2. Apply shift with modulo.
3. If result in `set('code')`, revert to original.
4. Process string: `''.join(transform(c) for c in s)`.

**Efficiency**: O(n) time (n = len(s)), O(1) extra space. Immutable strings make `join` efficient.

**Edge Cases**: Wrapping ('z'+9='i', 'a'-5='v'); reverts (e.g., 'i'-5='d' → revert to 'i'); specials don't need revert in base cases.

**Python Tips**: Use `input().strip()` for stdin; generator expressions avoid lists.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Caesar Cipher Shift
**Problem Description**: You are given a single line of input containing a string `s` of lowercase English letters. Shift every letter forward by exactly 5 positions in the alphabet, wrapping around from 'z' to 'a' (e.g., 'x'→'c', 'y'→'d', 'z'→'e'). Output the transformed string.

**Solution Explanation**: This is a standard Caesar cipher. For each character `c` in `s`:
1. Compute position: `pos = ord(c) - ord('a')`.
2. Shift: `new_pos = (pos + 5) % 26`.
3. New char: `chr(new_pos + ord('a'))`.
4. Build result string.
No exceptions or classification needed. Handles wrapping via modulo. Time: O(n), where n=len(s).

```python
s = input().strip()
result = ''.join(chr((ord(c) - ord('a') + 5) % 26 + ord('a')) for c in s)
print(result)
```

### Example Problem 2: Vowel-Consonant Separator
**Problem Description**: Given a single line of input with a lowercase string `s`, separate vowels ('a','e','i','o','u') and consonants. Output two space-separated strings: first all vowels in original order, second all consonants in original order.

**Solution Explanation**: Classify each character:
1. Define `vowels = set('aeiou')`.
2. Use two lists: `vow_list = []`, `cons_list = []`.
3. For each `c`: if `c in vowels`, append to `vow_list`; else to `cons_list`.
4. Join and print: `' '.join([vow_str, cons_str])`.
Efficient with sets. Handles 'y' as consonant. Time: O(n).

```python
s = input().strip()
vowels = set('aeiou')
vow_list, cons_list = [], []
for c in s:
    if c in vowels:
        vow_list.append(c)
    else:
        cons_list.append(c)
print(''.join(vow_list), ''.join(cons_list))
```

### Example Problem 3: Atbash Cipher (Reverse Alphabet)
**Problem Description**: Given a single line of input with a lowercase string `s`, apply Atbash cipher: map 'a'↔'z', 'b'↔'y', ..., 'm'↔'n' (reverse positions). Output the transformed string.

**Solution Explanation**: Equivalent to shifting by -1 or mapping to 25 - pos.
1. For each `c`: `pos = ord(c) - ord('a')`.
2. Reverse: `new_pos = (25 - pos) % 26` (modulo optional here as 25-pos >=0).
3. New char: `chr(new_pos + ord('a'))`.
Simpler than general Caesar but uses similar position math. Time: O(n).

```python
s = input().strip()
result = ''.join(chr((25 - (ord(c) - ord('a'))) + ord('a')) for c in s)
print(result)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
1. Define vowels as `set('aeiou')` and specials as dict `{'c':-1, 'o':-1, 'd':-3, 'e':-4}`.
2. For each char `c`:
   - If special, apply custom shift.
   - Else if vowel, shift -5.
   - Else (consonant), shift +9.
3. Compute new char with `(ord(c) - ord('a') + shift) % 26 + ord('a')`.
4. If new char in `'code'`, revert to `c`.
5. Read `s = input().strip()`, transform, join, and print.
Handles all wrapping, exceptions, and reverts correctly. O(n) time.

```python
s = input().strip()
vowels = set('aeiou')
specials = {'c': -1, 'o': -1, 'd': -3, 'e': -4}
revert_set = set('code')

def transform(c):
    if c in specials:
        shift = specials[c]
    elif c in vowels:
        shift = -5
    else:
        shift = 9
    new_pos = (ord(c) - ord('a') + shift) % 26
    new_c = chr(new_pos + ord('a'))
    return c if new_c in revert_set else new_c

result = ''.join(transform(c) for c in s)
print(result)
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **String tokenization**: Splitting the input string into words/tokens using `split()` while preserving attached trailing punctuation (e.g., "companions," is treated as one token).
- **Punctuation separation**: Iteratively identifying and stripping trailing punctuation characters (e.g., `.,!?;:`) from the end of each token to isolate the mutable "word_part" for length and content checks, without modifying the punctuation itself.
- **Conditional transformation with priority**: Applying rules in strict order (reversal first if length > 6 or ≥2 't'/'T'; else uppercase if length == 2 or followed by comma; else "0" if length == 1). This uses if-elif chains to ensure only the first matching rule applies.
- **Character frequency counting**: Case-insensitive counting of specific characters ('t') using list comprehension and `lower()`.
- **String reversal and case conversion**: Using slicing `[::-1]` for reversal and `.upper()` for uppercasing, applied only to the word_part.
- **String reconstruction**: Reassembling tokens (transformed word_part + original punctuation) and joining with spaces for output.
These operate in O(N) time, where N is the input length, suitable for competitive programming constraints.

## Tutorial
String manipulation problems like this require careful parsing to handle words, punctuation, and rules with priorities. Here's a step-by-step guide:

1. **Read input**: Use `input()` for a single line from stdin. Avoid stripping unless specified, but `split()` normalizes spaces.

2. **Tokenize**: Use `s.split()` to get tokens. This handles multiple spaces by collapsing them (common in CP; preserves word order).

3. **Parse each token**:
   - Define `punct_chars = set('.,!?;:')` based on common trailing punctuation (excluding '-', "'" as per notes).
   - From the end of the token, collect consecutive punctuation into `punct` while advancing a pointer `i`.
   - `word_part = token[:i+1]` is the transformable part (includes '-', "'", letters).

4. **Apply prioritized rules**:
   - Compute `len_w = len(word_part)` and `num_t = sum(1 for c in word_part if c.lower() == 't')`.
   - Use `if len_w > 6 or num_t >= 2: new_part = word_part[::-1]`
   - `elif len_w == 2 or ',' in punct: new_part = word_part.upper()`
   - `elif len_w == 1: new_part = '0'`
   - `else: new_part = word_part`
   - This ensures only the first rule triggers.

5. **Reconstruct**: `new_token = new_part + punct`, collect in list, `' '.join(new_tokens)`.

6. **Edge cases**:
   - Empty word_part (pure punct): Stays unchanged.
   - No punctuation: `punct=''`.
   - Multiple punct: e.g., "hi!!" → word_part="hi", punct="!!".
   - Special chars: '-', "'" stay in word_part, count toward length/'t'.
   - Single/multiple tokens, leading/trailing spaces (handled by split/join).

7. **Efficiency**: Linear time/space. Test with samples, vary lengths/'t' counts, punctuation.

8. **Debugging**: Print word_part, len_w, num_t for tokens.

This approach generalizes to similar problems (e.g., regex for complex punct, multi-line input via `sys.stdin.read()`).

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Reverse Even-Length Words
**Problem Description**: Read a single line from standard input representing a sentence. Reverse every word (space-separated, no punctuation) that has an even length. Output the modified sentence to standard output.  
**Sample Input**: `hello world hi there`  
**Sample Output**: `hello dlrow hi ereht` ("world" len=5 odd→unchanged? Wait, "world"5 odd keep, "there"5 odd keep, "hi"2 even→"ih")

**Solution Explanation**: Split the input into words. For each word, check if `len(word) % 2 == 0`; if yes, reverse using `[::-1]`, else keep original. Join with spaces and print. O(N) time.

```python
s = input()
words = s.split()
new_words = [w[::-1] if len(w) % 2 == 0 else w for w in words]
print(' '.join(new_words))
```

### Example Problem 2: Uppercase Words with Vowels
**Problem Description**: Read a single line from standard input. Convert to uppercase every word (space-separated, no punctuation) that contains at least one vowel (a, e, i, o, u, case-insensitive). Output the modified sentence.  
**Sample Input**: `hello world hi there`  
**Sample Output**: `HELLO WORLD hi there` (both have vowels).

**Solution Explanation**: Define vowels set. Split into words. For each, check `any(c.lower() in 'aeiou' for c in word)`; if yes, `word.upper()`, else unchanged. Join and print. Uses generator for efficient check.

```python
s = input()
words = s.split()
vowels = set('aeiou')
new_words = [w.upper() if any(c.lower() in vowels for c in w) else w for w in words]
print(' '.join(new_words))
```

### Example Problem 3: Replace Frequent 'A's
**Problem Description**: Read a single line from standard input. For each word (space-separated, no punctuation), if it contains 3 or more 'a'/'A', replace it with "AAA"; otherwise, reverse it. Output the modified sentence.  
**Sample Input**: `banana apple aa aardvark`  
**Sample Output**: `AAA elppa aa kravdraa` ("banana" has 3 'a'→"AAA", "apple"1→reverse, "aa"2→reverse, "aardvark"3→"AAA").

**Solution Explanation**: Split into words. For each, count `sum(1 for c in w.lower() if c == 'a')`; if >=3, "AAA", else `w[::-1]`. Join and print. Mirrors frequency-based conditional transform.

```python
s = input()
words = s.split()
new_words = []
for w in words:
    num_a = sum(1 for c in w.lower() if c == 'a')
    if num_a >= 3:
        new_words.append('AAA')
    else:
        new_words.append(w[::-1])
print(' '.join(new_words))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem

### Explanation of the solution:
We read the input line using `input()`. Split it into tokens using `split()` to handle space-separated words with possible trailing punctuation. For each token:
- Extract trailing punctuation by scanning from the end using a set `punct_chars = set('.,!?;:')` (matches common trailing punct; excludes '-', "'" per notes).
- Isolate `word_part` (prefix before trailing punct).
- Compute `len_w = len(word_part)` and `num_t` (case-insensitive 't' count).
- Apply rules in priority order via if-elif:
  - Reverse `word_part[::-1]` if `len_w > 6` or `num_t >= 2`.
  - Else uppercase `word_part.upper()` if `len_w == 2` or `',' in punct` (captures "before a comma").
  - Else replace with `'0'` if `len_w == 1`.
  - Else keep `word_part`.
- Reconstruct `new_part + punct`, collect, and `' '.join()` for output.
This handles samples (e.g., "companions," → len=10>6 → "snoinapmoc,"), 'sand-colored' (len=12>6 → full reverse incl. '-'), priorities, and edges (pure punct, empty word_part, multiple punct). Preserves spaces via split/join normalization.

```python
s = input()
tokens = s.split()
punct_chars = set('.,!?;:')
new_tokens = []
for token in tokens:
    i = len(token) - 1
    punct = ''
    while i >= 0 and token[i] in punct_chars:
        punct = token[i] + punct
        i -= 1
    word_part = token[:i + 1]
    len_w = len(word_part)
    num_t = sum(1 for c in word_part if c.lower() == 't')
    if len_w > 6 or num_t >= 2:
        new_part = word_part[::-1]
    elif len_w == 2 or ',' in punct:
        new_part = word_part.upper()
    elif len_w == 1:
        new_part = '0'
    else:
        new_part = word_part
    new_tokens.append(new_part + punct)
print(' '.join(new_tokens))
```
completed 23/150, Solved: True, number of success = 6/23, acc = 26.09
## Algorithms
- **Regular Expressions (Regex)**: Using Python's `re` module to define patterns for exact substring matches (case-sensitive) on specific words and phrases, ensuring replacements only occur for all-lowercase or title-case (first letter capitalized) variants.
- **String Replacement with `re.sub()`**: Chaining multiple `re.sub()` calls to perform sequential case-sensitive replacements while preserving the capitalization in the slang equivalents. Word boundaries (`\b`) are used to match whole words/phrases accurately, preventing partial matches within larger words.
- **Case Preservation**: Separate regex patterns for lowercase and title-case inputs, mapping to correspondingly cased outputs (e.g., `probably` → `prolly`, `Probably` → `Prolly`).

## Tutorial
Python's `re` module provides powerful tools for pattern matching and substitution in strings. The key function is `re.sub(pattern, replacement, string)`, which replaces all non-overlapping matches of `pattern` with `replacement`. By default, matching is **case-sensitive**.

### Basic Usage
```python
import re
s = "Hello world"
s = re.sub(r'world', 'Python', s)  # -> "Hello Python"
```

### Word Boundaries (`\b`)
`\b` matches the boundary between a word character (`\w`: letters, digits, _) and a non-word character, ensuring whole-word matches:
```python
s = "proball probably pro"
s = re.sub(r'\bprobably\b', 'prolly', s)  # -> "proball prolly pro"
```

### Handling Multiple Replacements
Chain `re.sub()` calls for multiple patterns:
```python
pairs = [(r'\bfoo\b', 'bar'), (r'\bFoo\b', 'Bar')]
for pat, repl in pairs:
    s = re.sub(pat, repl, s)
```

### Case Preservation Strategy
For this problem:
- Define separate patterns for lowercase (e.g., `r'\bprobably\b'`) and title-case (e.g., `r'\bProbably\b'`).
- Map to slang with matching case (e.g., `'prolly'`, `'Prolly'`).
- For phrases like `"i am"`, use `r'\bi am\b'` and `r'\bI am\b'`.

### Advanced: Replacement Function
For dynamic replacements (e.g., case-insensitive with preservation):
```python
def replacer(match):
    orig = match.group(0)
    if orig.islower():
        return orig.lower().replace('cat', 'dog')
    elif orig.istitle():
        return orig.title().replace('cat', 'dog')
    else:
        return orig.upper().replace('CAT', 'DOG')

s = re.sub(r'cat|Cat|CAT', replacer, s)
```
This captures the matched string and adjusts the replacement based on its case.

### Tips for Competitive Programming
- Import `re` once.
- Use raw strings (`r'pattern'`) to avoid escaping issues.
- Chain replacements in problem-specified order to handle potential overlaps.
- For stdin: `s = input()` for single line, `sys.stdin.read()` for multi-line.
- Regex is efficient for string problems but avoid for very large N (>10^6) in tight time limits.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: String Case-Preserving Replacement (Similar to LeetCode 833. Find And Replace in String, but simplified for CP)
**Problem Description**: You are given a string `s` and two strings `before` ("cat") and `after` ("dog"). Replace all occurrences of `before` with `after`, preserving the case of the original match. Matches can be lowercase, title-case, or uppercase. Input: First line N (1 <= N <= 100), second line string s (|s| <= 1000). Output the modified string. Examples: "cat" -> "dog", "Cat" -> "Dog", "CAT" -> "DOG".

**Solution Explanation**: Use a single regex pattern matching all case variants (`r'cat|Cat|CAT'`), but use a replacement function that inspects `match.group(0)`'s case (`islower()`, `istitle()`, `isupper()`) and returns the appropriately cased "dog". Chain if multiple pairs, but here single. Word boundaries optional as per problem (assume whole words).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
import re

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()
n = int(data[0])
s = data[1]

def case_preserver(match):
    orig = match.group(0)
    if orig.islower():
        return 'dog'
    elif orig.istitle():
        return 'Dog'
    elif orig.isupper():
        return 'DOG'
    return orig  # fallback

s = re.sub(r'\b(cat|Cat|CAT)\b', case_preserver, s)
print(s)
```

### Example Problem 2: Log Sanitization (Inspired by Codeforces 1280C - Game With Array, but string-focused variant)
**Problem Description**: Given a log string with timestamps and messages, replace sensitive phrases: "password" -> "SECRET" (title-case preserve), "error" -> "BUG" (same). Only whole words, case-sensitive for lowercase/title. Input: single line string (|s| <= 10^5). Output sanitized log. Example: "My password is 123. Error occurred." -> "My SECRET is 123. BUG occurred."

**Solution Explanation**: Define pattern pairs for each phrase (lowercase and title-case) with `\b`. Chain `re.sub()` in a loop over pairs. Efficient O(N) time due to regex linearity. Handles large strings well.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
import re

s = sys.stdin.read().strip()

pairs = [
    (r'\bpassword\b', 'SECRET'),
    (r'\bPassword\b', 'SECRET'),  # Note: SECRET is always upper as per problem variant
    (r'\berror\b', 'BUG'),
    (r'\bError\b', 'BUG')
]

for pat, repl in pairs:
    s = re.sub(pat, repl, s)

print(s)
```

### Example Problem 3: Acronym Expander (Similar to Google Code Jam string problems)
**Problem Description**: Expand acronyms in a sentence: "lol" -> "laugh out loud", "OMG" -> "oh my god" (preserve case: "Lol" -> "Laugh Out Loud", "lol" -> "laugh out loud"). Case-sensitive, whole words only. Input: First line T (test cases 1<=T<=100), then T lines (|s|<=500). Output T processed lines.

**Solution Explanation**: For each test case, apply paired `re.sub()` for lowercase and title-case acronyms/phrases. Use list of tuples for scalability. Process each line separately. Title-case check via separate patterns.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
import re

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()
t = int(data[0])
lines = data[1:]

expansions = [
    (r'\blol\b', 'laugh out loud'),
    (r'\bLol\b', 'Laugh Out Loud'),
    (r'\bOMG\b', 'oh my god'),
    (r'\bOmg\b', 'Oh My God')
]

for line in lines:
    s = line
    for pat, repl in expansions:
        s = re.sub(pat, repl, s)
    print(s)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
The solution reads a single line from standard input using `input()`. It defines a list of 12 regex pattern-replacement pairs covering all required lowercase and title-case variants for the 6 slang terms/phrases. Word boundaries (`\b`) ensure whole-word/phrase matches (e.g., won't replace "probably" inside "improbably"). Each pair uses case-sensitive `re.sub()` chained in the problem's specified order (probably → i am → instagram → do not → going to → combination). This handles all cases precisely, preserves capitalization, and uses regex as required. Finally, prints the result. Time complexity O(N) where N is string length, efficient for typical inputs.

```python
import re
import sys

# Read input
s = sys.stdin.read().rstrip()  # Handles potential multi-line, but works for single

# Define case-sensitive regex pairs (lowercase and title-case) with word boundaries
pairs = [
    # 1. probably
    (r'\bprobably\b', 'prolly'),
    (r'\bProbably\b', 'Prolly'),
    # 2. i am
    (r'\bi am\b', "i'm"),
    (r'\bI am\b', "I'm"),
    # 3. instagram
    (r'\binstagram\b', 'insta'),
    (r'\bInstagram\b', 'Insta'),
    # 4. do not
    (r'\bdo not\b', "don't"),
    (r'\bDo not\b', "Don't"),
    # 5. going to
    (r'\bgoing to\b', 'gonna'),
    (r'\bGoing to\b', 'Gonna'),
    # 6. combination
    (r'\bcombination\b', 'combo'),
    (r'\bCombination\b', 'Combo')
]

# Apply replacements in order
for pattern, replacement in pairs:
    s = re.sub(pattern, replacement, s)

# Output
print(s)
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
1. **Triangular Number Validation**: Determine if the array length `n` is a triangular number, i.e., `n = k*(k+1)/2` for some integer `k >= 2`. This is checked using the quadratic formula: `k = floor((-1 + sqrt(1 + 8*n)) / 2)`, then verify `k*(k+1)/2 == n`. This ensures the array can be perfectly divided into groups of sizes 1, 2, 3, ..., k.
2. **Iterative Group Summation**: Compute the sum of the first group (`a[0]`) as the target sum `s`. Then, iteratively sum each subsequent group of increasing size (2, 3, ..., k) and check if it equals `s`. Use a pointer to track the current position in the array.
- **Time Complexity**: O(n), as each element is visited exactly once.
- **Space Complexity**: O(1), excluding input storage.
- **Edge Cases**: Handle `n < 2` (return false), non-triangular `n` (false), and mismatched sums (false).

## Tutorial
### Triangular Numbers
Triangular numbers represent the sum of the first `k` natural numbers: `T_k = 1 + 2 + ... + k = k*(k+1)/2`. Examples: `k=1: 1`, `k=2: 3`, `k=3: 6`, `k=4: 10`, etc. To check if a number `n` is triangular:
1. Compute the discriminant `d = 1 + 8*n`.
2. Check if `d` is a perfect square: `sqrt_d = sqrt(d)`, `sqrt_d * sqrt_d == d`.
3. Compute `k = (-1 + sqrt_d) / 2`. If `k` is integer and `k*(k+1)/2 == n`, then yes.
In Python: `import math; k = math.floor((-1 + math.sqrt(1 + 8*n)) / 2); if k*(k+1)//2 == n: ...`

This derives from solving the quadratic equation `k^2 + k - 2n = 0`.

### Iterative Group Summation
Once `k` is confirmed:
- Target sum `s = arr[0]` (group size 1).
- Initialize position `pos = 1`.
- For `group_size` from 2 to `k`:
  - Sum `arr[pos : pos + group_size]`.
  - If sum != `s`, invalid.
  - `pos += group_size`.
This ensures no overlaps or gaps, covering exactly `n` elements.

**Tips**:
- Use integer arithmetic to avoid floating-point precision issues (`//` for division).
- For large `n` (up to 10^5 typical in CP), O(n) is efficient.
- Precompute prefix sums for faster subarray sums if needed, but here direct summation suffices.
- Test cases: `[2,2]` (len=2 invalid), `[0,0,0]` (true, k=2), `[3,3,3,3]` (len=4 invalid).

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Arithmetic Progression Check
**Problem**: Given an array of integers, return `true` if it forms an arithmetic progression (common difference `d`), otherwise `false`. Edge cases: length 0 or 1 return `false`. Assume input: first line `T`, then `T` test cases each with `N` followed by `N` integers.

**Solution Explanation**: If `n < 2`, false. Compute `d = arr[1] - arr[0]`. Then for `i=2` to `n-1`, check `arr[i] - arr[i-1] == d`. Time O(n), uses iterative difference validation similar to group sum checks.

```python
import sys

def is_arithmetic_progression(arr, n):
    if n < 2:
        return False
    d = arr[1] - arr[0]
    for i in range(2, n):
        if arr[i] - arr[i-1] != d:
            return False
    return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
idx = 1
for _ in range(T):
    n = int(data[idx])
    idx += 1
    arr = [int(x) for x in data[idx:idx + n]]
    idx += n
    print("true" if is_arithmetic_progression(arr, n) else "false")
```

### Example Problem 2: Equal Pair Sums (Fixed Group Size 2)
**Problem**: Given an array of even length, return `true` if every pair of consecutive elements sums to the same value (e.g., `a[0]+a[1] = a[2]+a[3] = ...`), otherwise `false`. Edge cases: odd length or length < 2 return `false`. Input format same as above.

**Solution Explanation**: Check even `n >= 2`. Target `s = arr[0] + arr[1]`. For `i=2` step 2 to `n-1`, check `arr[i] + arr[i+1] == s`. Mirrors Madhav's fixed group size but uniform size, iterative summation.

```python
import sys

def equal_pair_sums(arr, n):
    if n < 2 or n % 2 != 0:
        return False
    s = arr[0] + arr[1]
    for i in range(2, n, 2):
        if arr[i] + arr[i + 1] != s:
            return False
    return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
idx = 1
for _ in range(T):
    n = int(data[idx])
    idx += 1
    arr = [int(x) for x in data[idx:idx + n]]
    idx += n
    print("true" if equal_pair_sums(arr, n) else "false")
```

### Example Problem 3: Prefix Sum Arithmetic Progression
**Problem**: Given an array, compute prefix sums and check if they form an arithmetic progression. Return `true` if yes, else `false`. Edge: length 0/1 false. Input format same.

**Solution Explanation**: Compute prefix array `prefix[i] = sum(arr[0..i])`. Then treat `prefix` as array, check if arithmetic (reuse AP logic). Combines prefix sums (for subarray sums) with sequence validation, analogous to group sums.

```python
import sys

def prefix_ap(arr, n):
    if n < 2:
        return False
    prefix = [0] * n
    prefix[0] = arr[0]
    for i in range(1, n):
        prefix[i] = prefix[i-1] + arr[i]
    # Now check if prefix is AP
    d = prefix[1] - prefix[0]
    for i in range(2, n):
        if prefix[i] - prefix[i-1] != d:
            return False
    return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
idx = 1
for _ in range(T):
    n = int(data[idx])
    idx += 1
    arr = [int(x) for x in data[idx:idx + n]]
    idx += n
    print("true" if prefix_ap(arr, n) else "false")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: First, handle edge cases `n < 2` → false. Check if `n` is triangular using the formula to compute `k`. If not, false. Set `s = arr[0]`. Use a position pointer `pos = 1` and loop over group sizes 2 to `k`, summing each group and comparing to `s`. If all match and `pos == n`, true. Full program reads `T` test cases from stdin (standard CP format), processes each, prints "true"/"false".

```python
import sys
import math

def is_madhav_array(arr, n):
    if n < 2:
        return False
    # Check if n is triangular: k*(k+1)/2 == n
    k = math.floor((-1 + math.sqrt(1 + 8 * n)) / 2)
    if k * (k + 1) // 2 != n:
        return False
    s = arr[0]
    pos = 1
    for group_size in range(2, k + 1):
        curr_sum = 0
        for j in range(group_size):
            curr_sum += arr[pos]
            pos += 1
        if curr_sum != s:
            return False
    return True

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
T = int(data[index])
index += 1
for _ in range(T):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    arr = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(n)]
    index += n
    print("true" if is_madhav_array(arr, n) else "false")
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **String slicing and indexing**: Extract substrings or individual characters from the input string (e.g., first three and last three digits).
- **Character-to-integer conversion**: Convert each character digit (e.g., '1') to its integer value using `int(char)` for summation.
- **Summation**: Compute the sum of digits in each half of the string.
- **Equality comparison**: Check if the two sums are equal to determine if the ticket is "lucky".
This is a straightforward O(1) time complexity problem (fixed 6 digits) involving basic string manipulation and arithmetic, with no advanced data structures or algorithms required.

## Tutorial
Summing digits from a string is a fundamental technique in competitive programming, often used in problems involving number properties (e.g., palindromes, divisibility checks, or digit DP). Here's a step-by-step tutorial:

1. **Read input as string**: Use `input().strip()` to read the ticket number, ensuring no extra whitespace.
2. **Access digits**: Strings are iterable; use indexing `s[i]` for the i-th character (0-based).
3. **Convert to int**: `int(s[i])` converts '0'-'9' to 0-9. Assume valid input (digits only).
4. **Sum halves**:
   - First half sum: `sum1 = int(s[0]) + int(s[1]) + int(s[2])`
   - Second half sum: `sum2 = int(s[3]) + int(s[4]) + int(s[5])`
   - Generalize with loops: `sum(int(s[i]) for i in range(3))` and `sum(int(s[i]) for i in range(3,6))`.
5. **Compare**: `sum1 == sum2`.
6. **Output**: Print `"true"` or `"false"` (lowercase, as per common problem conventions).
7. **Edge cases**: Fixed 6 digits, but validate length if needed (though problem assumes valid input).
8. **Efficiency**: O(n) where n=6; scales to longer strings with loops.
9. **Python tips**: Use `sum(map(int, s[:3]))` for concise slicing + conversion + sum.
10. **Common extensions**: Modulo 9 for digital root (`sum_digits % 9`), or digit DP for counting valid numbers.

Practice by modifying for variable lengths or multiple test cases.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Digit Sum (Common in beginner contests, e.g., Codeforces Problem A-style)
**Problem Description**: Given a single string of digits (length up to 10^5), compute and print the sum of all its digits. Input: first line T (test cases), then T lines each with a string.

**Solution Explanation**: Read T, then for each test case: read string S, convert each char to int using `map(int, S)`, sum them with `sum()`, print result. Time: O(total digits), efficient for constraints.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    S = data[index]
    index += 1
    digit_sum = sum(int(c) for c in S)
    print(digit_sum)
```

### Example Problem 2: Armstrong Number Check (e.g., similar to HackerRank "Armstrong Numbers")
**Problem Description**: A number is Armstrong if the sum of its digits each raised to the power of the number of digits equals the number itself. Given a string N (1-10 digits), return "true" if Armstrong, else "false". Single input string.

**Solution Explanation**: Read string N. Compute length L = len(N). Sum `int(d)**L for d in N`. Convert N to int, compare sums. Handles leading zeros via string. Time: O(L).

```python
N = input().strip()
L = len(N)
arm_sum = sum(int(d)**L for d in N)
num = int(N)
print("true" if arm_sum == num else "false")
```

### Example Problem 3: Digital Root (e.g., LeetCode 258 or Codeforces starter)
**Problem Description**: Digital root of a number is sum of digits repeatedly until single digit. Given string num (up to 10^18 as string), output its digital root (1-9). Single input.

**Solution Explanation**: Sum digits repeatedly: while sum >= 10, recompute sum of its digits. Optimize: digital root = 1 + (int(num) - 1) % 9, but use string for large nums. String way: iterative sum until <10. Time: O(log num).

```python
num = input().strip()
while len(num) > 1:
    num = str(sum(int(d) for d in num))
print(num)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Define a function `is_lucky` that takes the ticket string, slices into first 3 and last 3 digits using `[:3]` and `[3:]`, converts to ints with `map(int, ...)`, sums each half, and returns `True` if equal. In main: read input string, call function, print `"true"` or `"false"` (lowercase). Assumes exactly 6 digits as per problem; uses concise `sum(map(int, slice))` for readability. O(1) time.

```python
def is_lucky(ticket: str) -> bool:
    sum1 = sum(map(int, ticket[:3]))
    sum2 = sum(map(int, ticket[3:]))
    return sum1 == sum2

ticket = input().strip()
print("true" if is_lucky(ticket) else "false")
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Array traversal with indexing**: Use `range(len(arr))` or `enumerate` to access both index `i` and value `arr[i]`.
- **Modulo operation (`%`)**: Check if `arr[i] % i == 0` to determine if the index divides the element evenly. Skip `i=0` to avoid `ZeroDivisionError`.
- **List filtering**: Collect matching elements into a new list using list comprehension or a loop, achieving O(n) time and O(n) space.
- **Input parsing**: Safely parse a string representation of a list (e.g., "[1,2,3]") into integers.
This is a linear scan with conditional filtering, common in array manipulation problems.

## Tutorial
This problem introduces fundamental techniques for competitive programming (CP) in Python:

1. **Input Parsing for List-like Strings**:
   - CP inputs often come as strings like "[22, -6, 32]".
   - Use `input().strip()[1:-1].split(',')` to remove brackets and split by commas.
   - Convert to ints: `arr = [int(x.strip()) for x in parts]` (handles spaces).
   - Alternative: `import ast; arr = ast.literal_eval(input())` (safe eval for literals).

2. **0-Based Indexing and Modulo**:
   - Python lists are 0-indexed. For `i=0`, `arr[0] % 0` raises `ZeroDivisionError`, so start loop from `i=1`.
   - Modulo `%`: `a % b` (b > 0) returns 0 <= result < b. For negatives, sign follows divisor: `-6 % 2 == 0`, `-7 % 3 == 2`.
   - `0 % i == 0` for any i > 0.

3. **List Comprehension for Filtering**:
   - Efficient: `result = [arr[i] for i in range(1, len(arr)) if arr[i] % i == 0]`.
   - Equivalent loop:
     ```
     result = []
     for i in range(1, len(arr)):
         if arr[i] % i == 0:
             result.append(arr[i])
     ```

4. **Output Formatting**:
   - Print as string: `'[' + ','.join(map(str, result)) + ']'`.
   - Handles empty lists: prints `[]`.

5. **Edge Cases**:
   - Length > 1 guaranteed.
   - Negatives/zeros: Handled by Python modulo.
   - Time: O(n), fine for CP constraints (n <= 10^5 typically).

Practice: Test with examples, handle `ZeroDivisionError` explicitly if needed (`if i != 0 and ...`).

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Fixed Points
**Problem Description**: Given an array of integers in the sample input format, return a new array consisting of all fixed points, i.e., elements where `arr[i] == i` (0-based indexing).  
**Sample Input**:  
`[0, 1, 3, 3, 5]`  
**Sample Output**:  
`[0, 1, 3]`  
(At i=0: 0==0; i=1:1==1; i=3:3==3)

**Solution Explanation**: Parse the input into a list. Use list comprehension to iterate over all indices `i` from 0 to `len(arr)-1`, checking if `arr[i] == i`. Collect matching `arr[i]` values. No division issues. Time O(n), space O(n). Print in the required format.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
if data.startswith('[') and data.endswith(']'):
    arr = [int(x.strip()) for x in data[1:-1].split(',')]
else:
    arr = []
result = [arr[i] for i in range(len(arr)) if arr[i] == i]
print('[' + ','.join(map(str, result)) + ']')
```

### Example Problem 2: Dominant Elements
**Problem Description**: Given an array of integers, return a new array consisting of elements where `arr[i] > i` (0-based indexing).  
**Sample Input**:  
`[3, 1, 4, 1, 5, 9]`  
**Sample Output**:  
`[3, 4, 5, 9]`  
( i=0:3>0; i=2:4>2; i=4:5>4; i=5:9>5)

**Solution Explanation**: Parse input as before. Filter using list comprehension: include `arr[i]` if `arr[i] > i`. Simple comparison, no special cases like division. O(n) time/space. Format output similarly.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read

if data.startswith('[') and data.endswith(']'):

print('[' + ','.join(map(str, result)) + ']')
```

### Example Problem 3: Parity Matches
**Problem Description**: Given an array of integers, return a new array consisting of elements where the parity (even/odd) of `arr[i]` matches the parity of `i` (0-based: even i even value, odd i odd value), i.e., `(arr[i] % 2 == i % 2)`.  
**Sample Input**:  
`[2, -1, 4, 3, 6, 5]`  
**Sample Output**:  
`[2, -1, 4, 3, 6]`  
(i=0 even:2 even; i=1 odd:-1 odd; i=2:4 even; i=3:3 odd; i=4:6 even; i=5:5 odd? Wait, sample adjusted: assume output without last if mismatch, but here all match except adjust sample. Actual: -1%2==-1%2=1 (Python -1%2=1, odd). All match.

**Solution Explanation**: Parse input. Use `(abs(arr[i]) % 2 == i % 2)` or directly `arr[i] % 2 == i % 2` (Python negatives %2: -1%2=1, -2%2=0, matches parity). List comp filters. O(n), handles negatives correctly.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
if data.startswith('[') and data.endswith(']'):
    arr = [int(x.strip()) for x in data[1:-1].split(',')]
else:
    arr = []
result = [arr[i] for i in range(len(arr)) if (arr[i] % 2 == i % 2)]
print('[' + ','.join(map(str, result)) + ']')
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read the input string from stdin and parse it into a list of integers by stripping brackets, splitting by commas, and converting with `int()`. Create a result list using list comprehension: for each index `i` from 1 to `len(arr)-1`, include `arr[i]` if `arr[i] % i == 0` (skips i=0 to avoid division by zero). This correctly handles negatives and zeros due to Python's modulo behavior. Format the output as a string in `[...]` format and print to stdout. Time O(n), space O(n). Robust parsing handles spaces.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
if data.startswith('[') and data.endswith(']'):
    arr = [int(x.strip()) for x in data[1:-1].split(',')]
else:
    arr = []
result = [arr[i] for i in range(1, len(arr)) if arr[i] % i == 0]
print('[' + ','.join(map(str, result)) + ']')
```
