## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms to solve this problem are:
- **Dynamic Programming (DP)**: Use a DP array where `dp[i]` represents the minimum steps to reach `i` from `1`. Update `dp[i+1]` and `dp[2*i]` from `dp[i]`.
- **Breadth-First Search (BFS)**: Treat numbers as graph nodes and operations (`+1`, `*2`) as edges. BFS finds the shortest path from `1` to `num`.
- **Bit Manipulation**: A direct O(1) formula: `(num.bit_length() - 1) + (bin(num).count('1') - 1)`, leveraging binary representation where doublings cover bit positions and increments cover set bits (minus the initial `1`).

DP and BFS are O(num) time/space, suitable for `num <= 10000`. The bit formula is optimal and always correct.

## Tutorial
### Dynamic Programming
Initialize `dp = [float('inf')] * (num + 1)` and `dp[1] = 0`.

Iterate `i` from `1` to `num`:
- If `i + 1 <= num`, update `dp[i + 1] = min(dp[i + 1], dp[i] + 1)`
- If `2 * i <= num`, update `dp[2 * i] = min(dp[2 * i], dp[i] + 1)`

This works because we process numbers in increasing order, ensuring predecessors are computed. The `+1` fills sequentially, `*2` jumps ahead optimally.

**Time**: O(num), **Space**: O(num)

### Breadth-First Search (BFS)
Use a queue for level-order traversal (shortest path in unweighted graph):
```
from collections import deque
q = deque([(1, 0)])  # (current, steps)
visited = {1}
while q:
    cur, steps = q.popleft()
    if cur == num:
        return steps
    for next_val in [cur + 1, cur * 2]:
        if next_val <= num and next_val not in visited:
            visited.add(next_val)
            q.append((next_val, steps + 1))
```
Each number is visited once. Prune `> num` to bound search.

**Time/Space**: O(num)

### Bit Manipulation
- `num.bit_length()`: position of highest set bit (e.g., 12=1100b → 4).
- `bin(num).count('1')`: population count (set bits).
Formula: doublings for highest bit (`bit_length() - 1`) + increments for extra 1s (`popcount - 1`).
Achievable by strategic `+1` before doublings to set lower bits.

**Time**: O(1), **Space**: O(1)

DP/BFS generalize to varied operations/constraints; bit formula is problem-specific insight.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: LeetCode 397 - Integer Replacement
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^9), return the minimum number of operations needed to reduce `n` to `1`. Allowed operations: if `n` is even, divide by 2; if odd, add 1 or subtract 1.

**Solution Explanation**: This is the reverse problem (n to 1). Use top-down DP with memoization (recursion). Base: if `n == 1`, return 0. If even, recurse on `n//2`. If odd, try both `n-1` and `n+1`, take min. `@lru_cache` memos states. Depth is O(log n) due to halvings; branches prune quickly despite n=10^9.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
from functools import lru_cache

class Solution:
    @lru_cache(None)
    def integerReplacement(self, n: int) -> int:
        if n == 1:
            return 0
        if n % 2 == 0:
            return 1 + self.integerReplacement(n // 2)
        return 1 + min(self.integerReplacement(n - 1), self.integerReplacement(n + 1))
```

### Example Problem 2: LeetCode 279 - Perfect Squares
**Problem Description**: Given a positive integer `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^4), return the least number of perfect square numbers (e.g., 1,4,9,16,...) that sum to `n`.

**Solution Explanation**: Classic unbounded knapsack DP. `dp[i]` = min squares summing to `i`. Initialize `dp[0] = 0`, `dp[1..n] = inf`. For each `i=1` to `n`, for each square `j*j <= i`, `dp[i] = min(dp[i], dp[i - j*j] + 1)`. Optimizations exist (e.g., Lagrange's theorem limits to 4), but DP is O(n √n).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numSquares(self, n: int) -> int:
        dp = [sys.maxsize] * (n + 1)
        dp[0] = 0
        for i in range(1, n + 1):
            j = 1
            while j * j <= i:
                dp[i] = min(dp[i], dp[i - j * j] + 1)
                j += 1
        return dp[n]
```

### Example Problem 3: LeetCode 752 - Open the Lock
**Problem Description**: A 4-digit lock starts at '0000'. Turn a wheel +1/-1 (mod 10) per turn. Given `deadends` (forbidden states), find min turns to `target`. If impossible, -1. (4*10^4 states max.)

**Solution Explanation**: BFS on string states (graph: 10^4 nodes, 8 edges/node). Queue `(state, steps)`, visited set. Generate 8 neighbors by rotating each of 4 positions ±1. Skip deadends/visited. Return steps on target.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
from collections import deque
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def openLock(self, deadends: List[str], target: str) -> int:
        dead = set(deadends)
        if '0000' in dead:
            return -1
        q = deque([('0000', 0)])
        visited = {'0000'}
        while q:
            state, steps = q.popleft()
            if state == target:
                return steps
            for i in range(4):
                digit = int(state[i])
                for d in [-1, 1]:
                    new_digit = (digit + d) % 10
                    new_state = state[:i] + str(new_digit) + state[i+1:]
                    if new_state not in visited and new_state not in dead:
                        visited.add(new_state)
                        q.append((new_state, steps + 1))
        return -1
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: We use Dynamic Programming for clarity and efficiency. `dp[i]` stores the min steps to reach `i`. Start with `dp[1] = 0`. Iterate from `1` to `num`, updating `+1` and `*2` targets with `min` to handle multiple paths. Since operations increase the value and we process ascending, all predecessors are resolved. Time O(num), space O(num). Handles input/output as specified (stdin int, stdout ans). Special case `num=1` is 0.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
num = int(data)

if num == 1:
    print(0)
    sys.exit()

INF = float('inf')
dp = [INF] * (num + 1)
dp[1] = 0

for i in range(1, num + 1):
    if dp[i] != INF:
        if i + 1 <= num:
            dp[i + 1] = min(dp[i + 1], dp[i] + 1)
        if i * 2 <= num:
            dp[i * 2] = min(dp[i * 2], dp[i] + 1)

print(dp[num])
```

## Algorithms
The core algorithms and concepts used to solve this problem are:
- **Sieve of Eratosthenes**: Efficiently generate all prime numbers strictly less than `N` in O(N log log N) time, which is crucial for handling large `N` (up to ~10^7).
- **Combinatorics for counting pairs**: The count of "prime prime" fractions <1 is simply the number of ways to choose 2 distinct primes `p < q < N`, which is `C(k, 2) = k*(k-1)/2` where `k` is the number of primes < `N`. This avoids O(k^2) iteration.
- **Prefix sums with integer division and modular arithmetic**: To compute the sum `S = sum_{p < q, both prime < N} p/q` efficiently in O(k) time:
  - Maintain a running prefix sum of primes up to the previous prime.
  - For each prime `p_j`, compute `prefix / p_j = (prefix // p_j) + (prefix % p_j)/p_j`.
  - Accumulate integer parts exactly (`sum_q`).
  - Accumulate fractional parts in double-precision float (`sum_frac`), which is safe because `sum_frac < k ≈ 10^6`, and floating-point error is <<1 (absolute error ~10^{-10}).
- **Floor computation**: `floor(S) = sum_q + floor(sum_frac)`, leveraging the precision guarantee of IEEE 754 double for the small fractional sum.

These ensure the solution is efficient (O(N log log N + k)) and precise without big-integer fractions or O(k^2) loops.

## Tutorial
### 1. Sieve of Eratosthenes
To generate all primes < `N`:
- Create a boolean array `is_prime[0..N-1]` initialized to `True` (except 0 and 1).
- For each `i` from 2 to `sqrt(N)`, if `is_prime[i]`, mark multiples `i*i, i*i+i, ...` as `False`.
- Collect indices where `is_prime[i]` is `True`.
This is optimal for multiple queries or precomputation up to 10^7-10^8.

### 2. Counting Pairs
With `k` primes `p_0 < p_1 < ... < p_{k-1}`, every pair `i < j` gives `p_i / p_j < 1`. Count = `k*(k-1)//2` (integer arithmetic to avoid overflow).

### 3. Efficient Sum Computation Using Prefix Sums
Naive O(k^2) summation is too slow for `k ≈ 10^6`. Instead:
```
S = sum_{j=1}^{k-1} (sum_{i=0}^{j-1} p_i) / p_j
```
- Initialize `prefix = 0`, `sum_q = 0`, `sum_frac = 0.0`.
- For `j = 0` to `k-1`:
  - If `j > 0`: `q = prefix // p_j`, `r = prefix % p_j`, `sum_q += q`, `sum_frac += r / p_j` (float).
  - `prefix += p_j`.
- `floor(S) = sum_q + int(sum_frac)` (Python `int()` floors positives).
**Precision justification**: Each `r / p_j < 1`, `sum_frac < k`. Double has ~15 decimal digits; per-term error ~2^{-53} ≈ 10^{-15}, total error < `k * 10^{-15}` ≈ 10^{-9} << 1. Thus, `int(sum_frac)` is exact.

This scales to `N=10^7` (`k≈6.6e5`) in <1s in Python.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Prime Counting (π(N-1))
**Problem Description**: Given an integer `N` (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^6), output the number of prime numbers strictly less than `N`.

**Sample Input**:
```
6
```
**Sample Output**:
```
3
```

**Solution Explanation**: Use the Sieve of Eratosthenes to mark all primes < `N`, then count the number of primes. Time: O(N log log N), optimal for the range.

```python
import sys

def get_primes(n):
    if n < 2:
        return []
    is_prime = [True] * n
    is_prime[0] = is_prime[1] = False
    for i in range(2, int(n**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[i]:
            for j in range(i*i, n, i):
                is_prime[j] = False
    return [i for i in range(n) if is_prime[i]]

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
primes = get_primes(N)
print(len(primes))
```

### Example Problem 3: Floor of Harmonic Sum of Primes
**Problem Description**: Given an integer `N` (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^7), compute the floor of the sum of `1/p` over all primes `p < N`.

**Sample Input**:
```
6
```
**Sample Output**:
```
1
```
*(1/2 + 1/3 + 1/5 ≈ 1.033, floor=1)*

**Solution Explanation**: Generate primes with sieve, sum `1.0 / p` using float (safe as total ~log log N ≈ 5-10, error negligible). Output `int(total)`. Demonstrates float precision for reciprocal sums.

```python
import sys

def get_primes(n):
    if n < 2:
        return []
    is_prime = [True] * n
    is_prime[0] = is_prime[1] = False
    for i in range(2, int(n**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[i]:
            for j in range(i*i, n, i):
                is_prime[j] = False
    return [i for i in range(n) if is_prime[i]]

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
primes = get_primes(N)
total = sum(1.0 / p for p in primes)
print(int(total))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- Generate all primes `< N` using Sieve of Eratosthenes.
- Compute `count = k*(k-1)//2` where `k = len(primes)`.
- Initialize `prefix = 0`, `sum_q = 0`, `sum_frac = 0.0`.
- Iterate over each prime `p_j` (index `j`):
  - If `j > 0`, compute `q = prefix // p_j`, `r = prefix % p_j`; add `q` to `sum_q`, `r / p_j` to `sum_frac`.
  - Update `prefix += p_j`.
- Compute `floor_s = sum_q + int(sum_frac)` (exact due to float precision analysis).
- Output `[{count}, {floor_s}]`.
This is O(N log log N + k), precise, and handles large `N` (e.g., 10^7).

```python
import sys

def get_primes(n):
    if n < 2:
        return []
    is_prime = [True] * n
    is_prime[0] = is_prime[1] = False
    for i in range(2, int(n**0.5) + 1):
        if is_prime[i]:
            for j in range(i * i, n, i):
                is_prime[j] = False
    return [i for i in range(n) if is_prime[i]]

N = int(input().strip())
primes = get_primes(N)
k = len(primes)
count = k * (k - 1) // 2
prefix = 0
sum_q = 0
sum_frac = 0.0
for j in range(k):
    if j > 0:
        pj = primes[j]
        q = prefix // pj
        r = prefix % pj
        sum_q += q
        sum_frac += r / pj
    prefix += primes[j]
floor_s = sum_q + int(sum_frac)
print(f"[{count}, {floor_s}]")
```
completed 43/150, Solved: False, number of success = 10/43, acc = 23.26
## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Greedy digit processing from right to left (LSB to MSB)**: We process the decimal digits of `n` starting from the least significant digit, making locally optimal choices to maximize a global objective (total carries).
- **Carry maximization in addition**: When `a + b = n`, the sum of digit sums satisfies `digitSum(a) + digitSum(b) = digitSum(n) + 9 × total_carry`, where `total_carry` is the sum of all carry values generated across positions (each carry is 0 or 1). Maximizing the digit sum sum requires maximizing `total_carry`.
- **Feasibility check per position**: For position `i` with digit `d_i`, incoming carry `c_in`, and chosen outgoing carry `c_out`, the required digit sum `target = d_i - c_in + 10 × c_out` must satisfy `0 ≤ target ≤ 18` (since digits are 0-9).

This is optimal because setting `c_out = 1` when possible always adds 1 to `total_carry` and provides `c_in = 1` to the next position, which *enables* the next `c_out = 1` (since `c_in = 1` always allows `target = d + 9 ≤ 18`). Skipping a possible carry misses at least 1 carry and risks breaking propagation.

## Tutorial
### Key Insight: Digit Sums and Carries
Consider adding `a` and `b` digit-by-digit to form `n`:
```
  ... a2 a1 a0
+ ... b2 b1 b0
-----------
  ... d2 d1 d0   (digits of n)
```
For position `i` (from right, `i=0` LSB), let `c_i` be carry *into* `i` (`c_0 = 0`), `c_{i+1}` carry *out* to `i+1`. Then:
```
a_i + b_i + c_i = d_i + 10 × c_{i+1}
```
So `a_i + b_i = d_i - c_i + 10 × c_{i+1}` (`target`). Constraints: `0 ≤ a_i, b_i ≤ 9` ⇒ `0 ≤ target ≤ 18`, `c_i ∈ {0,1}`.

Summing over all positions:
```
∑ (a_i + b_i) = ∑ d_i + 10 × ∑ c_{i+1} - ∑ c_i
```
This telescopes to `digitSum(a) + digitSum(b) = digitSum(n) + 9 × ∑ c_j` (all `c_0 = 0`, no extra MSB carry). Each carry adds **9** to the digit sum sum!

### Greedy Algorithm
1. Extract digits of `n` from LSB: `digits[0] = n % 10`, etc.
2. Initialize `s = digitSum(n)`, `total_carry = 0`, `carry_in = 0`.
3. For each position `i = 0` to `len(digits)-1`:
   - If `i` is the last position: set `carry_out = 0`.
   - Else: try `carry_out = 1`; compute `target = digits[i] - carry_in + 10`. If `0 ≤ target ≤ 18`, set it (add 1 to `total_carry`); else `carry_out = 0`.
   - Set `carry_in = carry_out` for next.
4. Return `s + 9 × total_carry`.

### Why Optimal?
- When `carry_in = 0`: Can set `carry_out = 1` iff `d_i ≤ 8` (`target = d_i + 10 ≤ 18`).
- When `carry_in = 1`: *Always* can set `carry_out = 1` (`target = d_i + 9 ∈ [9,18]`).
- Propagation: Once started (`carry_in = 1`), continues until end (or last position).
- Breaks only at `d_i = 9` with `carry_in = 0` (`target = 19 > 18`).
- Proof sketch: Setting `carry_out = 1` when possible adds +1 immediately and enables next (+1 potential). Skipping loses ≥1 and may lose more (if next `d_{i+1} = 9`).

**Time**: O(log n) (digits ≤ 11).

**Examples**:
- `n=20` (digits [0,2]): Pos0: `carry_in=0`, `d=0 ≤8` → `carry_out=1`. Pos1 (last): `carry_out=0`. `total_carry=1`, `s=2`, ans=11.
- `n=29` (digits [9,2]): Pos0: `d=9 >8` → `carry_out=0`. Pos1: last. `total_carry=0`, `s=11`, ans=11.
- `n=100` (digits [0,0,1]): Chains 2 carries. `s=1`, ans=19.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Maximum Total Carries (Codeforces-style, e.g., similar to EDU 1.4 #F)
**Problem Description**:  
Given an integer `n` (0 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), compute the *maximum total carry amount* (sum of all `c_j` for j≥1) possible when expressing `n = a + b` for non-negative integers `a, b`. Input: single integer `n`. Output: single integer (the max total carries).

**Solution Explanation**:  
This is the subproblem of computing `total_carry` from our algorithm (the full digit sum is `digitSum(n) + 9 × this`). Use the exact greedy: process digits LSB-first, set `carry_out=1` at non-last positions if `target ∈ [0,18]`. Counts the max number of 1-carries. Optimal by same reasoning (propagation maximizes count).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
n = int(data)

if n == 0:
    print(0)
    sys.exit()

digits = []
temp = n
while temp > 0:
    digits.append(temp % 10)
    temp //= 10

m = len(digits)
carry_in = 0
total_carries = 0
for i in range(m):
    d = digits[i]
    if i == m - 1:
        carry_out = 0
    else:
        target = d - carry_in + 10
        if 0 <= target <= 18:
            carry_out = 1
            total_carries += 1
        else:
            carry_out = 0
    carry_in = carry_out

print(total_carries)
```

### Example Problem 2: Max Digit Sum for x ≤ n (AtCoder-style digit greedy, ABC div2 D-ish)
**Problem Description**:  
Given `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^18), find the maximum possible digit sum of any integer `x` (0 ≤ x ≤ n). Input: `T` test cases, then `T` lines with `n`. Output: `T` lines with answers. (T≤10^5, but log n small.)

**Solution Explanation**:  
This uses *left-to-right greedy with borrow propagation* (dual to our right-to-left carries). Convert `n` to digit list (MSB first). To max S(x), construct x by copying prefix of n, then at first opportunity decrease a digit >0 by 1 and fill remainder with 9's; compute S for that x (optimal single construction). Repeat for shorter lengths (all 9's). But efficient: actually, the max is `9 * (len-1)` if possible, else adjust leading. Standard impl: simulate subtraction of minimal to fill 9's from right.
- Extract digits MSB-first.
- Propagate "borrow" from right: set trailing to 9 until hit non-9, decrease it.
- Compute S of resulting x (always optimal).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    n_str = data[index]
    index += 1
    digits = [int(c) for c in n_str]
    l = len(digits)
    
    # Find rightmost non-9, decrease it, fill right 9's
    i = l - 1
    while i >= 0 and digits[i] == 9:
        i -= 1
    if i >= 0:
        digits[i] -= 1
        for j in range(i + 1, l):
            digits[j] = 9
        # Now sum (ignore leading zeros implicitly)
        s = sum(digits)
    else:
        # All 9's, max is 9*(l-1)
        s = 9 * (l - 1)
    
    print(s)
```
*(Note: This works e.g. n=100 → digits[1,0,0] → i=0 (0!=9? wait adjust: actually standard impl needs handling leading after decrease. This is simplified; for full, use string manip. But illustrates greedy digit adjust.)*

### Example Problem 3: Min Changes for No-Carry Addition (LeetCode/CP style, e.g., 2828-ish variant)
**Problem Description**:  
Given `n` as string (1 ≤ |s| ≤ 10^5, but here small), find the minimum number of digit increases (each ≤9) to `n`'s digits so that there exist a,b with a + b = new_n and **no carries** anywhere. Output the min total increases.

**Solution Explanation**:  
No carries means for every pos `a_i + b_i = d_i` exactly (`c_in=0 always`). To enable, need `d_i ≤ 18`, but since no carry prop, actually per pos `d_i ≤ 9*2=18` always true? Wait, variant: assume we can only *increase* digits of n to make it possible for some split with no carry (i.e. each new_d_i ≤18, but min increases). Trivial. Better: min changes so n can be sum WITHOUT carry, meaning each d_i ≤9 (since max a_i+b_i=9+9=18 but no carry means <10? No carry means a_i + b_i <10 per pos? Standard "check no carry" is sum digits <10 per pos.
Adjusted: Min increases to digits (each change +1 to +9-d_i) so that EVERY d_i' ≤9 (trivially 0), no.
**Real variant**: Min cost to make all d_i ≤8, so can always split with carry prop possible? But to fit.
Simple: Process right-to-left, if d_i >9? Impossible. But assume valid.
Actually, for this: to enable MAX carry possible=0? Opposite.
**Corrected simple**: Compute min digit changes (increase by 1 each time, cost=amount) to make carry propagation possible across ALL positions (force full carry chain).
But to fit: greedy right-left, for each pos compute min increase to d_i so target for c_out=1 feasible.

**Python3 Code** (for min increases to enable full carry chain):
```python
n_str = input().strip()
digits = [int(c) for c in n_str[::-1]]  # LSB first
m = len(digits)
carry_in = 0
total_cost = 0
for i in range(m):
    d = digits[i]
    if i == m-1:
        target = d - carry_in
        if target < 0:
            total_cost += -target  # increase d
        carry_out = 0
    else:
        # Min increase to make target for carry_out=1 feasible
        target = d - carry_in + 10
        if target > 18:
            inc = target - 18
            total_cost += inc
            target = 18
        elif target < 0:
            inc = -target
            total_cost += inc
            target = 0
        carry_out = 1
    carry_in = carry_out
print(total_cost)
```
*(Illustrates similar greedy adjustment for carry feasibility.)*

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
We implement the greedy algorithm described: extract digits LSB-first while computing `digitSum(n)`. Process each position, greedily setting `carry_out=1` for non-last positions if feasible (`target ∈ [0,18]`), accumulating `total_carries`. Final answer: `digitSum(n) + 9 * total_carries`. Handles `n=0`. O(log n) time, perfect for `n ≤ 10^{10}`. No need to construct `a,b`.

```python
n = int(input())

if n == 0:
    print(0)
else:
    digits = []
    s = 0
    temp = n
    while temp > 0:
        d = temp % 10
        digits.append(d)
        s += d
        temp //= 10
    
    m = len(digits)
    carry_in = 0
    total_carries = 0
    for i in range(m):
        d = digits[i]
        if i == m - 1:
            carry_out = 0
        else:
            target = d - carry_in + 10
            if 0 <= target <= 18:
                carry_out = 1
                total_carries += 1
            else:
                carry_out = 0
        carry_in = carry_out
    
    print(s + 9 * total_carries)
```

## Algorithms
The core concept is **2D axis-aligned rectangle packing** for two squares. Specifically, it involves determining if two squares of side lengths `a` and `b` can be packed without overlap into a rectangle of dimensions `m` x `n`. 

Key insights:
- Squares are rotation-invariant (90-degree rotations don't change shape).
- The optimal non-overlapping placements are **adjacent placements**: either side-by-side (bounding box: `(a + b) x max(a, b)`) or one-above-the-other (bounding box: `max(a, b) x (a + b)`).
- No other placements (e.g., L-shaped or offset) reduce the bounding box size for squares; they either match or exceed these dimensions.
- The squares fit if **at least one** of these bounding boxes fits entirely within the suitcase `(m, n)`, i.e., `(width <= m and height <= n)`.
- Additional checks like area (`a*a + b*b <= m*n`) are necessary but insufficient (shape matters).
- Time complexity: O(1), pure geometry.

No advanced algorithms (e.g., dynamic programming or backtracking) needed due to only two items.

## Tutorial
Packing two squares into a rectangle is a basic **geometric fitting problem** common in competitive programming under "geometry" or "ad-hoc" categories. Here's a step-by-step guide:

1. **Normalize inputs**: Assume `a <= b` by swapping if needed (optional, as `max(a, b)` handles it).
2. **Identify minimal bounding boxes**:
   - Horizontal adjacency: width = `a + b`, height = `max(a, b)`.
   - Vertical adjacency: width = `max(a, b)`, height = `a + b`.
3. **Check fitting**:
   - For horizontal: `a + b <= m` and `max(a, b) <= n`.
   - For vertical: `max(a, b) <= m` and `a + b <= n`.
   - Return true if either holds.
4. **Edge cases**:
   - Zero/negative sizes: Typically invalid, but assume positive integers.
   - Equal sizes: Simplifies to `2*a <= m and a <= n` or `a <= m and 2*a <= n`.
   - Tight fits: e.g., `a=1, b=1, m=1, n=2` → true (vertical).
5. **Why it works**: Any valid packing must cover at least these dimensions in one orientation; proofs via pigeonhole principle on projections (x and y spans).
6. **Extensions**:
   - More squares: Use shelf-packing or guillotine cuts (NP-hard, but approximations exist).
   - Rotatable rectangles: Check 4 orientations per item.
   - In CP: Often combined with sorting (sort sides) or BFS for small N.

Visualize:
```
Horizontal: [a][b]  -> (a+b) x max(a,b)
Vertical: 
  [ ]  
 [a][b] -> max(a,b) x (a+b)
```
Practice by computing manually for examples.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Rectangle Fitting with Rotation (Similar to "Cake in Box" from CodeChef)
**Problem Description**: You are given a cake of rectangular dimensions `w` x `h` and a box of dimensions `l` x `b`. Determine if the cake can fit inside the box, allowing 90-degree rotation of the cake (but not the box). Input: First line T (test cases), then T lines each with 4 integers `w h l b`. Output: "YES" or "NO" per test case.

**Solution Explanation**: Unlike squares, rectangles have orientation. Check both orientations:
- No rotation: `w <= l and h <= b`.
- Rotated: `h <= l and w <= b`.
This is O(1) per test. Handles all cases since only two orientations possible. Area check optional (insufficient alone).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    w = int(data[index])
    h = int(data[index + 1])
    l = int(data[index + 2])
    b = int(data[index + 3])
    index += 4
    if (w <= l and h <= b) or (h <= l and w <= b):
        print("YES")
    else:
        print("NO")
```

### Example Problem 2: Two Identical Squares in Rectangle (Variant of "Square Packing" from AtCoder ABC)
**Problem Description**: Given suitcase `m` x `n` and square side `s`, can you fit **exactly two** identical squares of side `s`? Input: First line T, then T lines with `s m n`. Output: "Yes" or "No" (case-sensitive). Assume `1 <= s,m,n <= 10^9`.

**Solution Explanation**: Special case of original (`a = b = s`). Bounding boxes: horizontal `(2*s) x s`, vertical `s x (2*s)`.
- Check: `(2*s <= m and s <= n)` or `(s <= m and 2*s <= n)`.
- O(1), careful with large numbers (Python handles bigints). Area `2*s^2 <= m*n` insufficient (e.g., s=2, m=3,n=3: 8<=9 but false).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
T = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    s = int(data[index])
    m = int(data[index + 1])
    n = int(data[index + 2])
    index += 3
    if (2 * s <= m and s <= n) or (s <= m and 2 * s <= n):
        print("Yes")
    else:
        print("No")
```

### Example Problem 3: Largest Square Pair in Bin (Inspired by "Max Squares" from Codeforces)
**Problem Description**: Given bin `m` x `n`, find the maximum side length `k` such that two squares of side `k` fit (output `k`, or 0 if impossible). Input: Single line `m n`. Constraints: `1 <= m,n <= 10^9`. Output: single integer.

**Solution Explanation**: Binary search on `k` (low=0, high=min(m,n)//1). For mid=k, check if two `k x k` fit using the two-square condition. Since monotonic, log(10^9)=30 checks. Optimal `k` is min(floor(m/2), n) or min(m, floor(n/2)), but verify:
- Max possible: `k = min(m//2, n)` if horizontal fits, or `min(m, n//2)` vertical.
- Take max of those two candidates, as direct formula.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
m, n = map(int, input().split())
k1 = min(m // 2, n)
k2 = min(m, n // 2)
k = max(k1, k2)
print(k)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem:
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read four integers `a`, `b`, `m`, `n` from standard input (one per line). Define `fit_in(a, b, m, n)` that returns `True` if either horizontal packing `(a + b <= m and max(a, b) <= n)` or vertical packing `(max(a, b) <= m and a + b <= n)` fits. Call the function and print the boolean result (`True` or `False`). This covers all cases in O(1) time, matching examples (e.g., 1+2=3<=3, max=2<=2).

```python
def fit_in(a, b, m, n):
    return (a + b <= m and max(a, b) <= n) or (max(a, b) <= m and a + b <= n)

a = int(input())
b = int(input())
m = int(input())
n = int(input())
print(fit_in(a, b, m, n))
```

## Algorithms
- **Brute Force**: Iterate over all pairs (i, j) where i < j and check if arr[i] > arr[j], incrementing a counter if true. Time complexity: O(n²), suitable only for small n (e.g., n ≤ 10³).
- **Merge Sort (Divide and Conquer)**: A modified version of merge sort that counts inversions during the merge step. Split inversions (across halves) are counted when merging sorted halves: if an element from the right half is taken before the left half's current element, all remaining elements in the left half form inversions with it. Intra-half inversions are recursively counted. Time complexity: O(n log n).
- **Fenwick Tree (Binary Indexed Tree, BIT)**: After coordinate compression (mapping values to ranks 1 to n'), process the array left-to-right. For each element, query the number of previously inserted elements with higher rank (sum from rank+1 to max_rank), add to inversion count, then insert the current rank. Time complexity: O(n log n).
- **Segment Tree**: Similar to BIT, supports range sum queries and updates for counting higher-ranked previous elements. Time complexity: O(n log n).

The core efficient algorithms are Merge Sort and Fenwick Tree, both O(n log n), ideal for competitive programming (n up to 10⁵–10⁶).

## Tutorial
### Brute Force (Simple but Inefficient)
```python
def brute_inversions(arr):
    count = 0
    for i in range(len(arr)):
        for j in range(i+1, len(arr)):
            if arr[i] > arr[j]:
                count += 1
    return count
```
This works for the example `(1,2,5,3,4,7,6)`: pairs (2,5)-(3), (2,5)-(4), (5,7)-(6) → 3.

### Merge Sort for Inversions (Recommended for Simplicity)
Merge sort recursively splits the array into halves, sorts them, and merges while counting **split inversions**:
1. **Base case**: Single element → 0 inversions.
2. **Recursive step**: Count inversions in left half + right half.
3. **Merge step**:
   - Two sorted halves: left `[l_start..mid]`, right `[mid+1..end]`.
   - Use a temporary array.
   - While merging:
     - If `left[i] <= right[j]`, take `left[i]`.
     - Else (`left[i] > right[j]`), take `right[j]` and add `(mid - i + 1)` to count (all remaining left elements > `right[j]`, since left is sorted).
   - Copy leftovers.

**Example walkthrough** for `(1,2,5,3,4,7,6)`:
- Split: left=`(1,2,5)`, right=`(3,4,7,6)`
- Left: split `(1,2)` (0 inv), `(5)` (0 inv); merge: `1<=5,2<=5` → 0 split inv → sorted `[1,2,5]`, total left=0
- Right: split `(3,4)` (0), `(7,6)`: merge `7>6` → when take 6, add 1 → sorted `[6,7]`, subtotal=1; now merge `(3,4)`+`(6,7)`: `3<6,4<6,4<7` → 0 split → sorted `[3,4,6,7]`, total right=1
- Merge left+right: `[1,2,5]` + `[3,4,6,7]`
  - 1<3 → take 1
  - 2<3 → take 2
  - 5>3 → take 3, add 1 (5 left)
  - 5>4 → take 4, add 1 (5 left)
  - 5<6 → take 5
  - ... rest no inv
  - Split inv=2
- Total: 0 (left) + 1 (right) + 2 (split) = 3 ✓

**Advantages**: Stable, handles duplicates/negatives, no compression needed. **Python tip**: Use indices + temp array to avoid O(n log n) slicing overhead; set `sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)` for n=10⁵.

### Fenwick Tree (Faster in Practice for Large n)
1. **Coordinate compression**: Sort unique values, map to ranks 1 to m (m ≤ n).
2. Initialize Fenwick Tree (prefix sums).
3. Left-to-right:
   - `r = rank[arr[j]]`
   - Query sum(r+1 to m) → previous elements > arr[j]
   - Update: add 1 at r
4. Fenwick operations:
   ```python
   def update(idx, val): while idx <= size: tree[idx] += val; idx += idx & -idx
   def query(idx): res=0; while idx>0: res += tree[idx]; idx -= idx & -idx; return res
   # sum(l to r) = query(r) - query(l-1)
   ```

**Example**: `(1,2,5,3,4,7,6)` → unique sorted `[1,2,3,4,5,6,7]` ranks 1-7.
- Process: 1 (r=1), query(2-7)=0, inv=0, insert1
- 2(2), query(3-7)=0, inv=0, insert2
- 5(5), query(6-7)=0, inv=0, insert5
- 3(3), query(4-7)=1 (5), inv=1, insert3
- 4(4), query(5-7)=1 (5), inv=2, insert4
- 7(7), query(8-7)=0, inv=2, insert7
- 6(6), query(7-7)=1 (7), inv=3, insert6 ✓

**Advantages**: Iterative (no recursion), fast constants. **Use when**: n=10⁶ or queries heavy.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Inversion Count (Merge Sort)
**Problem Description**: You are given an integer n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10⁵), followed by an array of n integers (0 ≤ a_i ≤ 10⁹). Output the number of inversions (pairs i < j where a_i > a_j).

**Solution Explanation**: Use the index-based modified merge sort. It sorts the array in-place (using a temp array) while counting:
- `_msort(left, right)`: Recurse on halves, then merge.
- `_merge`: Counts split inversions precisely when right element is smaller. Total time O(n log n), space O(n).
Handles duplicates (≤ comparison), negatives, large values.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(100010)

def _merge(arr, temp, left, mid, right):
    i = left
    j = mid + 1
    k = left
    inv_count = 0
    while i <= mid and j <= right:
        if arr[i] <= arr[j]:
            temp[k] = arr[i]
            i += 1
        else:
            temp[k] = arr[j]
            inv_count += (mid - i + 1)
            j += 1
        k += 1
    while i <= mid:
        temp[k] = arr[i]
        i += 1
        k += 1
    while j <= right:
        temp[k] = arr[j]
        j += 1
        k += 1
    for idx in range(left, right + 1):
        arr[idx] = temp[idx]
    return inv_count

def _msort(arr, temp, left, right):
    inv_count = 0
    if left < right:
        mid = (left + right) // 2
        inv_count += _msort(arr, temp, left, mid)
        inv_count += _msort(arr, temp, mid + 1, right)
        inv_count += _merge(arr, temp, left, mid, right)
    return inv_count

def count_inversions(arr):
    n = len(arr)
    temp = [0] * n
    return _msort(arr, temp, 0, n - 1)

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
arr = list(map(int, data[1:1 + n]))
print(count_inversions(arr))
```

### Example Problem 2: Inversion Count (Fenwick Tree)
**Problem Description**: Same as Problem 1: n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10⁵), array of n integers (possibly negative, duplicates; |a_i| ≤ 10⁹). Output number of inversions.

**Solution Explanation**: Coordinate compress to ranks 1..m (m≤n). Use Fenwick Tree for prefix sums.
- Left-to-right: For each a[j], inv += query(rank[a[j]]+1 to m) [previous larger].
- update(rank[a[j]], 1).
Fenwick: lowbit trick for O(log m) update/query. Handles all cases perfectly.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys

class FenwickTree:
    def __init__(self, size):
        self.size = size
        self.tree = [0] * (size + 2)

    def update(self, idx, val):
        while idx <= self.size:
            self.tree[idx] += val
            idx += idx & -idx

    def query(self, idx):
        res = 0
        while idx > 0:
            res += self.tree[idx]
            idx -= idx & -idx
        return res

def count_inversions(arr):
    if not arr:
        return 0
    sorted_unique = sorted(set(arr))
    rank = {val: i + 1 for i, val in enumerate(sorted_unique)}
    m = len(sorted_unique)
    ft = FenwickTree(m)
    inv = 0
    for num in arr:
        r = rank[num]
        inv += ft.query(m) - ft.query(r)
        ft.update(r, 1)
    return inv

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
arr = list(map(int, data[1:1 + n]))
print(count_inversions(arr))
```

### Example Problem 3: Minimum Adjacent Swaps to Sort
**Problem Description**: Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10⁵) and array of n DISTINCT integers (1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10⁹). Output the minimum number of adjacent swaps to sort the array in ascending order.

**Solution Explanation**: Each adjacent swap reduces the inversion count by exactly 1 (swaps a larger before smaller). Bubble sort performs exactly this many swaps. Any sequence of adjacent swaps to sort requires precisely the inversion count (invariant). Thus, compute inversions using merge sort (as in Example 1). Time O(n log n).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(100010)

def _merge(arr, temp, left, mid, right):
    i = left
    j = mid + 1
    k = left
    inv_count = 0
    while i <= mid and j <= right:
        if arr[i] <= arr[j]:
            temp[k] = arr[i]
            i += 1
        else:
            temp[k] = arr[j]
            inv_count += (mid - i + 1)
            j += 1
        k += 1
    while i <= mid:
        temp[k] = arr[i]
        i += 1
        k += 1
    while j <= right:
        temp[k] = arr[j]
        j += 1
        k += 1
    for idx in range(left, right + 1):
        arr[idx] = temp[idx]
    return inv_count

def _msort(arr, temp, left, right):
    inv_count = 0
    if left < right:
        mid = (left + right) // 2
        inv_count += _msort(arr, temp, left, mid)
        inv_count += _msort(arr, temp, mid + 1, right)
        inv_count += _merge(arr, temp, left, mid, right)
    return inv_count

def min_adj_swaps(arr):
    n = len(arr)
    temp = [0] * n
    return _msort(arr, temp, 0, n - 1)

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
arr = list(map(int, data[1:1 + n]))
print(min_adj_swaps(arr))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Use the efficient O(n log n) index-based modified merge sort (as detailed in the tutorial and Example 1). It handles the given input format: a single line like `[1, 2, 5, 3, 4, 7, 6]`. Parse by stripping brackets and splitting on `', '`, convert to ints. Compute inversions, print the count. Sets recursion limit for safety. Works for duplicates/negatives/large n.

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)

def _merge(arr, temp, left, mid, right):
    i = left
    j = mid + 1
    k = left
    inv_count = 0
    while i <= mid and j <= right:
        if arr[i] <= arr[j]:
            temp[k] = arr[i]
            i += 1
        else:
            temp[k] = arr[j]
            inv_count += (mid - i + 1)
            j += 1
        k += 1
    while i <= mid:
        temp[k] = arr[i]
        i += 1
        k += 1
    while j <= right:
        temp[k] = arr[j]
        j += 1
        k += 1
    for idx in range(left, right + 1):
        arr[idx] = temp[idx]
    return inv_count

def _msort(arr, temp, left, right):
    inv_count = 0
    if left < right:
        mid = (left + right) // 2
        inv_count += _msort(arr, temp, left, mid)
        inv_count += _msort(arr, temp, mid + 1, right)
        inv_count += _merge(arr, temp, left, mid, right)
    return inv_count

def count_inversions(arr):
    n = len(arr)
    if n <= 1:
        return 0
    temp = [0] * n
    return _msort(arr, temp, 0, n - 1)

# Read input
line = input().strip()
if line.startswith('[') and line.endswith(']'):
    nums_str = line[1:-1].split(', ')
else:
    nums_str = line.split()
arr = [int(x) for x in nums_str]
print(count_inversions(arr))
```

## Algorithms
- **Counting and Feasibility Check**: Count vowels (a, e, i, o, u) and consonants. Feasibility requires |vowels - consonants| ≤ 1 to allow strict alternation.
- **Pattern Determination**: If vowel count == consonant count, start with vowel. If vowels = consonants + 1, start with vowel. If consonants = vowels + 1, start with consonant.
- **Greedy Sorting and Merging**: Sort vowels and consonants in ascending order separately. Interleave them into the fixed alternation pattern (V-C-V-C... or C-V-C-V...) by assigning the smallest unused vowel/consonant to each required position in sequence. This ensures lexicographical minimality because earlier positions prioritize smaller characters, and lex order is prefix-determined.
- **Core Algorithm**: Greedy assignment from sorted pools to constrained positions (alternation enforces position types).

## Tutorial
Solving problems requiring the lexicographically smallest string under constraints (e.g., alternation of two character groups) involves these steps:

1. **Separate into Groups**: Partition the input into required subsets (e.g., vowels vs. consonants) based on properties.
2. **Feasibility Check**: Verify counts allow the constraint (here, |group1 - group2| ≤ 1 for alternation in a linear string).
3. **Determine Pattern**: Decide the starting group based on rules (e.g., prefer one group when equal).
4. **Sort Each Group**: Sort subsets ascending (`sorted()`) to enable greedy smallest-first assignment.
5. **Greedy Merge**:
   - Traverse positions 0 to N-1.
   - For each position, determine required group (e.g., even indices vowel if starting vowel).
   - Append the next smallest from that group's sorted list.
6. **Why Greedy Works**: Lex smallest means minimizing prefix. Assigning the smallest possible at position i (constrained by group) cannot be improved by swapping later elements, as it would either worsen i or keep prefix identical (but we want global min). Proof by contradiction: if optimal differs at first mismatch j, optimal must use larger at j (violating greedy) or rearrange earlier (impossible by induction).

**Edge Cases**:
- Length 0/1: Handle trivially (feasible if |diff| ≤ 1).
- All one type: Feasible only if length ≤ 1.
- Time: O(N log N) from sorting (N ≤ 10^5 typical).

**Implementation Tip**: Use lists for groups, indices to pop/track next element. Avoid `pop(0)` on lists (O(N)); use index counters.

This applies to similar problems: alternating parity, cases, or custom groups.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Smallest Even-Odd Digit String
**Problem Description**: Given a string `S` of digits '0'-'9' (lowercase input, stdin), separate into even digits (0,2,4,6,8) and odd digits (1,3,5,7,9). Form the lexicographically smallest string alternating even-odd, following rules: if counts equal, start even; if even=odd+1 start even; else start odd. If |even-odd| > 1, output "IMPOSSIBLE". No leading zero concerns (output as-is).

**Solution Explanation**: Identical to original: count E/O, check |E-O|≤1, determine start_even (True if E>=O), sort even/odd lists, interleave using position parity: if start_even, even on even indices.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()

even = sorted(c for c in data if int(c) % 2 == 0)
odd = sorted(c for c in data if int(c) % 2 == 1)
E, O = len(even), len(odd)

if abs(E - O) > 1:
    print("IMPOSSIBLE")
else:
    start_even = (E >= O)
    even_idx, odd_idx = 0, 0
    result = []
    for i in range(E + O):
        if (i % 2 == 0) == start_even:
            result.append(even[even_idx])
            even_idx += 1
        else:
            result.append(odd[odd_idx])
            odd_idx += 1
    print(''.join(result))
```

### Example Problem 2: Lex Smallest Alternating Parity Array String
**Problem Description**: Given a string `S` of lowercase letters 'a'-'z', classify as "low" ('a'-'m') and "high" ('n'-'z'). Rearrange to smallest string alternating low-high. Rules: equal counts → start low; low=high+1 → start low; high=low+1 → start high. If |low-high|>1, "FAILED". Input stdin, output stdout.

**Solution Explanation**: Analogous: groups are low/high instead of vowel/cons. Sort each, feasibility same, start_low if low >= high. Merge greedily by index parity matching start.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()

low = sorted(c for c in data if 'a' <= c <= 'm')
high = sorted(c for c in data if 'n' <= c <= 'z')
L, H = len(low), len(high)

if abs(L - H) > 1:
    print("FAILED")
else:
    start_low = (L >= H)
    low_idx, high_idx = 0, 0
    result = []
    for i in range(L + H):
        if (i % 2 == 0) == start_low:
            result.append(low[low_idx])
            low_idx += 1
        else:
            result.append(high[high_idx])
            high_idx += 1
    print(''.join(result))
```

### Example Problem 3: Smallest Merging of Two Sorted Groups
**Problem Description**: Given string `S`, split into group A (vowels + digits) and group B (consonants + letters b-z). Form lex smallest alternating A-B string. Equal → start A; A=B+1 → A; B=A+1 → B. |A-B|>1 → "NO". Stdin input.

**Solution Explanation**: Generalizes: define groups dynamically, sort, same count/start/merge logic. Demonstrates extensibility to custom partitions.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()

def is_A(c):
    return c in 'aeiou0123456789'

A = sorted(c for c in data if is_A(c))
B = sorted(c for c in data if not is_A(c))
An, Bn = len(A), len(B)

if abs(An - Bn) > 1:
    print("NO")
else:
    start_A = (An >= Bn)
    a_idx, b_idx = 0, 0
    result = []
    for i in range(An + Bn):
        if (i % 2 == 0) == start_A:
            result.append(A[a_idx])
            a_idx += 1
        else:
            result.append(B[b_idx])
            b_idx += 1
    print(''.join(result))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read string from stdin. Collect and sort vowels/consonants. Compute counts V/C. If |V-C|>1, "failed". Else, set `start_vowel = (V >= C)`. Use index trackers to build result: for each position i, if `(i % 2 == 0) == start_vowel`, take next vowel; else next consonant. This greedily assigns smallest to earliest possible positions per type, yielding lex min. O(N log N) time.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
s = input().strip()

vowels = sorted(c for c in s if c in 'aeiou')
cons = sorted(c for c in s if c not in 'aeiou')
V, C = len(vowels), len(cons)

if abs(V - C) > 1:
    print("failed")
else:
    start_vowel = (V >= C)
    v_idx, c_idx = 0, 0
    result = []
    for i in range(V + C):
        if (i % 2 == 0) == start_vowel:
            result.append(vowels[v_idx])
            v_idx += 1
        else:
            result.append(cons[c_idx])
            c_idx += 1
    print(''.join(result))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Finding the minimum value in an array**: Traverse the array once (linear scan, O(N) time) to identify the smallest element, initializing with the first element or infinity.
- **Finding the maximum value in an array**: Similarly, traverse the array once to identify the largest element, initializing with the first element or negative infinity.
- **Computing the difference**: Simple subtraction (O(1)) between max and min.
These can be implemented via manual loops for control or Python's built-in `min()` and `max()` functions for simplicity (both O(N) time, O(1) extra space). Edge cases include single-element arrays (min=max, diff=0), all equal elements, empty arrays (though unlikely), and zero/negative ages (handled naturally).

## Tutorial
Finding min/max in an array is fundamental in competitive programming for range queries, statistics, and optimizations.

### Manual Implementation (Loop):
```python
def find_min_max(arr):
    if not arr:
        return None, None
    min_val = max_val = arr[0]
    for num in arr[1:]:
        if num < min_val:
            min_val = num
        if num > max_val:
            max_val = num
    return min_val, max_val
```
- **Time**: O(N), **Space**: O(1).
- Initialize with first element to handle small arrays.
- Single pass combines min/max search, efficient.

### Built-in Functions:
```python
min_val, max_val = min(arr), max(arr)
```
- Faster in practice due to C-optimized implementation.
- Handles empty lists by raising ValueError (check len(arr) > 0).

### When to Use Each:
- **Built-ins**: For simplicity, readability in contests (Python allows it).
- **Loops**: Interviews, custom logic (e.g., indices), or non-Python langs.
- **Complexity**: Always O(N) worst-case; no better general algorithm without preprocessing (e.g., segment trees for ranges: O(N log N) build, O(1) query).
- **Edge Cases**: N=1 (diff=0), sorted/unsorted (doesn't matter), negatives/zeros.
- **Optimizations**: For huge N (10^5+), single pass is ideal; avoid sorting (O(N log N)).

Practice by modifying for second min/max or counting extremes.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: "Minimum and Maximum in Array" (Inspired by Codeforces A problems)
**Problem Description**:  
You are given multiple test cases. For each test case:  
- First line: integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^5), number of elements.  
- Second line: N integers A_i (0 ≤ A_i ≤ 10^9).  
Output for each test case: two space-separated integers, the minimum and maximum values in the array.

**Solution Explanation**:  
Read T (number of test cases). For each: read N, read array, compute min and max using built-ins or loop (built-ins fine for N=10^5). Print min max. Single pass per test case, total time O(T*N) = O(10^5). Handles large inputs efficiently. Edge: N=1.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
T = int(data[index])
index += 1
for _ in range(T):
    N = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    arr = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(N)]
    index += N
    print(min(arr), max(arr))
```

### Example Problem 2: "Maximum Difference" (LeetCode 1984 / AtCoder-style)
**Problem Description**:  
Single test case.  
- First line: integer N (2 ≤ N ≤ 10^5).  
- Second line: N integers A_i (-10^9 ≤ A_i ≤ 10^9).  
Output a single integer: the maximum difference (max(A) - min(A)).

**Solution Explanation**:  
Read N and array. Find min and max in one pass (initialize min=inf, max=-inf). Compute diff = max - min. O(N) time. Why not sort? Sorting O(N log N) slower. Edge: negatives (diff still positive), N=2 (direct subtract).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
N = int(data[0])
arr = list(map(int, data[1:]))
min_val = float('inf')
max_val = float('-inf')
for num in arr:
    if num < min_val:
        min_val = num
    if num > max_val:
        max_val = num
diff = max_val - min_val
print(diff)
```

### Example Problem 3: "Age Range Statistics" (Similar to CodeChef Starter problems)
**Problem Description**:  
Multiple test cases. For each:  
- First line: integer N (1 ≤ N ≤ 10^3).  
- Second line: N integers ages (0 ≤ age ≤ 120).  
Output for each test case: three space-separated integers [youngest, oldest, count of members strictly between youngest and oldest].

**Solution Explanation**:  
Read T. For each test case: read N, array. Find min_age and max_age (loop or built-in). Count elements where min_age < age < max_age (O(N)). If N=1 or all equal, count=0. Time O(T*N)=O(10^6) fine. Builds on min/max by adding conditional count.

```python
T = int(input())
for _ in range(T):
    N = int(input())
    ages = list(map(int, input().split()))
    min_age = min(ages)
    max_age = max(ages)
    count_between = sum(1 for age in ages if min_age < age < max_age)
    print(min_age, max_age, count_between)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**:  
  Assume standard competitive input: first line N (number of family members, 1 ≤ N ≤ 10^5), second line N non-negative integers (ages, 0 ≤ age). Use built-in `min()` and `max()` for efficiency (O(N)). Compute difference as max - min. Print single line: "youngest oldest difference" space-separated. Handles edges: N=1 (diff=0), all zeros, babies (0). No multiple test cases per problem statement. Use `sys.stdin.read()` for fast input if large N.

```python
import sys

# Read all input at once for efficiency
data = sys.stdin.read().split()
N = int(data[0])
ages = list(map(int, data[1:1+N]))

if N == 0:
    # Edge case, though unlikely
    print("0 0 0")
else:
    youngest = min(ages)
    oldest = max(ages)
    difference = oldest - youngest
    print(youngest, oldest, difference)
```

## Algorithms:
- **Trial division up to square root**: Iterate from 1 to √n to find divisors efficiently, as divisors come in pairs (d and n/d) where one is ≤ √n.
- **Pair collection**: For each divisor i ≤ √n, add i and n//i (if distinct) to avoid missing larger divisors and duplicates.
- **Sorting**: Collect all divisors in a list and sort in descending order.
- **Input validation**: Parse input as integer; return -1 if invalid or n < 1.

## Tutorial:
Finding all divisors of a number n efficiently is a fundamental technique in competitive programming, especially for problems involving factorization, divisor counts, sums, or multiples. Naive O(n) checking (1 to n) is too slow for n up to 10^12. Instead, use **trial division up to √n** (O(√n) time, ~10^6 operations max for n=10^12):

1. Import `math` for `sqrt`.
2. Initialize an empty list `divisors = []`.
3. Loop `i` from 1 to `int(math.sqrt(n)) + 1`:
   - If `n % i == 0`:
     - Append `i` to `divisors`.
     - If `i != n // i`, append `n // i` (handles pairs; skips duplicate for perfect squares like 36 where i=6).
4. Sort the list: `divisors.sort(reverse=True)` for descending order.
5. Edge cases:
   - n=1: divisors=[1]
   - n prime: [n, 1]
   - n<1 or non-integer: handle separately.

This captures all positive divisors exactly once. Time: O(√n + d log d) where d is number of divisors (~log n factors). Space: O(d). Use counters/sums instead of lists for count/sum problems to optimize space.

## Example Problems:

### Example Problem 1: Divisor Count
**Problem Description**: Given a single integer n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), output the number of positive divisors of n.  
**Sample Input**:  
```
54
```  
**Sample Output**:  
```
8
```

**Solution Explanation**: Use trial division up to √n, but instead of storing divisors, maintain a counter. For each i where n % i == 0, increment count by 1; if i != n//i, increment by another 1. This avoids list storage, ideal for large n. Handles perfect squares correctly (no double-count middle). Time O(√n).

```python
import sys
import math

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
try:
    n = int(data)
    if n < 1:
        print(0)  # or -1, but 1 has 1 divisor
        sys.exit()
except ValueError:
    print(0)
    sys.exit()

count = 0
for i in range(1, int(math.sqrt(n)) + 1):
    if n % i == 0:
        count += 1
        if i != n // i:
            count += 1
print(count)
```

### Example Problem 2: Sum of Divisors
**Problem Description**: Given a single integer n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), output the sum of all positive divisors of n.  
**Sample Input**:  
```
54
```  
**Sample Output**:  
```
120
```

**Solution Explanation**: Similar to divisor finding, but accumulate sum instead of list. For each i ≤ √n where n % i == 0, add i; if i != n//i, add n//i. No sorting needed. Efficient for large n; avoids overflow in Python (arbitrary precision). For n=1, sum=1. Time O(√n).

```python
import sys
import math

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
try:
    n = int(data)
    if n < 1:
        print(0)
        sys.exit()
except ValueError:
    print(0)
    sys.exit()

total = 0
for i in range(1, int(math.sqrt(n)) + 1):
    if n % i == 0:
        total += i
        if i != n // i:
            total += n // i
print(total)
```

### Example Problem 3: Largest Proper Divisor
**Problem Description**: Given a single integer n (2 ≤ n ≤ 10^12), output the largest divisor of n that is strictly less than n (proper divisor). If n is prime, output 1.  
**Sample Input**:  
```
54
```  
**Sample Output**:  
```
27
```

**Solution Explanation**: Find all divisors using trial division, store in list, sort descending, return second element (largest < n). Since n is always first after descending sort, index[1] works. For primes, list=[n,1], so 1. Efficient; could optimize by tracking max during loop (max(n//i for i=2 to √n)), but list is simple and d small. Time O(√n + d log d).

```python
import sys
import math

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
try:
    n = int(data)
    if n < 2:
        print(-1)
        sys.exit()
except ValueError:
    print(-1)
    sys.exit()

divisors = []
for i in range(1, int(math.sqrt(n)) + 1):
    if n % i == 0:
        divisors.append(i)
        if i != n // i:
            divisors.append(n // i)
divisors.sort(reverse=True)
print(divisors[1])  # largest < n
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem:
- **Explanation of the solution**: Read input as string, parse to int with try-except for validation. If invalid or n < 1, print -1. Else, use trial division up to √n to collect all divisors in a list (append i and n//i if distinct). Sort descending. Print the list directly (Python formats as [54, 27, ...]). Handles n=1 as [1]. Time O(√n + d log d), efficient. Full script for stdin/stdout.

```python
import sys
import math

def factors(n):
    divisors = []
    for i in range(1, int(math.sqrt(n)) + 1):
        if n % i == 0:
            divisors.append(i)
            if i != n // i:
                divisors.append(n // i)
    divisors.sort(reverse=True)
    return divisors

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().strip()
try:
    n = int(data)
except ValueError:
    print(-1)
else:
    if n < 1:
        print(-1)
    else:
        result = factors(n)
        print(result)
```

## Algorithms
- **Primality testing for the exponent n**: A necessary condition for \(2^n - 1\) to be prime is that n must be a prime number (for n > 1). This can be checked efficiently using trial division optimized with 6k±1 increments, which is O(√n) time, sufficient for n ≤ 2000.
- **Lucas-Lehmer test for Mersenne primality**: To check if \(M_n = 2^n - 1\) is prime (where n is an odd prime), compute the Lucas-Lehmer sequence starting with s₀ = 4, where s_{i} = (s_{i-1}² - 2) mod (2^n - 1) for i = 1 to n-2. If s_{n-2} ≡ 0 mod (2^n - 1), then \(M_n\) is prime. This is highly efficient for Mersenne numbers, leveraging Python's arbitrary-precision integers. Special case: n=2 is a Mersenne prime (3). Time complexity per test: O(n · b²), where b ≈ n bits, but Python's optimized bigint operations make it practical for n ≤ 2000 (~0.1s worst-case).

## Tutorial
Mersenne primes are primes of the form \(2^n - 1\), where n is prime (necessary but not sufficient). Direct primality testing on \(2^n - 1\) is infeasible for large n due to size, but the **Lucas-Lehmer test** exploits the special form:

1. **Verify n is prime**: Use trial division:
   ```
   def is_prime(n):
       if n <= 1: return False
       if n <= 3: return True
       if n % 2 == 0 or n % 3 == 0: return False
       i = 5
       while i * i <= n:
           if n % i == 0 or n % (i + 2) == 0: return False
           i += 6
       return True
   ```
   This skips multiples of 2/3 and checks 6k±1.

2. **Lucas-Lehmer algorithm**:
   - For n=2: Return True (\(2^2 - 1 = 3\)).
   - For odd prime n: Compute M = 2^n - 1 (Python: `(1 << n) - 1`).
   - Initialize s = 4.
   - Iterate n-2 times: s = (s * s - 2) % M.
   - If final s == 0, \(2^n - 1\) is prime.
   
   **Why it works**: The test is derived from Lucas pseudoprime criteria tailored to Mersenne numbers. The sequence relates to the order of 2 modulo factors of M_n. Proven correct for Mersenne form.

3. **Implementation tips**:
   - Python handles bigints natively; no overflow.
   - Modulo operation ensures s stays < M.
   - For n=2000 (~600 digits), ~2000 squarings; each O(n log n) with FFT mul, total <1s.
   - Edge cases: n<2 (False), even n>2 (False via is_prime).

This combination is deterministic, efficient, and standard for Mersenne primality.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Prime Checker (Basic Primality Testing)
**Problem Description**: In a competitive programming problem (similar to Codeforces "Prime Test" style), you are given T (1 ≤ T ≤ 10^5) integers n (2 ≤ n ≤ 10^6). For each, output "YES" if n is prime, else "NO". Time limit: 1s.

**Solution Explanation**: For multiple queries up to 10^6, precompute all primes using the Sieve of Eratosthenes (O(N log log N)). Create a boolean array is_prime[0..10^6], mark multiples of each prime starting from 2. Answer queries in O(1) using the array. Handles large T efficiently.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

MAXN = 1000001
is_prime = [True] * MAXN
is_prime[0] = is_prime[1] = False
for i in range(2, int(MAXN**0.5) + 1):
    if is_prime[i]:
        for j in range(i*i, MAXN, i):
            is_prime[j] = False

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    print("YES" if is_prime[n] else "NO")
```

### Example Problem 2: Large Prime Verification (Miller-Rabin)
**Problem Description**: (Similar to SPOJ "PRIME1" extension or Codeforces large prime check). Given T (1 ≤ T ≤ 100) numbers n (2 ≤ n ≤ 10^18). Output "YES" if n prime, "NO" otherwise. Need deterministic test for 64-bit numbers.

**Solution Explanation**: Use Miller-Rabin primality test, probabilistic but deterministic for n < 2^64 with fixed witnesses (2, 3, 5, 7, 11, 13, 23, 29, 31, 37). Write n-1 = 2^s * d. For each witness a: compute a^d mod n; if !=1 and !=n-1, square up to s times checking n-1. If passes all, prime. Python pow(a, exp, n) for fast modpow. O(T * k * log^3 n) with k=10 witnesses.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

def miller_rabin(n):
    if n < 2: return False
    if n in [2, 3]: return True
    if n % 2 == 0: return False
    # Write n-1 = 2^s * d
    s, d = 0, n - 1
    while d % 2 == 0:
        s += 1
        d //= 2
    # Witnesses for n < 2^64
    witnesses = [2, 3, 5, 7, 11, 13, 23, 29, 31, 37]
    for a in witnesses:
        if a >= n: break
        x = pow(a, d, n)
        if x == 1 or x == n - 1: continue
        cont = False
        for _ in range(s - 1):
            x = pow(x, 2, n)
            if x == n - 1:
                cont = True
                break
        if cont: continue
        return False
    return True

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    print("YES" if miller_rabin(n) else "NO")
```

### Example Problem 3: Mersenne Prime Checker (Lucas-Lehmer Application)
**Problem Description**: (Hypothetical, similar to Project Euler #50 extensions or UVa Mersenne problems). Given T (1 ≤ T ≤ 100) primes p (2 ≤ p ≤ 5000), check if 2^p - 1 is Mersenne prime. Output "YES"/"NO". Time limit: 2s.

**Solution Explanation**: Assume p prime (as given). Use Lucas-Lehmer directly: special case p=2, else iterate s = (s^2 - 2) % (2^p -1) for p-2 steps. Python bigints handle up to 5000 bits efficiently. Verify with is_prime(p) for safety. Time: feasible as explained.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

def is_prime(n):
    if n <= 1: return False
    if n <= 3: return True
    if n % 2 == 0 or n % 3 == 0: return False
    i = 5
    while i * i <= n:
        if n % i == 0 or n % (i + 2) == 0: return False
        i += 6
    return True

def mersenne_prime(p):
    if p == 2: return True
    if not is_prime(p): return False
    m = (1 << p) - 1
    s = 4
    for _ in range(p - 2):
        s = (s * s - 2) % m
    return s == 0

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    p = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    print("YES" if mersenne_prime(p) else "NO")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Implement `is_mersenne_prime(n)` using the algorithms above: check if n is prime (except n=2), then apply Lucas-Lehmer test. Handle input as standard competitive format: first line T (number of test cases), then T lines each with n (1 ≤ n ≤ 2000). For each, output "YES" if 2^n -1 is Mersenne prime, else "NO". This is efficient (total time <<1s even for T=1000, n=2000). Uses optimized is_prime and native bigints.

```python
import sys

def is_prime(n):
    if n <= 1: return False
    if n <= 3: return True
    if n % 2 == 0 or n % 3 == 0: return False
    i = 5
    while i * i <= n:
        if n % i == 0 or n % (i + 2) == 0: return False
        i += 6
    return True

def is_mersenne_prime(n):
    if n == 2:
        return True
    if n < 2 or not is_prime(n):
        return False
    m = (1 << n) - 1
    s = 4
    for _ in range(n - 2):
        s = (s * s - 2) % m
    return s == 0

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

t = int(data[0])
index = 1
for _ in range(t):
    n = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    print("YES" if is_mersenne_prime(n) else "NO")
```

## Algorithms
- **Digit Dynamic Programming (Digit DP)**: The core algorithm used to efficiently count the numbers from 1 to `n` (where `n` has up to 100 digits) that have exactly `k` non-zero digits. We process the digits of `n` from left to right (MSB to LSB), maintaining states for the current position, whether the prefix is tight (matching `n`'s prefix), the count of non-zero digits so far, and whether the number has started (leading zeros flag). Memoization avoids recomputing states, achieving O(100 * 2 * 101 * 2 * 10) = O(10^6) time complexity.

## Tutorial
**Digit Dynamic Programming (Digit DP)** is a powerful technique for counting numbers ≤ `N` (often very large, up to 10^18 or more) that satisfy digit-based constraints (e.g., sum of digits, count of specific digits, no adjacent repeats). Direct iteration is impossible for large `N`, so we treat `N` as a string and build numbers digit-by-digit.

### Key Steps:
1. **Convert N to digit array**: `s = str(N)`, `digits = [int(c) for c in s]`, `L = len(s)`.
2. **Define DP States**:
   - `pos`: Current digit position (0 to L-1).
   - `tight`: 1 if the prefix matches `N`'s prefix exactly (limits current digit ≤ `digits[pos]`), else 0 (digit 0-9).
   - Problem-specific: e.g., `cnt` (count of non-zero digits), `sum` (digit sum), `mod` (sum % M).
   - `lead`: 1 if only leading zeros placed so far (simulates shorter numbers; digits placed after start count fully).
3. **Transitions**:
   - Upper limit `up = digits[pos] if tight else 9`.
   - For `d` in 0 to `up`:
     - `new_tight = 1 if tight and d == digits[pos] else 0`.
     - Update problem state (e.g., if `lead and d == 0`: `new_lead=1`, `new_cnt = cnt`; else: `new_lead=0`, `new_cnt = cnt + (1 if d != 0 else 0)`).
     - Skip if state exceeds limit (e.g., `new_cnt > k`).
     - Recurse: `ans += dp(pos+1, new_tight, new_cnt, new_lead)`.
4. **Base Case**: `if pos == L`: return 1 if condition met (e.g., `cnt == k` and `lead == 0`), else 0.
5. **Memoization**: Use `@lru_cache(None)` or dict for states.
6. **Invocation**: `dp(0, 1, 0, 1)` (start at pos 0, tight=1, cnt=0, lead=1).
7. **Edge Cases**: Handles `N=0`, shorter numbers (via `lead`), excludes 0 for 1-based counts.

**Complexity**: O(L * 10 * S), where S is product of state sizes (e.g., 2 for tight/lead, K+1 for count). Ideal for L≤100, K≤100.
**Tips**: Use integers for flags (0/1). Prune impossible states. Python handles big integers natively.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Digit Sum Equals K
**Problem Description**: Given `N` (1 ≤ N < 10^100) and `K` (1 ≤ K ≤ 900), count integers X (1 ≤ X ≤ N) where the sum of digits of X equals exactly K.  
**Input**: First line: N (as string), second line: K.  
**Output**: Single integer (count).

**Solution Explanation**:  
Use Digit DP with states `dp[pos][tight][cur_sum][lead]`.  
- Track `cur_sum`: sum of digits placed (only after leading zeros end).  
- Transition: If `lead and d==0`: `new_sum = cur_sum`, `new_lead=1`. Else: `new_sum = cur_sum + d`, `new_lead=0`.  
- Prune if `new_sum > K`.  
- Base: `pos==L` and `cur_sum==K` and `lead==0` → 1.  
- Starts from `dp(0,1,0,1)`. Automatically handles shorter numbers.

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
from functools import lru_cache

n_str = input().strip()
k = int(input().strip())
digits = [int(c) for c in n_str]
L = len(digits)

@lru_cache(None)
def dp(pos: int, tight: int, cur_sum: int, lead: int) -> int:
    if pos == L:
        return 1 if cur_sum == k and lead == 0 else 0
    ans = 0
    up = digits[pos] if tight else 9
    for d in range(up + 1):
        new_tight = 1 if tight and d == digits[pos] else 0
        if lead and d == 0:
            new_lead = 1
            new_sum = cur_sum
        else:
            new_lead = 0
            new_sum = cur_sum + d
        if new_sum > k:
            continue
        ans += dp(pos + 1, new_tight, new_sum, new_lead)
    return ans

print(dp(0, 1, 0, 1))
```

### Example Problem 2: Exactly K Digits Equal to 1
**Problem Description**: Given `N` (1 ≤ N < 10^100) and `K` (1 ≤ K ≤ 100), count integers X (1 ≤ X ≤ N) with exactly K digits equal to 1 (other digits 0 or 2-9).  
**Input**: First line: N (as string), second line: K.  
**Output**: Single integer (count).

**Solution Explanation**:  
Digit DP with states `dp[pos][tight][cnt_one][lead]`.  
- `cnt_one`: count of '1's placed (only after leading zeros).  
- Transition: If `lead and d==0`: `new_cnt = cnt_one`, `new_lead=1`. Else: `new_lead=0`, `new_cnt = cnt_one + (1 if d==1 else 0)`.  
- Prune if `new_cnt > K`.  
- Base: `pos==L` and `cnt_one==K` and `lead==0` → 1.  
- Excludes leading 1's from counting if simulating shorter numbers.

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
from functools import lru_cache

n_str = input().strip()
k = int(input().strip())
digits = [int(c) for c in n_str]
L = len(digits)

@lru_cache(None)
def dp(pos: int, tight: int, cnt_one: int, lead: int) -> int:
    if pos == L:
        return 1 if cnt_one == k and lead == 0 else 0
    ans = 0
    up = digits[pos] if tight else 9
    for d in range(up + 1):
        new_tight = 1 if tight and d == digits[pos] else 0
        if lead and d == 0:
            new_lead = 1
            new_cnt = cnt_one
        else:
            new_lead = 0
            new_cnt = cnt_one + (1 if d == 1 else 0)
        if new_cnt > k:
            continue
        ans += dp(pos + 1, new_tight, new_cnt, new_lead)
    return ans

print(dp(0, 1, 0, 1))
```

### Example Problem 3: Numbers Divisible by 3 (Digit Sum % 3 == 0)
**Problem Description**: Given `N` (1 ≤ N < 10^100), count integers X (1 ≤ X ≤ N) where X % 3 == 0 (equivalent to digit sum % 3 == 0).  
**Input**: Single line: N (as string).  
**Output**: Single integer (count). (K=0 implicitly for mod 0).

**Solution Explanation**:  
Digit DP with states `dp[pos][tight][mod_so_far][lead]` (mod_so_far = current digit sum % 3).  
- Transition: If `lead and d==0`: `new_mod = mod_so_far`, `new_lead=1`. Else: `new_mod = (mod_so_far + d) % 3`, `new_lead=0`.  
- Base: `pos==L` and `mod_so_far==0` and `lead==0` → 1.  
- Efficient (mod only 0-2).

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
from functools import lru_cache

n_str = input().strip()
digits = [int(c) for c in n_str]
L = len(digits)

@lru_cache(None)
def dp(pos: int, tight: int, mod_so_far: int, lead: int) -> int:
    if pos == L:
        return 1 if mod_so_far == 0 and lead == 0 else 0
    ans = 0
    up = digits[pos] if tight else 9
    for d in range(up + 1):
        new_tight = 1 if tight and d == digits[pos] else 0
        if lead and d == 0:
            new_lead = 1
            new_mod = mod_so_far
        else:
            new_lead = 0
            new_mod = (mod_so_far + d) % 3
        ans += dp(pos + 1, new_tight, new_mod, new_lead)
    return ans

print(dp(0, 1, 0, 1))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**:  
  Convert `n` to a list of digits. Use memoized recursive Digit DP with states `(pos, tight, cnt, lead)`:  
  - `pos`: Current position (0 to L-1).  
  - `tight`: 1 if prefix matches `n`, else 0.  
  - `cnt`: Non-zero digits so far (0 to k).  
  - `lead`: 1 if only leading zeros placed.  
  - Transition: For each digit `d` (0 to upper limit), update `new_cnt = cnt + 1` if `d != 0` and number has started (not `lead or d!=0`), else `cnt`. Update `lead` accordingly. Prune if `new_cnt > k`.  
  - Base: At `pos == L`, return 1 if `cnt == k` (implies `lead == 0` since k ≥ 1).  
  - Call `dp(0, 1, 0, 1)`. Handles all cases including shorter numbers via `lead`. Time: O(10^6), Python handles large counts.

```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
from functools import lru_cache

n_str = input().strip()
k = int(input().strip())
digits = [int(c) for c in n_str]
L = len(digits)

@lru_cache(None)
def dp(pos: int, tight: int, cnt: int, lead: int) -> int:
    if pos == L:
        return 1 if cnt == k else 0
    ans = 0
    up = digits[pos] if tight else 9
    for d in range(up + 1):
        new_tight = 1 if tight and d == digits[pos] else 0
        if lead and d == 0:
            new_lead = 1
            new_cnt = cnt
        else:
            new_lead = 0
            new_cnt = cnt + (1 if d != 0 else 0)
        if new_cnt > k:
            continue
        ans += dp(pos + 1, new_tight, new_cnt, new_lead)
    return ans

print(dp(0, 1, 0, 1))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:

1. **Incremental Base Conversion and Divisibility Check**: Convert the digit string prefix-by-prefix to its decimal equivalent incrementally (`current = current * b + digit_value`), and check divisibility by the prefix length `k` at each step. This avoids redundant computations and handles large numbers efficiently using Python's arbitrary-precision integers.

2. **Digit Value Mapping for Arbitrary Bases**: Map characters `[0-9A-Za-z]` to integer values (0-9, 10-35, 36-61) to validate digits against base `b` and perform conversion.

3. **Tree Search / Digit-by-Digit Construction (for generation)**: To find the nth polydivisible number, perform a depth-first search (DFS) on possible digit sequences, pruning branches where the current prefix fails the divisibility check. Count valid leaves/subtrees to locate the nth valid number efficiently without enumerating all smaller numbers.

These ensure O(length) time for `is_polydivisible` and efficient generation for small `n` (as polydivisible numbers are sparse).

## Tutorial
### Checking Polydivisibility (`is_polydivisible(n: str, b: int)`)

1. **Digit Mapping**: Create a function `digit_to_int(c)`:
   - `0-9`: `ord(c) - ord('0')`
   - `A-Z`: `10 + ord(c) - ord('A')`
   - `a-z`: `36 + ord(c) - ord('a')`
   - Validate `0 <= val < b`.

2. **Incremental Computation**:
   - Initialize `current = 0`.
   - For each position `k = 1` to `len(n)`:
     - Get `d = digit_to_int(n[k-1])`.
     - Update `current = current * b + d`.
     - If `current % k != 0`, return `False`.
   - Return `True`.

This works because the prefix of length `k` in base `b` has decimal value exactly `current` at step `k`. Python handles large `current` seamlessly.

**Edge Cases**:
- `"0"`: `0 % 1 == 0` → `True`.
- Leading zeros (e.g., `"01"`, base 10): `0%1==0`, but `0*10+1=1 %2 !=0` → `False`.
- Invalid digit (e.g., `'A'` in base 10): `10 >= 10` → `False`.

### Generating the nth Polydivisible (`get_polydivisible(nth: int, b: int)`)

Use DFS to build digit strings:
- Track current string/prefix value, current length.
- At each step, try digits `0` to `b-1` (no leading zero after first digit).
- Prune if current prefix not divisible by its length.
- Use counting: recurse to compute subtree size (number of valid extensions), skip if > remaining needed.

**Chars for Output**: `'0123456789ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZabcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz'[:b]`

For small `nth` (e.g., <100), brute-force `i=0,1,2,...` → convert `i` to base-`b` string → check → collect is viable.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Count Polydivisible Numbers <= N (Base 10)
**Problem Description**: Given multiple test cases (T <= 100), each with N (1 <= N <= 10^18, given as string), count polydivisible numbers from 1 to N in base 10 (no leading zeros, ignore 0).

**Solution Explanation**: Polydivisible numbers up to 18 digits are few (~2000 total). Precompute all using DFS: start with digits 1-9, extend with 0-9 if new prefix % new_length == 0. Store their decimal values in a sorted list. For each query, use binary search (`bisect_right`) to count how many <= int(N).

Time: Precompute O(10^18 exploration pruned to ~10^4), queries O(log 2000).

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

T = int(data[0])
queries = []
index = 1
for _ in range(T):
    N = data[index]
    queries.append(N)
    index += 1

# Precompute all polydiv <=10^18 -1
LCM_NOT_NEEDED = True  # Use big int
polydivs = []

def dfs(curr_val, curr_len):
    if curr_len > 18:
        return
    if curr_len > 0:
        polydivs.append(curr_val)
    for d in range(10):
        new_val = curr_val * 10 + d
        new_len = curr_len + 1
        if new_val % new_len == 0:
            dfs(new_val, new_len)

for first in range(1, 10):
    dfs(first, 1)

polydivs.sort()

import bisect

for N_str in queries:
    N = int(N_str)
    # Count strictly <= N (since 1 to N)
    cnt = bisect.bisect_right(polydivs, N)
    print(cnt)
```

### Example Problem 3: Count Polydivisible with Exactly D Digits (Arbitrary Base)
**Problem Description**: Given D (1 <= D <= 20), B (2 <= B <= 62), count polydivisible numbers with **exactly** D digits in base B (no leading zeros).

**Solution Explanation**: DFS count: recurse on position (0 to D-1), track current decimal value. Start first digit 1 to B-1. At each step, update value, check % (pos+1)==0. Prune early. Use memo? Not needed as D=20, B=62 worst 62^20 pruned heavily by div checks.

Time: Exponential pruned by divisibility (very fast in practice).

```python
def digit_chars(b):
    chars = '0123456789ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZabcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz'
    return chars[:b]

def count_exact_d(d, b):
    def dfs(pos, curr_val, curr_len):
        if pos == d:
            return 1
        cnt = 0
        start_d = 1 if pos == 0 else 0
        for dig in range(start_d, b):
            new_val = curr_val * b + dig
            new_len = curr_len + 1
            if new_val % new_len == 0:
                cnt += dfs(pos + 1, new_val, new_len)
        return cnt

    return dfs(0, 0, 0)

D = int(input())
B = int(input())
print(count_exact_d(D, B))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
The program reads two lines from stdin: first the digit string `n`, second the base `b`. It implements `is_polydivisible(n, b)` using incremental base conversion: maps each char to digit value, builds `current` decimal prefix value, checks `current % k == 0` for k=1 to len(n). Handles bases up to 62 with case-sensitive digit mapping. Outputs `True` or `False`. Efficient O(len(n)), works for long strings (Python bigints).

Assumes valid input as per problem (non-empty, valid digits).

```python
import sys

def digit_to_int(c):
    if c.isdigit():
        return ord(c) - ord('0')
    elif c.isupper():
        return 10 + ord(c) - ord('A')
    elif c.islower():
        return 36 + ord(c) - ord('a')
    else:
        return -1  # Invalid

def is_polydivisible(n, b):
    if not n:
        return False
    current = 0
    for k in range(1, len(n) + 1):
        d = digit_to_int(n[k-1])
        if d < 0 or d >= b:
            return False
        current = current * b + d
        if current % k != 0:
            return False
    return True

# Read from stdin
input_data = sys.stdin.read().strip().splitlines()
n = input_data[0].strip()
b = int(input_data[1].strip())

print(is_polydivisible(n, b))
```
