## Algorithms
- **Union-Find (Disjoint Set Union - DSU)**: Used to efficiently identify connected components among employees. Each language acts as a "union" operation for all employees who know it, merging them into the same component since they can communicate directly.
- **Component Classification**: After DSU, classify components into:
  - "Non-empty" components (those with at least one language known by any member).
  - "Empty" components (isolated employees knowing no languages; always singletons).
- **Minimum Cost Calculation**: 
  - Non-empty components can be connected pairwise at cost 1 (teach a language from one to an employee in the other).
  - Empty employees can be connected to a non-empty component at cost 1 each.
  - If no non-empty components exist, teach one common language to all empty employees at cost equal to the number of empties.
  - Formula: `empties + max(0, num_nonempty - 1)`.

This leverages the fact that post-DSU, components have disjoint language sets, making cost-1 connections possible between non-empties or non-empty to empty.

## Tutorial
### Union-Find (DSU) Basics
Union-Find is a data structure for tracking partitions of a set into disjoint subsets (components). It supports:
- **Find**: Determine the representative (root) of a node's component.
- **Union**: Merge two components.
With path compression and union-by-rank, it's nearly O(1) per operation (amortized O(α(n)) where α is inverse Ackermann).

**Implementation**:
```python
parent = list(range(n))
rank = [0] * n

def find(x):
    if parent[x] != x:
        parent[x] = find(parent[x])
    return parent[x]

def union(x, y):
    px, py = find(x), find(y)
    if px != py:
        if rank[px] < rank[py]:
            parent[px] = py
        elif rank[px] > rank[py]:
            parent[py] = px
        else:
            parent[py] = px
            rank[px] += 1
```

### Modeling the Problem
1. **Graph Interpretation**: Employees are nodes; an edge exists if they share a language (direct communication). Indirect paths allow translation.
2. **Efficient Union**: Instead of O(n^2) edges, for each language, union all employees knowing it (clique).
3. **Post-Processing**:
   - Track employees knowing ≥1 language (`knows_something`).
   - Count `empties` (k_i = 0).
   - Collect unique roots of `knows_something` employees → `num_nonempty`.
4. **Cost Insight**:
   - Non-empty components form a "backbone"; connect like MST with uniform cost 1: `num_nonempty - 1`.
   - Attach each empty: +1 each.
   - All-empty case: Use one hub language for all: cost = `empties` (no backbone needed).
   - Unified: `empties + max(0, num_nonempty - 1)`.
5. **Why Optimal?** No shared languages between components (by DSU). Cost-2 links (new language between two empties) are suboptimal vs. hub.

**Time Complexity**: O(n + m + n * α(n)) ≈ O(n + m).

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Number of Connected Components
**Problem**: You are given n nodes (numbered 0 to n-1) and m undirected edges. Find the number of connected components in the graph.

**Input**:
```
First line: n m (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5, 0 ≤ m ≤ 10^5)
Next m lines: u v (0 ≤ u, v < n, u ≠ v)
```
**Output**: Single integer — number of connected components.

**Solution Explanation**: Use DSU to union endpoints of each edge. Count distinct `find(i)` for i in 0 to n-1. Path compression + union-by-rank ensures efficiency.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index]); index += 1
m = int(data[index]); index += 1
parent = list(range(n))
rank = [0] * n
def find(x):
    if parent[x] != x:
        parent[x] = find(parent[x])
    return parent[x]
def union(x, y):
    px, py = find(x), find(y)
    if px != py:
        if rank[px] < rank[py]:
            parent[px] = py
        elif rank[px] > rank[py]:
            parent[py] = px
        else:
            parent[py] = px
            rank[px] += 1
for _ in range(m):
    u = int(data[index]); index += 1
    v = int(data[index]); index += 1
    union(u, v)
components = len(set(find(i) for i in range(n)))
print(components)
```

### Example Problem 2: Maximum Component Size After Unions
**Problem**: n students (0 to n-1), m clubs. Each club has a list of members. Students in the same club are connected. Find the size of the largest connected component.

**Input**:
```
First line: n m (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5, 1 ≤ m ≤ 10^5)
Next m lines: First integer k (1 ≤ k ≤ n), then k student IDs (0-based, distinct)
```
**Output**: Single integer — size of largest component.

**Solution Explanation**: DSU on students. For each club, union all members to the first. Track component sizes via `size[root] += size[other]`. Max over sizes of roots.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
sys.setrecursionlimit(10**6)
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index]); index += 1
m = int(data[index]); index += 1
parent = list(range(n))
rank = [0] * n
size = [1] * n
def find(x):
    if parent[x] != x:
        parent[x] = find(parent[x])
    return parent[x]
def union(x, y):
    px, py = find(x), find(y)
    if px != py:
        if rank[px] < rank[py]:
            parent[px] = py
            size[py] += size[px]
        elif rank[px] > rank[py]:
            parent[py] = px
            size[px] += size[py]
        else:
            parent[py] = px
            size[px] += size[py]
            rank[px] += 1
for _ in range(m):
    k = int(data[index]); index += 1
    club = [int(data[index + j]) for j in range(k)]
    index += k
    if k >= 2:
        first = club[0]
        for other in club[1:]:
            union(first, other)
max_size = max(size[find(i)] for i in range(n))
print(max_size)
```

### Example Problem 3: Min Cost to Connect Components (Simplified)
**Problem**: n cities (0 to n-1), some in "hubs" (first k cities have airports). Cost to build road between hubs: 1, hub to non-hub: 1, non-hub to non-hub: 2. Find min cost to connect all (indirectly).

**Input**:
```
First line: n k (2 ≤ n ≤ 100, 0 ≤ k ≤ n)
```
Cities 0 to k-1 are hubs.

**Output**: Min cost.

**Solution Explanation**: Similar to original. `num_hubs_comp = 1` (assume hubs pre-connected, simplify). But general: hubs are "non-empty". Non-hubs: empties. Cost = `max(0, 1 - 1) + (n - k)` if k>=1 else n. Wait, adapt: here hubs are one comp if k>=1. But to demo: count hub components (here 1), empties=n-k. Cost=(1-1 if k>=1 else 0) + (n-k), special all non-hub: n.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
n, k = map(int, input().split())
num_nonempty = 1 if k >= 1 else 0
empties = n - k
if num_nonempty == 0:
    ans = empties
else:
    ans = (num_nonempty - 1) + empties
print(ans)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- Read n, m.
- For each of n employees: read k and languages, count empties (k==0), mark knows_something[i]=True if k>0, collect lang_emps[l].append(i).
- Initialize DSU on n employees.
- For each language 1 to m: union all employees in lang_emps[l].
- Collect unique find(i) for all i where knows_something[i]: this gives num_nonempty.
- Compute cost: empties + max(0, num_nonempty - 1).
- This is optimal as explained: backbone of non-empties costs num_nonempty-1, each empty attaches for 1; all-empty uses one shared language.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index]); index += 1
m = int(data[index]); index += 1

lang_emps = [[] for _ in range(m + 1)]
knows_something = [False] * n
empties = 0

for i in range(n):
    k = int(data[index]); index += 1
    if k == 0:
        empties += 1
    else:
        knows_something[i] = True
    for _ in range(k):
        l = int(data[index]); index += 1
        lang_emps[l].append(i)

parent = list(range(n))
rank = [0] * n

def find(x):
    if parent[x] != x:
        parent[x] = find(parent[x])
    return parent[x]

def union(x, y):
    px = find(x)
    py = find(y)
    if px != py:
        if rank[px] < rank[py]:
            parent[px] = py
        elif rank[px] > rank[py]:
            parent[py] = px
        else:
            parent[py] = px
            rank[px] += 1

for l in range(1, m + 1):
    emps = lang_emps[l]
    if len(emps) >= 2:
        first = emps[0]
        for j in range(1, len(emps)):
            union(first, emps[j])

roots = set()
for i in range(n):
    if knows_something[i]:
        roots.add(find(i))

num_nonempty = len(roots)
if num_nonempty == 0:
    ans = empties
else:
    ans = (num_nonempty - 1) + empties
print(ans)
```

## Algorithms
The core concept is **state toggling with parity checking**. Each flight toggles Jinotega's location between "home" and "away" (since every flight connects home to an away airport or vice versa). Starting at home (even parity, 0 flights), the final location depends solely on the parity of the total number of flights `n`:
- Even `n`: back at home.
- Odd `n`: at a contest.
No need to parse individual flights or track airports per destination, as the problem guarantees consistency (valid ordering exists, no invalid states). This is efficient (O(n) time to read input, O(1) decision).

Alternative approach (equivalent here): Count outbound (`home -> away`) and inbound (`away -> home`) flights per away airport. If all counts balance (`out == in` for every away), at home; else (exactly one away with `out == in + 1`), at contest. Parity is simpler as total flights = sum of all out + in, and balance implies even total.

## Tutorial
In competitive programming, **state toggling problems** model systems flipping between discrete states (e.g., home/away, on/off, 0/1) with each action. Key insight:
1. Identify the state transition: Here, every action (flight) flips the state.
2. Note initial state (home = even parity).
3. Final state = initial XOR (n % 2). Parity suffices because transitions are uniform.
4. Guarantees (valid prefixes, no self-loops) ensure no exceptions.

**Steps to solve**:
- Read `n` and ignore details if transitions are identical.
- Compute `n % 2`.
- Map parity to output.

**Extensions**:
- **Multi-state**: Use modular arithmetic (e.g., position % k).
- **Per-type toggles**: Count per category (e.g., airports), check balances.
- **With constraints**: Simulate stack/queue if order matters (O(n log n) sort or O(n) greedy).
- **Graphs**: Eulerian paths (degrees even except possibly two).
- Pitfalls: Read all input (don't early-exit); handle guarantees.

Time: O(n), Space: O(1). Scales to n=1e5+.

**Practice tips**: Look for "toggle", "flip", "parity", "Eulerian star graph". Verify with samples; prove invariance (each pair out-in preserves parity).

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Light Switch
**Problem Description**: There is a single light switch, initially off (0). Given `n` toggle operations (1 ≤ n ≤ 100), determine if the light is on (1) or off (0) at the end. Input: First line `n`, followed by `n` lines each containing "toggle".

**Solution Explanation**: Each "toggle" flips the state (0↔1). Starting from 0, final state = `n % 2` (odd: 1, even: 0). Ignore operation strings (all identical). O(n) read, O(1) compute.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().splitlines()
n = int(data[0])
print(1 if n % 2 == 1 else 0)
```

### Example Problem 2: Two Cities Travel
**Problem Description**: Jinotega travels between home "H" and work "W". Starts at "H". Given `n` flights (1 ≤ n ≤ 100), each "H->W" or "W->H" (guaranteed consistent). Output "H" if at home, "W" if at work. Input: First line `n`, second line home ("H"), then `n` lines of flights.

**Solution Explanation**: Every flight toggles between H/W. Parity of `n` decides: even → H, odd → W. No need to parse flights (guaranteed valid). Simulates single-type toggle.

```python
n = int(input())
home = input().strip()
for _ in range(n):
    input()
print("H" if n % 2 == 0 else "W")
```

### Example Problem 3: Multi-Balancer (Multi-Type Parentheses Counts)
**Problem Description**: Check if multiple bracket types (A,B,C) are balanced given counts. Input: First line `n` (total brackets, 1 ≤ n ≤ 100), second line `k` (types 1≤k≤10), then `n` lines each "openX" or "closeX" (X=A,B,C...). Guaranteed no prefix invalid. Output "balanced" if all types have equal open/close, else "unbalanced".

**Solution Explanation**: For multi-types, count open/close per type (dict/counter). Balanced if all `open[type] == close[type]`. Equivalent to all even per-type (total even). Uses hashmap O(n), checks O(k). (Parity check: sum opens even iff balanced.)

```python
from collections import defaultdict
n = int(input())
k = int(input())
open_count = defaultdict(int)
close_count = defaultdict(int)
for _ in range(n):
    s = input().strip()
    if s.startswith("open"):
        typ = s[4:]
        open_count[typ] += 1
    else:
        typ = s[5:]
        close_count[typ] += 1
balanced = all(open_count[t] == close_count[t] for t in set(list(open_count) + list(close_count)))
print("balanced" if balanced else "unbalanced")
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Each flight toggles location (home ↔ away) because every flight has exactly one home endpoint. Start at home (even flights: 0). After `n` toggles: home if `n` even, contest if odd. Guaranteed consistency means no invalid cases (e.g., can't end away on even `n`). Read `n`, home, and `n` flights (ignore contents), compute parity. O(n) time, O(1) extra space. Matches samples: 4 even → "home", 3 odd → "contest".

```python
n = int(input())
home = input().strip()
for _ in range(n):
    input()
if n % 2 == 0:
    print("home")
else:
    print("contest")
```
completed 105/150, Solved: True, number of success = 23/105, acc = 21.9
## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Timestamp-based tracking of updates**: Assign each event a timestamp (index from 0 to q-1). For each citizen, record the timestamp and value of their **last receipt** (point update). If no receipt, use initial value and timestamp -1.
- **Suffix maximum computation**: Precompute a suffix maximum array for payout values. `suffix_max[t]` is the maximum payout `x` from event `t` to the end (initialize end as 0). This allows O(1) queries for the effective maximum payout after any timestamp.
- **Efficient final state computation**: For each citizen, take `max(last_value, suffix_max[last_timestamp + 1])` (or `suffix_max[0]` if no receipt). This simulates all operations in O(n + q) time, avoiding O(nq) naive simulation.
- **Key insight**: Payouts are idempotent global `max(current, x)` operations. Only payouts **after** the last receipt affect the final value, as receipts override prior history.

This approach generalizes to other global updates (e.g., add, min) using suffix sums/mins.

## Tutorial
### Solving Problems with Global Updates and Point Updates Efficiently
In competitive programming, problems with **global updates** (affecting all elements) and **point updates/queries** (affecting/querying one element) often arise, especially with n, q ≤ 2⋅10⁵. Naive simulation is O(nq), too slow. The key is **offline processing** using **timestamps** and **suffix/prefix aggregates**.

#### Step 1: Model the Operations
- **Point update** (receipt): Sets a[i] = x at time t, overriding history.
- **Global update** (payout): Applies to current state, e.g., a[i] = max(a[i], x) for all i.
- Insight: Final a[i] depends only on the **last point update** for i and **global updates after it**.

#### Step 2: Timestamp Everything
- Number events 0 to q-1.
- For each i: Track `last_t[i]` (last point update time, -1 if none) and `last_v[i]` (value at that time; initial a[i] if none).
- Process events forward to capture last point updates.

#### Step 3: Precompute Global Effects (Suffix Arrays)
For **max** global updates:
- Build `suffix_max[q+1]`, where `suffix_max[q] = 0` (neutral: max(v, 0) = v since values ≥ 0).
- Backward pass:
  ```
  for t = q-1 downto 0:
      if event[t] is global max x:
          suffix_max[t] = max(x, suffix_max[t+1])
      else:
          suffix_max[t] = suffix_max[t+1]
  ```
- `suffix_max[t]` = max global x from t to end.

Variants:
- **Global add y**: Use `suffix_sum[t] = y + suffix_sum[t+1]` (init 0).
- **Global min x**: Use `suffix_min[t] = min(x, suffix_min[t+1])` (init 10¹⁸).
- Time: O(q).

#### Step 4: Compute Finals
```
for i in 0..n-1:
    t = last_t[i]
    after = suffix_max[0] if t == -1 else suffix_max[t+1]
    final[i] = max(last_v[i], after)
```
- Time: O(n).

#### Why It Works
- Receipts set exact value; prior globals are baked in.
- Only post-receipt globals matter.
- Neutral init (0 for max/add, INF for min) handles "no updates."

#### Generalizations & Tips
- **Multiple global types**: Track multiple suffix arrays.
- **Queries intermixed**: Use segment tree/fenwick for dynamic globals.
- **Reversing operations**: Sometimes process backward for assigns.
- Python pitfalls: Use `sys.stdin.read()` for fast input (2e5 ok).
- Extensions: Range updates → segment tree with lazy propagation.

This technique appears in ~10% of array update problems (e.g., CF Div2 C/D).

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Global Increments and Point Sets
**Problem Description**: There are n elements with initial values a₁..aₙ. There are q events: `1 i x` sets aᵢ = x; `2 x` adds x to **all** elements. Output the final array after all events. (1 ≤ n,q ≤ 2e5, 0 ≤ values ≤ 1e9)

**Solution Explanation**: Track last point set time t and value v for each i. Precompute `suffix_sum[t]` = sum of all adds from event t to end (init suffix_sum[q]=0). Backward pass: if add y, suffix_sum[t] = y + suffix_sum[t+1]; else copy next. Final[i] = last_v[i] + (suffix_sum[0] if t=-1 else suffix_sum[t+1]). O(n+q).

**Sample Input**:
```
2
1 2
3
1 1 10
2 3
1 2 5
```
**Sample Output**:
```
13 5
```

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

idx = 0
n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
a = [int(data[idx + i]) for i in range(n)]; idx += n
q = int(data[idx]); idx += 1

last_t = [-1] * n
last_v = a[:]
is_add = [False] * q
add_x = [0] * q

for t in range(q):
    typ = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
    if typ == 1:
        p = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        last_t[p - 1] = t
        last_v[p - 1] = x
    else:
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        is_add[t] = True
        add_x[t] = x

suffix_sum = [0] * (q + 1)
for t in range(q - 1, -1, -1):
    if is_add[t]:
        suffix_sum[t] = add_x[t] + suffix_sum[t + 1]
    else:
        suffix_sum[t] = suffix_sum[t + 1]

final = []
for i in range(n):
    t = last_t[i]
    after = suffix_sum[0] if t == -1 else suffix_sum[t + 1]
    final.append(last_v[i] + after)

print(' '.join(map(str, final)))
```

### Example Problem 2: Global Minimum Caps and Point Sets
**Problem Description**: n elements a₁..aₙ initial. q events: `1 i x` sets aᵢ = x; `2 x` sets **every** aⱼ = min(aⱼ, x). Output final array. (1 ≤ n,q ≤ 2e5, 0 ≤ values ≤ 1e9)

**Solution Explanation**: Track last point set t and v. Precompute `suffix_min[t]` = minimum cap x from t to end (init suffix_min[q] = 10**18). Backward: if cap x, suffix_min[t] = min(x, suffix_min[t+1]); else copy. Final[i] = min(last_v[i], (suffix_min[0] if t=-1 else suffix_min[t+1])). O(n+q).

**Sample Input**:
```
3
5 10 3
3
2 4
1 2 6
2 7
```
**Sample Output**:
```
4 4 3
```
(Init 5,10,3 → min4: 4,4,3 → set2=6: 4,6,3 → min7: 4,6,3)

```python
import sys

INF = 10**18 + 5
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

idx = 0
n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
a = [int(data[idx + i]) for i in range(n)]; idx += n
q = int(data[idx]); idx += 1

last_t = [-1] * n
last_v = a[:]
is_cap = [False] * q
cap_x = [0] * q

for t in range(q):
    typ = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
    if typ == 1:
        p = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        last_t[p - 1] = t
        last_v[p - 1] = x
    else:
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        is_cap[t] = True
        cap_x[t] = x

suffix_min = [INF] * (q + 1)
suffix_min[q] = INF
for t in range(q - 1, -1, -1):
    if is_cap[t]:
        suffix_min[t] = min(cap_x[t], suffix_min[t + 1])
    else:
        suffix_min[t] = suffix_min[t + 1]

final = []
for i in range(n):
    t = last_t[i]
    after = suffix_min[0] if t == -1 else suffix_min[t + 1]
    final.append(min(last_v[i], after))

print(' '.join(map(str, final)))
```

### Example Problem 3: Global Assignments and Point Sets (Last Wins)
**Problem Description**: n elements a₁..aₙ. q events: `1 i x` sets aᵢ = x; `2 x` sets **all** aⱼ = x. Output final. (1 ≤ n,q ≤ 2e5, 0 ≤ x ≤ 1e9)

**Solution Explanation**: Track global last assign time `g_t=-1`, `g_v=0`. Per i: last_t[i], last_v[i]. Process forward, updating global on type 2. For final[i]: if last_t[i] > g_t: last_v[i] else g_v. (Globals override until point set.) O(n+q).

**Sample Input**:
```
2
1 2
3
2 10
1 1 5
2 3
```
**Sample Output**:
```
3 3
```
(Init1,2 → all10 → set1=5:5,10 → all3:3,3)

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

idx = 0
n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
a = [int(data[idx + i]) for i in range(n)]; idx += n
q = int(data[idx]); idx += 1

last_t = [-1] * n
last_v = a[:]
g_t = -1
g_v = 0

for t in range(q):
    typ = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
    if typ == 1:
        p = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        last_t[p - 1] = t
        last_v[p - 1] = x
    else:
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        g_t = t
        g_v = x

final = []
for i in range(n):
    if last_t[i] > g_t:
        final.append(last_v[i])
    else:
        final.append(g_v)

print(' '.join(map(str, final)))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- Read all input at once for speed.
- Initialize `last_t` (-1) and `last_v` (initial a) for each citizen.
- Process q events forward: On receipt `1 p x`, update `last_t[p-1] = t`, `last_v[p-1] = x`. Record payouts in `is_payout` and `payout_x`.
- Compute `suffix_max[q+1]` backward: init `suffix_max[q] = 0`. If payout at t, `max(payout_x[t], suffix_max[t+1])`; else copy.
- For each i: `after = suffix_max[0]` if `last_t[i]==-1` else `suffix_max[last_t[i]+1]`. Final = `max(last_v[i], after)`.
- Output space-separated finals. Correctness verified on samples; handles edges (no events, x=0, no receipts). Time O(n+q), space O(n+q).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

idx = 0
n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
a = [int(data[idx + i]) for i in range(n)]; idx += n
q = int(data[idx]); idx += 1

last_t = [-1] * n
last_v = a[:]
is_payout = [False] * q
payout_x = [0] * q

for t in range(q):
    typ = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
    if typ == 1:
        p = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        last_t[p - 1] = t
        last_v[p - 1] = x
    else:
        x = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        is_payout[t] = True
        payout_x[t] = x

suffix_max = [0] * (q + 1)
for t in range(q - 1, -1, -1):
    if is_payout[t]:
        suffix_max[t] = max(payout_x[t], suffix_max[t + 1])
    else:
        suffix_max[t] = suffix_max[t + 1]

final = []
for i in range(n):
    t = last_t[i]
    after = suffix_max[0] if t == -1 else suffix_max[t + 1]
    final.append(max(last_v[i], after))

print(' '.join(map(str, final)))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts involve **position classification** and **combinatorial pair counting**:
- Classify each position into one of four categories based on the pair `(a[i], b[i])`: (0,0), (0,1), (1,0), (1,1). Let `cnt00`, `cnt01`, `cnt10`, `cnt11` be their counts.
- Total swaps that exchange a 0 and a 1 in `a`: `(cnt00 + cnt01) * (cnt10 + cnt11)`.
- Swaps that do **not** change the bitwise OR `c = a OR b` are those between (0,1) and (1,1) positions: `cnt01 * cnt11`.
- Answer: total differing swaps minus non-changing swaps, computed in **O(n)** time via a single pass.
This leverages the fact that only swaps between differing bits can affect `a`, and OR changes unless both positions have `b[i] = 1`.

No advanced algorithms (e.g., no segment trees or FFT); pure counting suffices due to the pairwise independence of OR bits.

## Tutorial
### Core Idea: When Does a Swap Change the OR?
- Swapping identical bits (both 0 or both 1) leaves `a` unchanged → OR unchanged.
- Only swaps between a 0 (at position `i`) and a 1 (at `j`) matter. After swap:
  - Position `i` (0 → 1): OR changes from `b[i]` to `1` **iff** `b[i] == 0`.
  - Position `j` (1 → 0): OR changes from `1` to `b[j]` **iff** `b[j] == 0`.
- OR changes **unless** both positions have `b[i] == 1` and `b[j] == 1` (no flip at either).
- Thus, count all 0-1 pairs, subtract "bad" pairs where both have `b == 1`.

### Step-by-Step Solution Approach
1. Iterate over the `n` positions, count:
   - `ztot`: total positions with `a[i] == '0'`.
   - `z1`: positions with `a[i] == '0'` and `b[i] == '1'`.
   - `o1`: positions with `a[i] == '1'` and `b[i] == '1'`.
2. Compute `otot = n - ztot`.
3. Total 0-1 swaps: `ztot * otot`.
4. Bad swaps (no OR change): `z1 * o1`.
5. Answer: `ztot * otot - z1 * o1`.

### Time/Space Complexity
- Time: **O(n)** (single pass).
- Space: **O(1)** (counters only; strings are O(n) input).
- Handles `n ≤ 10^5` efficiently; 64-bit integers suffice (`ztot * otot ≤ (10^5)^2 / 4 ≈ 2.5×10^9 < 2^63`).

### Edge Cases
- All `a` bits same (`ztot=0` or `otot=0`): Answer 0.
- All `b=1`: All 0-1 swaps are bad → Answer 0.
- No bad pairs (`z1=0` or `o1=0`): Answer = `ztot * otot`.

### Proof of Correctness
- Swaps only affect OR at `i` and `j`.
- Exhaustive case analysis (as in thinking trace) confirms only bad pairs preserve OR everywhere.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: "Binary Pair Count" (Inspired by AtCoder ABC 128B - but simplified pair counting)
**Problem Description**:  
Given a binary string `s` of length `n` (2 ≤ n ≤ 10^5), count the number of unordered pairs `{i, j}` (i < j) such that `s[i] != s[j]`. Output the count.

**Solution Explanation**:  
Classify positions: `cnt0` = number of '0's, `cnt1` = n - cnt0.  
Every pair with one '0' and one '1' satisfies the condition.  
Answer: `cnt0 * cnt1`. O(n) time via counting.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
s = data[1]
assert len(s) == n
cnt0 = s.count('0')
cnt1 = n - cnt0
print(cnt0 * cnt1)
```

### Example Problem 2: "XOR Swap Effects" (Inspired by Codeforces 1354D - bit manipulation with pairs)
**Problem Description**:  
Given two binary strings `a` and `b` of length `n` (2 ≤ n ≤ 10^5), count the number of swaps of two differing bits in `a` (i < j, a[i] != a[j]) such that the bitwise XOR of new `a` and `b` changes. (XOR changes unless both positions have b[k] == 0.)

**Solution Explanation**:  
Similar classification: `z0` = a='0' b='0', `z1` = a='0' b='1', etc.  
XOR at i (0→1): changes iff b[i]==1 (0 XOR 1 → 1 XOR 1 = 0? Wait, detailed: orig XOR_i = 0 XOR b_i = b_i, new=1 XOR b_i = 1 - b_i → changes always unless b_i=0.5 impossible. Actually for XOR, it always changes at least one bit.  
Wait, correction for tutorial: Bad swaps (no XOR change) are those where b[i]==b[j]==0 (both flips preserve XOR).

```python
    if a[i] == '0':
        ztot += 1
        if b[i] == '0': z0 += 1
otot = n - ztot
o0 = 0  # Would count a=1 b=0, but for XOR bad is z0 * o0
# Full count for o0:
o0 = 0
for i in range(n):
    if a[i] == '1' and b[i] == '0': o0 += 1

```

### Example Problem 3: "Good Pairs in Binary Grid" (Inspired by LeetCode 2956 / CF pair counting)

No ordering issue: number of such pairs is exactly z1 * o0, regardless of positions (since i<j not position-dependent in count).  
O(n) counting.

## Python3 code to solve the original problem:
### Explanation of the solution:
- Read `n`, string `a`, string `b`.
- Count `ztot` (positions with `a[i]=='0'`), `z1` (a='0' b='1'), `o1` (a='1' b='1') in O(n) pass.
- Compute `otot = n - ztot`.

```python
a = data[1]
b = data[2]

assert len(a) == n and len(b) == n

ztot = 0
z1 = 0
o1 = 0

    if a[i] == '0':
        ztot += 1
        if b[i] == '1':
```

## Algorithms
The core concept is **modeling the erosion process as a minimax distance computation in 1D**. Specifically, the number of operations required is the maximum over all towers `i` (1 to `n`) of the minimum over all positions `j` (including virtual ends `j=0` and `j=n+1` with `h[0] = h[n+1] = 0`) of `h[j] + |i - j|`. 

This arises from analyzing the iterative height transformation `new_h[i] = max(0, min(h[i-1], h[i+1], h[i] - 1))`. By induction, the remaining height after `t` operations satisfies `h_t[i] ≤ min_j (h[j] + |i - j| - t)` for all `i, j`. Thus, `h_t[i] ≤ 0` for all `i` iff `t ≥ max_i min_j (h[j] + |i - j|)`. This bound is tight, as verified by samples and edge cases, matching the exact number of operations.

The algorithm computes this in **O(n)** time using **prefix and suffix minima** on transformed arrays:
- Left contribution for `i`: `i + min_{j=0 to i} (h[j] - j)`
- Right contribution for `i`: `-i + min_{j=i to n+1} (h[j] + j)`
- `val[i] = min(left[i], right[i])`
- Answer = `max_i val[i]`

This is a standard technique for 1D **concave/convex envelope queries** or **min-plus convolution with absolute value distance**.

## Tutorial
The block destruction process removes all boundary blocks simultaneously each operation. A block at `(i, k)` (column `i`, height level `k`) is internal (survives) only if `k < h_i` (top neighbor), `h_{i-1} ≥ k` (left), and `h_{i+1} ≥ k` (right), with virtual `h_0 = h_{n+1} = 0`. The remaining heights after one operation are `new_h[i] = max(0, min(h_{i-1}, h_{i+1}, h_i - 1))`.

Simulating directly is O(n * max_h) = O(10^{14}), too slow. Instead, derive that remaining height `h_t[i] ≤ h[i] - t` (self-decay) and `h_t[i] ≤ h_{t-1}[i±1]` (neighbor cap). Chaining `d = |i - j|` neighbor inequalities gives `h_t[i] ≤ h[j] - (t - d) = h[j] + d - t`.

Thus, `h_t[i] ≤ max(0, min_j (h[j] + |i-j| - t))`. All `h_t = 0` iff `t ≥ max_i min_j (h[j] + |i-j|)`. This `t` is the answer.

To compute:
1. Augment `H = [0] + h + [0]` (length `n+2`, indices 0 to `n+1`).
2. Compute `a[k] = H[k] - k`, prefix mins `pm[i] = min_{k=0 to i} a[k]`. Left: `i + pm[i]`.
3. Compute `b[k] = H[k] + k`, suffix mins `sm[i] = min_{k=i to n+1} b[k]`. Right: `-i + sm[i]`.
4. For `i=1 to n`: `val[i] = min(left[i], right[i])`.
5. Answer = `max(val[1..n])`.

This works because `min_j h[j] + |i-j| = min( min_{j≤i} h[j] + (i-j), min_{j≥i} h[j] + (j-i) )`, rewritable as above. Time: O(n), space: O(n).

## Example Problems
Here are three relevant competitive programming problems involving the same **max-min with absolute distance** algorithm (prefix/suffix on `a[j] ± j`). Each includes a description, detailed solution explanation, and correct Python3 code.

### Example Problem 1: "Deepest Point" (Hypothetical, similar to CF 429B/689C variants)
**Problem:** You have `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5) points on a line at positions 1 to n with "depths" `d_1, ..., d_n` (0 ≤ d_i ≤ 10^9). The "exposure" propagates from ends (depth 0 at 0 and n+1). Compute the maximum "buried depth" over all points i, defined as `min_j d_j + |i - j|` (j=0 to n+1, d_0=d_{n+1}=0).

**Solution:** Identical to the original: max_i min_j (d[j] + |i-j|). Use prefix/suffix mins on `d[j] - j` and `d[j] + j`. O(n).

**Python3 code:**
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
d = list(map(int, data[1:]))
H = [0] + d + [0]
N = n + 2
a = [H[k] - k for k in range(N)]
pm = [0] * N
pm[0] = a[0]
for k in range(1, N):
    pm[k] = min(pm[k-1], a[k])
b = [H[k] + k for k in range(N)]
sm = [0] * N
sm[n+1] = b[n+1]
for k in range(n, -1, -1):
    sm[k] = min(sm[k+1], b[k])
ans = 0
for i in range(1, n+1):
    leftv = i + pm[i]
    rightv = -i + sm[i]
    valv = min(leftv, rightv)
    ans = max(ans, valv)
print(ans)
```

### Example Problem 2: "Facility Placement" (Similar to AtCoder/ CF facility location problems, e.g., CF 1280E)
**Problem:** `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5) cities at positions 1..n. Building a facility at city j costs `c_j` (1 ≤ c_j ≤ 10^9), plus transport `|i - j|` to serve city i. Find the maximum over i of the minimum cost to serve i from any j (no ends). Output that max min-cost.

**Solution:** Now no virtual ends, so H = c (1 to n only). Compute max_i min_{j=1 to n} c[j] + |i-j| using same prefix/suffix on indices 1 to n (adjust ranges). Left: min_{j=1 to i} c[j] + (i-j) = i + min_{j=1 to i} (c[j]-j), etc. O(n).

**Python3 code:**
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index])
index += 1
c = [0] + [int(data[i]) for i in range(index, index + n)]  # 1-based
N = n + 1  # 0 unused, 1 to n
a = [0] * N
for k in range(1, N):
    a[k] = c[k] - k
pm = [float('inf')] * N
pm[1] = a[1]
for k in range(2, N):
    pm[k] = min(pm[k-1], a[k])
b = [0] * N
for k in range(1, N):
    b[k] = c[k] + k
sm = [float('inf')] * N
sm[n] = b[n]
for k in range(n-1, 0, -1):
    sm[k] = min(sm[k+1], b[k])
ans = 0
for i in range(1, n+1):
    leftv = i + pm[i]
    rightv = -i + sm[i]
    valv = min(leftv, rightv)
    ans = max(ans, valv)
print(ans)
```

### Example Problem 3: "Erosion Time" (Variant like Google Code Jam/ CF tower erosion, e.g., CF 165E)
**Problem:** `n` (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5) sand dunes with heights `h_i` (1 ≤ h_i ≤ 10^9). Each second, every dune loses 1 unit unless capped by min(neighbor heights). Ends are height 0. Compute seconds until all flat (0). (Exact same as original!)

**Solution:** Identical algorithm and proof. Demonstrates direct application.

**Python3 code:** (Same as original, included for completeness)
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
h = list(map(int, data[1:]))
H = [0] + h + [0]
N = n + 2
a = [H[k] - k for k in range(N)]
pm = [0] * N
pm[0] = a[0]
for k in range(1, N):
    pm[k] = min(pm[k-1], a[k])
b = [H[k] + k for k in range(N)]
sm = [0] * N
sm[N-1] = b[N-1]
for k in range(N-2, -1, -1):
    sm[k] = min(sm[k+1], b[k])
ans = 0
for i in range(1, n+1):
    leftv = i + pm[i]
    rightv = -i + sm[i]
    valv = min(leftv, rightv)
    ans = max(ans, valv)
print(ans)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
The solution computes `max_{i=1 to n} min_{j=0 to n+1} (h[j] + |i-j|)` where `h[0] = h[n+1] = 0` and `h[1..n]` are input heights. This exactly equals the number of operations, derived from the tight upper bound on remaining heights after `t` steps. Implemented in O(n) using prefix minima on `h[j] - j` (left) and suffix minima on `h[j] + j` (right), then taking `min(left, right)` per `i` and max over `i`.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

n = int(data[0])
h = list(map(int, data[1:1+n]))

# H[0] = 0, H[1..n] = h, H[n+1] = 0
H = [0] + h + [0]
N = n + 2  # indices 0 to n+1

# Left: i + min_{j=0 to i} (H[j] - j)
a = [H[k] - k for k in range(N)]
pm = [0] * N
pm[0] = a[0]
for k in range(1, N):
    pm[k] = min(pm[k - 1], a[k])

# Right: -i + min_{j=i to n+1} (H[j] + j)
b = [H[k] + k for k in range(N)]
sm = [0] * N
sm[n + 1] = b[n + 1]
for k in range(n, -1, -1):
    sm[k] = min(sm[k + 1], b[k])

ans = 0
for i in range(1, n + 1):
    leftv = i + pm[i]
    rightv = -i + sm[i]
    valv = min(leftv, rightv)
    if valv > ans:
        ans = valv

print(ans)
```

## Algorithms
- **Blocking configurations in 2xn grid**: Path existence is determined by the absence of specific minimal cuts that separate the start (1,1) from the end (2,n). These are: (1) any column where both cells are blocked, (2) any two consecutive columns where bottom(i) and top(i+1) are both blocked, (3) any two consecutive columns where top(i) and bottom(i+1) are both blocked. These are the only configurations that prevent crossing between columns i and i+1.
- **Dynamic counter maintenance**: Use boolean arrays to track blocked status per cell. Maintain three counters (`num_both`, `num_diag1`, `num_diag2`) for the number of each blocking type. Each toggle affects at most 3 counters (local to the column and adjacent columns), updated in O(1) time. Path exists if all counters are zero.
- **Time complexity**: O(n + q) preprocessing and updates, ideal for n, q ≤ 10^5.

## Tutorial
In a 2×n grid with obstacles (lava cells), finding a path from (1,1) to (2,n) after dynamic updates requires avoiding O(n) per-query pathfinding like BFS or DP, which would be too slow (O(nq) = 10^{10}).

**Key Insight**: Due to the grid's narrow width (2 rows), all possible paths must cross between every pair of consecutive columns (i to i+1). A cross is impossible exactly in these cases:
- **Full block** (column i both blocked): No entry to column i.
- **Diag1** (bottom i blocked ∧ top i+1 blocked): Cannot cross top (top i+1 blocked), cannot cross bottom (bottom i blocked), cannot switch rows at i (bottom i blocked) or i+1 (top i+1 blocked).
- **Diag2** (top i blocked ∧ bottom i+1 blocked): Symmetric blockage.

No other configurations block the path, as zigzagging is always possible otherwise (e.g., blocked top i and bottom i+2 allows switch at i+1).

**Maintenance**:
- Boolean arrays `blocked_top[1..n]`, `blocked_bot[1..n]` (initially False).
- Counters: `num_both`, `num_diag1` (bottom i & top i+1), `num_diag2` (top i & bottom i+1).
- On toggle row r, col c (flip blocked[r][c]):
  - Update `num_both` if the other row in c is blocked: Δ = +1 if now both blocked, -1 if now not.
  - Update affected diags (at most 2):
    | Toggle | Affected Diag1 | Affected Diag2 |
    |--------|----------------|----------------|
    | Top c  | i=c-1 (if c>1) | i=c (if c<n)  |
    | Bot c  | i=c (if c<n)   | i=c-1 (if c>1)|
    - For each: if partner blocked, Δ = +1 if now contributing, -1 otherwise.
- After update: "Yes" if all counters == 0, else "No".

This ensures O(1) per query. Note: (1,1) and (2,n) never toggled, always open.

**Proof of completeness**: Any blocking must hit a min-cut of size 2 across some column boundary; the listed configs are exactly those cuts.

## Example Problems
### Example Problem 1: Check for Adjacent 1s (Similar Local Counter Maintenance)
**Problem**: Given a 1×n binary array (initially all 0s), perform q flips on positions. After each flip, output "YES" if no two consecutive 1s exist, else "NO". (n,q ≤ 10^5)

**Solution**: Maintain a boolean array `a[1..n]` and counter `num_adj` (# of i where a[i]=a[i+1]=1). Flip affects at most 2 adj pairs (i-1 and i). Update counter locally: for flip at c, check pairs (c-1,c) and (c,c+1) before/after. "YES" if num_adj==0.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index]); index += 1
q = int(data[index]); index += 1
a = [False] * (n + 2)  # 1-indexed, sentinels
num_adj = 0
ans = []
for _ in range(q):
    c = int(data[index]); index += 1
    was = a[c]
    a[c] = not was
    # Check pair c-1, c
    if c > 1:
        partner_prev = a[c-1]
        if partner_prev:
            num_adj += 1 if not was else -1
    # Check pair c, c+1
    if c < n:
        partner_next = a[c+1]
        if partner_next:
            num_adj += 1 if not was else -1
    ans.append("YES" if num_adj == 0 else "NO")
print("\n".join(ans))
```

### Example Problem 2: Union-Find Path in Line Graph with Toggles
**Problem**: 1×n line graph (nodes 1 to n connected sequentially), start 1 to end n. q toggles remove/add nodes (except 1,n). After each, check if 1 connected to n. (n,q ≤ 10^5)

**Solution**: Path exists iff no blocked node i (1<i<n). But to generalize, maintain rightmost reachable from 1 using two pointers/sets, but efficient: use set of blocked positions, check max blocked <n and min gap. Actually, since line, blocked if exists blocked i with all left reachable but i blocks. Simple: path blocked iff exists any blocked i (2<=i<=n-1). Counter trivial. More relevant: maintain using segment tree max prefix reachable.

But for similarity: use counter of "blocks" (each blocked i=2..n-1 is a block). Toggle updates counter.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index]); index += 1
q = int(data[index]); index += 1
blocked = [False] * (n + 1)
num_blocks = 0
ans = []
for _ in range(q):
    c = int(data[index]); index += 1
    if 1 < c < n:
        was = blocked[c]
        blocked[c] = not was
        num_blocks += 1 if not was else -1
    ans.append("YES" if num_blocks == 0 else "NO")
print("\n".join(ans))
```

### Example Problem 3: No Matching Brackets in String with Flips
**Problem**: 1×n string of '(',')','.' initially. q flips on positions. After each, "YES" if no substring "()" (matching pair adjacent), else "NO". (n,q ≤ 10^5)

**Solution**: Similar to adj check. Maintain counter of adjacent "()" pairs. Flip affects left/right pairs. Local O(1) update.

```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index]); index += 1
q = int(data[index]); index += 1
s = ['.'] * (n + 2)  # 1-indexed
num_pairs = 0
ans = []
for _ in range(q):
    c, ch = int(data[index]), data[index+1]; index += 2
    prev_ch = s[c]
    s[c] = ch
    # Check left pair (c-1,c)
    if c > 1 and s[c-1] == '(' and prev_ch == ')':
        num_pairs -= 1
    if c > 1 and s[c-1] == '(' and ch == ')':
        num_pairs += 1
    # Check right pair (c,c+1)
    if c < n and prev_ch == '(' and s[c+1] == ')':
        num_pairs -= 1
    if c < n and ch == '(' and s[c+1] == ')':
        num_pairs += 1
    ans.append("YES" if num_pairs == 0 else "NO")
print("\n".join(ans))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**: Track blocked status with two boolean lists `blocked_top` and `blocked_bot`. Maintain three counters: `num_both` (# columns with both blocked), `num_diag1` (# i with bot[i] & top[i+1] blocked), `num_diag2` (# i with top[i] & bot[i+1] blocked). Each toggle flips one cell and updates at most three counters in O(1) by checking the relevant partner cells (same column or adjacent). Output "Yes" if all counters zero (no blocks), else "No". Handles edges (c=1,n) naturally. Uses fast input for efficiency.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
n = int(data[index])
index += 1
q = int(data[index])
index += 1

blocked_top = [False] * (n + 1)
blocked_bot = [False] * (n + 1)

num_both = 0
num_diag1 = 0  # bot[i] and top[i+1]
num_diag2 = 0  # top[i] and bot[i+1]

ans = []
for _ in range(q):
    r = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    c = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    
    if r == 1:  # toggle top c
        was = blocked_top[c]
        blocked_top[c] = not was
        # num_both
        if blocked_bot[c]:
            num_both += 1 if not was else -1
        # diag1 i = c-1: bot[c-1] & top[c]
        if c > 1 and blocked_bot[c - 1]:
            num_diag1 += 1 if not was else -1
        # diag2 i = c: top[c] & bot[c+1]
        if c < n and blocked_bot[c + 1]:
            num_diag2 += 1 if not was else -1
    else:  # toggle bot c
        was = blocked_bot[c]
        blocked_bot[c] = not was
        # num_both
        if blocked_top[c]:
            num_both += 1 if not was else -1
        # diag1 i = c: bot[c] & top[c+1]
        if c < n and blocked_top[c + 1]:
            num_diag1 += 1 if not was else -1
        # diag2 i = c-1: top[c-1] & bot[c]
        if c > 1 and blocked_top[c - 1]:
            num_diag2 += 1 if not was else -1
    
    if num_both == 0 and num_diag1 == 0 and num_diag2 == 0:
        ans.append("Yes")
    else:
        ans.append("No")

print("\n".join(ans))
```

## Algorithms
- **Sparse Table for Range Bitwise OR Queries**: Preprocess the array in O(n log n) time and O(n log n) space to answer the bitwise OR of any subarray [l, r] in O(1) time. This leverages the associativity and idempotence of the OR operation.
- **Binary Search for Plateau Detection**: For each left endpoint l, use binary search to find the farthest right endpoint where the subarray OR remains constant (a "plateau"). This exploits the monotonic non-decreasing nature of subarray ORs (OR[l..r] ≤ OR[l..r+1] bitwise).
- **Bounded Distinct Values per Starting Point**: For each fixed l, the OR changes at most O(log MAX_A) = O(20) times as r increases, because each change sets at least one new bit, and there are at most 20 relevant bits (since MAX_A ≤ 10^6 < 2^20).
- **Union via Set**: Collect all plateau OR values across all l into a set for O(1) amortized distinct count, with total insertions O(20n) = O(2×10^6).

Overall time: O(n log n + n × 20 × log n) = O(3×10^7) operations, efficient for n=10^5.

## Tutorial
Bitwise OR of subarrays has two key properties:
1. **Monotonicity**: Extending a subarray [l, r] to [l, r+1] can only keep the OR the same or set additional bits (OR[l..r+1] = OR[l..r] | a[r+1] ≥ OR[l..r]).
2. **Limited Changes**: For fixed l, as r increases from l to n, the OR value changes at most 20 times (each change increases the numeric value by setting new bits).

**Step 1: Build Sparse Table**
- `st[k][i]` = OR of subarray starting at i with length 2^k.
- Initialize `st[0][i] = a[i]`.
- For k=1 to logN: `st[k][i] = st[k-1][i] | st[k-1][i + 2^{k-1}]` for valid i.
- Query OR[l..r]: Let k = floor(log2(r-l+1)), return `st[k][l] | st[k][r - 2^k + 1]`.

**Step 2: Precompute Logarithms**
- `logs[len] = floor(log2(len))` for fast query k computation.

**Step 3: Enumerate Distinct ORs per Left Endpoint**
- For each l in 0..n-1:
  - Start with `current_or = a[l]`, add to set.
  - Set current r = l.
  - While r < n-1:
    - Binary search farthest rr where OR[l..rr] == current_or.
    - next_r = farthest + 1.
    - If next_r >= n, break.
    - new_or = OR[l..next_r], add to set.
    - Update current_or = new_or, r = next_r.
- Each l contributes ≤20 ORs; binary searches ensure efficiency.

This covers all possible subarray ORs (each [l,r] falls into one plateau for its l). Use a set for deduplication.

**Why Correct?** Every subarray OR[l..r] equals the OR of the plateau containing r for starting l. Time/space efficient due to bounds.

**Optimizations**: Fast input, bounded loops, precompute logs.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Longest OR-Stable Subarray Starting at Each Position
**Problem Description**:  
Given n (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5) and array a (0 ≤ a_i ≤ 10^6), for each starting index i (1-based), find the length of the longest subarray starting at i where the bitwise OR equals a_i. Output n space-separated integers.

**Solution Explanation**:  
Use sparse table for O(1) OR queries. For each l (0-based), binary search the farthest r ≥ l such that OR[l..r] == a[l]. The length is r - l + 1. Total time: O(n log n + n log n) since one binary search per l.

```python
import sys
import math

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
a = list(map(int, data[1:]))
LOG = 18
st = [[0] * n for _ in range(LOG)]
logs = [0] * (n + 2)
for i in range(2, n + 1):
    logs[i] = logs[i // 2] + 1

for i in range(n):
    st[0][i] = a[i]
for k in range(1, LOG):
    for i in range(n - (1 << k) + 1):
        st[k][i] = st[k - 1][i] | st[k - 1][i + (1 << (k - 1))]

def query(l, r):
    length = r - l + 1
    k = logs[length]
    return st[k][l] | st[k][r - (1 << k) + 1]

ans = []
for l in range(n):
    target = a[l]
    left, right = l, n - 1
    farthest = l - 1
    while left <= right:
        mid = (left + right) // 2
        if query(l, mid) == target:
            farthest = mid
            left = mid + 1
        else:
            right = mid - 1
    ans.append(str(farthest - l + 1))
print(' '.join(ans))
```

### Example Problem 2: Number of Subarrays Where OR Equals First Element
**Problem Description**:  
Same input as original (n ≤ 10^5, a_i ≤ 10^6). Output a single integer: the total number of subarrays [l, r] (1-based) such that OR[l..r] == a_l.

**Solution Explanation**:  
Build sparse table. For each l, binary search farthest r where OR[l..r] == a[l], add (farthest - l + 1) to total. Only checks first plateau per l. Time: O(n log n + n log n).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
a = list(map(int, data[1:]))
LOG = 18
st = [[0] * n for _ in range(LOG)]
logs = [0] * (n + 2)
for i in range(2, n + 1):
    logs[i] = logs[i // 2] + 1

for i in range(n):
    st[0][i] = a[i]
for k in range(1, LOG):
    for i in range(n - (1 << k) + 1):
        st[k][i] = st[k - 1][i] | st[k - 1][i + (1 << (k - 1))]

def query(l, r):
    length = r - l + 1
    k = logs[length]
    return st[k][l] | st[k][r - (1 << k) + 1]

total = 0
for l in range(n):
    target = a[l]
    left, right = l, n - 1
    farthest = l - 1
    while left <= right:
        mid = (left + right) // 2
        if query(l, mid) == target:
            farthest = mid
            left = mid + 1
        else:
            right = mid - 1
    total += farthest - l + 1
print(total)
```

### Example Problem 3: Number of Distinct Prefix ORs
**Problem Description**:  
Same input. Output the number of distinct values among prefix ORs OR[1..1], OR[1..2], ..., OR[1..n].

**Solution Explanation**:  
No sparse table needed (O(n) time). Compute prefix OR iteratively: prefix[0] = a[0], prefix[i] = prefix[i-1] | a[i]. Use a set to collect distinct values. Relies on same monotonicity (≤20 distinct total).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
a = list(map(int, data[1:]))
s = set()
cur = 0
for i in range(n):
    cur |= a[i]
    s.add(cur)
print(len(s))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
- **Explanation of the solution**:  
  Build a sparse table for O(1) range OR queries (O(n log n) preprocess). Precompute log2 for query speedup. For each left endpoint l, iteratively find OR plateaus using binary search: start with current_or = a[l], find farthest r with same OR, jump to next_r = farthest+1, compute new_or, repeat (≤20 times per l). Insert all such ORs into a set. The set size is the answer, as all subarray ORs are captured. Time: O(n log n + 20 n log n), space: O(n log n).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index])
index += 1
a = [int(data[i]) for i in range(index, index + n)]
LOG = 18
st = [[0] * n for _ in range(LOG)]
logs = [0] * (n + 2)
for i in range(2, n + 1):
    logs[i] = logs[i // 2] + 1

for i in range(n):
    st[0][i] = a[i]
for k in range(1, LOG):
    pw = 1 << k
    for i in range(n - pw + 1):
        st[k][i] = st[k - 1][i] | st[k - 1][i + (pw >> 1)]

def query(l, r):
    length = r - l + 1
    k = logs[length]
    pw = 1 << k
    return st[k][l] | st[k][r - pw + 1]

s = set()
for l in range(n):
    if n == 0:
        break
    current_or = a[l]
    s.add(current_or)
    r = l
    while r < n - 1:
        left = r + 1
        right = n - 1
        farthest = r
        while left <= right:
            mid = (left + right) // 2
            if query(l, mid) == current_or:
                farthest = mid
                left = mid + 1
            else:
                right = mid - 1
        next_r = farthest + 1
        if next_r >= n:
            break
        new_or = query(l, next_r)
        s.add(new_or)
        current_or = new_or
        r = next_r
print(len(s))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Generating Functions**: The problem is modeled as finding the coefficient of \(x^m\) in the generating function \(\prod_{k=0}^\infty \sum_{d=0}^7 x^{d \cdot 2^k}\), which simplifies dramatically to \(\frac{1}{(1-x)(1-x^2)(1-x^4)}\) due to telescoping products.
- **Combinatorial Interpretation**: This generating function counts the number of non-negative integer solutions to \(a + 2b + 4c = m\).
- **Closed-Form Formula Derivation**: By enumerating solutions via summation over \(c\), the count simplifies to a direct formula: \(f(m) = (C+1) \times (H - C + 1)\), where \(C = \lfloor m/4 \rfloor\) and \(H = \lfloor m/2 \rfloor\), computed modulo \(10^9+7\).
- **Efficient Large Integer Arithmetic**: Python's arbitrary-precision integers handle multiplications up to \(O(10^{36})\) and modulo operations efficiently for \(10^5\) queries with \(m \leq 10^{18}\).

No dynamic programming, matrix exponentiation, or digit DP is needed due to the closed form, enabling \(O(1)\) time per query after input.

## Tutorial
### Generating Functions for Digit Representations
In problems counting polynomials \(P(b) = m\) with coefficients in a set \(D\) (here \(D = \{0,1,\dots,7\}\), \(b=2\)), the generating function is \(G(x) = \prod_{k=0}^\infty \left( \sum_{d \in D} x^{d \cdot b^k} \right)\). The answer is \([x^m] G(x)\).

For \(D = \{0,\dots,r-1\}\), \( \sum_{d=0}^{r-1} x^{d \cdot b^k} = \frac{1 - x^{r \cdot b^k}}{1 - x^{b^k}} \). Here, \(r=8\), \(b=2\), so \(G(x) = \prod_{k=0}^\infty \frac{1 - x^{2^{k+3}}}{1 - x^{2^k}}\).

This telescopes: the numerator shifts indices, canceling all but the first three denominator terms, yielding \(G(x) = \frac{1}{(1-x)(1-x^2)(1-x^4)}\).

### Combinatorial Equivalence
\(\frac{1}{(1-x)(1-x^2)(1-x^4)} = \sum_{a=0}^\infty x^a \cdot \sum_{b=0}^\infty x^{2b} \cdot \sum_{c=0}^\infty x^{4c}\), so \([x^m] G(x)\) is the number of \(a,b,c \geq 0\) with \(a + 2b + 4c = m\).

### Deriving the Closed Form
Fix \(c = 0\) to \(\lfloor m/4 \rfloor = C\). For each \(c\), let \(r = m - 4c\); then \(b = 0\) to \(\lfloor r/2 \rfloor\), giving \(\lfloor r/2 \rfloor + 1\) choices (one \(a\) each). Thus,
\[
f(m) = \sum_{c=0}^C \left( \lfloor (m - 4c)/2 \rfloor + 1 \right) = (C+1) + \sum_{c=0}^C \lfloor (m - 4c)/2 \rfloor.
\]
Key identity: \(\lfloor (m - 4c)/2 \rfloor = \lfloor m/2 \rfloor - 2c\) (since \(4c/2 = 2c\) is integer and the floor function subtracts integers correctly, verified to stay non-negative up to \(C\)).

Sum: \(\sum_{c=0}^C (\lfloor m/2 \rfloor - 2c) = (C+1) \lfloor m/2 \rfloor - 2 \cdot \frac{C(C+1)}{2} = (C+1) H - C(C+1)\), where \(H = \lfloor m/2 \rfloor\).

Thus, \(f(m) = (C+1) + (C+1) H - C(C+1) = (C+1)(H - C + 1)\).

### Implementation Notes
- Compute floor divisions with `//` in Python.
- For \(m \leq 10^{18}\), products are up to \(\sim 10^{36}\); Python handles `a * b % MOD` natively.
- Use fast input (`sys.stdin.read()`) for \(t = 5 \times 10^5\).

This approach generalizes to other small-degree rational generating functions via similar telescoping or partial fractions.

## Example Problems

### Example Problem 1: Ways to Make n with Coins 1 and 2 (Codeforces-like)
**Problem**: Given \(t\) (\(1 \leq t \leq 10^5\)) queries, each with \(n_i\) (\(1 \leq n_i \leq 10^{18}\)), compute the number of non-negative integer solutions to \(x + 2y = n_i\) modulo \(10^9+7\).

**Solution Explanation**: Fix \(y = 0\) to \(\lfloor n/2 \rfloor\); for each, one \(x = n - 2y \geq 0\). Number of \(y\) is \(\lfloor n/2 \rfloor + 1\). Closed form: \(f(n) = \lfloor n/2 \rfloor + 1\). \(O(1)\) per query.

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
t = int(data[0])
queries = [int(x) for x in data[1:]]
MOD = 10**9 + 7

for n in queries:
    ways = (n // 2) + 1
    print(ways % MOD)
```

### Example Problem 2: Ways to Make n with Coins 1, 2, 3 (AtCoder-like)
**Problem**: Given \(t\) (\(1 \leq t \leq 10^5\)) queries, each with \(n_i\) (\(1 \leq n_i \leq 10^{18}\)), compute the number of non-negative integer solutions to \(x + 2y + 3z = n_i\) modulo \(10^9+7\).

**Solution Explanation**: Sum over \(z = 0\) to \(\lfloor n/3 \rfloor = C\). For each \(z\), remaining \(r = n - 3z\), ways = \(\lfloor r/2 \rfloor + 1\). Key: \(\lfloor (n-3z)/2 \rfloor = \lfloor n/2 \rfloor - \lfloor (3z)/2 \rfloor\), but group by \(n \mod 6\) for closed form. Actual closed form derived similarly: \(f(n) = \frac{(n+3)^2}{12}\) rounded appropriately (exact formula per residue class mod 6). For simplicity, use the general sum simplification: after verification, \(f(n) = \round{\frac{(n+3)^2}{12}}\) (works for all n).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
t = int(data[0])
queries = [int(x) for x in data[1:]]
MOD = 10**9 + 7

for n in queries:
    # Closed form: round( (n+3)**2 / 12 )
    tmp = (n + 3) * (n + 3)
    ways = (tmp + 6) // 12  # Equivalent to round(tmp / 12)
    print(ways % MOD)
```

### Example Problem 3: Restricted Base-b Representations (Codeforces-like)
**Problem**: Given \(t\) (\(1 \leq t \leq 10^5\)) queries, each with \(m_i\) (\(1 \leq m_i \leq 10^{18}\)), compute the number of polynomials \(P\) with coefficients in \(\{0,1,2\}\) such that \(P(2) = m_i\), modulo \(10^9+7\).

**Solution Explanation**: GF \(\prod_k \sum_{d=0}^2 x^{d 2^k} = \prod_k \frac{1 - x^{3 \cdot 2^k}}{1 - x^{2^k}} = \frac{1}{(1-x)(1-x^2)(1-x^4)}\) (telescopes to same as original, since 3*2^k = 2^k + 2^{k+1}). Wait, actually for r=3, it telescopes to 1/((1-x)(1-x^2)). Coeff is \(\lfloor (n+2)/2 \rfloor + something? Standard: solutions to a+2b=n, which is floor(n/2)+1. (Note: smaller case of original.)

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
t = int(data[0])
queries = [int(x) for x in data[1:]]
MOD = 10**9 + 7

for n in queries:
    ways = (n // 2) + 1
    print(ways % MOD)
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
The number of polynomials is the coefficient of \(x^m\) in \(\frac{1}{(1-x)(1-x^2)(1-x^4)}\), equal to the number of \(a,b,c \geq 0\) with \(a + 2b + 4c = m\). This sums to the closed form \(f(m) = (C+1) \times (H - C + 1) \mod (10^9+7)\), where \(C = \lfloor m/4 \rfloor\), \(H = \lfloor m/2 \rfloor\). Verified correct via samples and small cases. Use fast input for large \(t\).

```python
import sys

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
t = int(data[index])
index += 1

MOD = 10**9 + 7
answers = []

for _ in range(t):
    m = int(data[index])
    index += 1
    C = m // 4
    H = m // 2
    ways = ((C + 1) * (H - C + 1)) % MOD
    answers.append(str(ways))

print('\n'.join(answers))
```

## Algorithms
The core concepts and algorithms used to solve this problem are:
- **Game Theory on Graphs with Draws**: Model the game as a directed graph where nodes are game states (distance to win modulo n, player to move). Outcomes are Win (current player forces win), Lose (opponent forces win), or Loop (draw via infinite play). Players prefer Loop over Lose.
- **State Reduction**: Represent positions by clockwise distance `d` (1 to n-1) to the black hole (0). A move `x` transitions to `(d - x) % n`; landing on 0 is an immediate Win.
- **Partizan Game Classification**: Two outcome arrays (`outcome1` for Rick-to-move, `outcome2` for Morty-to-move). Use monotonic propagation (BFS-like queue) to classify states:
  - Direct Wins: `d in set` (move to 0).
  - Propagate Wins: Exists move to opponent's Lose.
  - Propagate Loses: All moves to opponent's Win (tracked via safe move counts).
- **Efficient Propagation**: Use counters (`safe_count` for non-Win opponent moves, `num_win_moves` for Lose opponent moves) and reverse edges (predecessors via `(pos + s) % n`) to update in O(n * max(k1, k2)) time total, avoiding explicit graph storage.
This handles cycles by only marking forced finite wins/losses; unmarked states are Loops.

## Tutorial
### Problem Modeling
- Renumber states: Distance `d` (1 to n-1) to black hole. Original position `p = i+1` maps to `d = n - i`.
- Transition: From `d`, player moves `x` to `e = (d - x) % n`. If `e == 0`, immediate Win.
- Two state types: Rick-turn (`outcome1[d]`), Morty-turn (`outcome2[d]`).
- Optimal play: Current player Wins if can force Win; Loses if opponent forces Win; else Loop (prefers Loop over Lose).

### Classification Rules
- **Win**: Direct to 0 (`d in set`) OR exists move to opponent's Lose.
- **Lose**: No direct Win AND all moves to opponent's Win.
- **Loop**: Neither (has move to opponent's Loop).

### Algorithm: Queue-Based Propagation
1. Initialize:
   - Mark direct Wins (`d in S1_set` → `outcome1[d]="Win"`, enqueue `(1,d)`; similarly for Morty).
   - For undecided `d`: `safe[d] = k` (all moves initially "safe"), `num_win[d] = 0`.
2. Process queue:
   - For marked `(p, pos)` (p=1 Rick, p=2 Morty):
     - Status = outcome_p[pos].
     - Predecessors (opponent states reaching `pos`): For each `s` in opponent_set, `pred_d = (pos + s) % n`; skip if `pred_d == 0` or decided.
     - If status == "Win": Decrement `safe[pred_d]`; if `safe[pred_d] == 0`, mark opponent `pred_d` "Lose", enqueue.
     - If status == "Lose": Increment `num_win[pred_d]`; if `num_win[pred_d] == 1`, mark opponent `pred_d` "Win", enqueue.
3. Unmarked states → "Loop".
- **Correctness**: Monotonic (new Wins/Loses only add classifications); processes finite forced wins (cycles stay Loop).
- **Time/Space**: O(n + ∑ k) preprocessing; propagation O(2n * max(k)) = O(n^2) worst-case, fine for n=7000.
- **Mapping**: Output `outcome1[n-i]` for i=1..n-1 (Rick first); similarly `outcome2`.

This is a standard technique for impartial/partizan games with draws on dense graphs (e.g., circulant graphs).

### Example 1: Codeforces 1420E - Game with Cards (Graph Game with Draws)
**Problem Description**: n positions in a line/cycle-like, two players with move sets, determine win/lose/draw for starting positions. Similar modular moves, but line; need to compute forced wins via propagation.
**Solution Explanation**: Model states as position + turn. Use queue to propagate Wins (move to opp Lose) and Loses (all to opp Win), with counters for efficiency. Undecided = Draw. Similar to here, but acyclic (line).

```python
# Simplified Python3 code for illustration (full problem has more constraints)

# Assume input parsed: n, moves1, moves2
```

### Example 2: AtCoder ABC 228 F - Impartial Game on Cycle (Subtraction Modulo)
**Problem Description**: n stones in circle, players subtract from set S (shared), last move wins. Compute grundy or win/lose for each starting size mod n, detect draws via cycles.
**Solution Explanation**: Use mex for grundy, but for draws, propagate as above (Win if move to 0 or opp mex=0; Lose if all opp mex!=0? Adapt to single outcome array. Queue from terminals, count safe moves.
```python
# Python3 code
n = int(input())
S = list(map(int, input().split()))
S_set = set(S)
outcome = [""] * n
queue = deque()
for d in range(1, n):
    if d in S_set:
        outcome[d] = "Win"
        queue.append(d)
safe = [len(S)] * n
for d in range(1, n):
    if outcome[d] == "":
        safe[d] = len(S)
while queue:
    pos = queue.popleft()
    status = outcome[pos]
    for s in S:
        pred = (pos + s) % n
        if pred == 0 or outcome[pred] != "":
            continue
        if status == "Win":
            safe[pred] -= 1
            if safe[pred] == 0:
                outcome[pred] = "Lose"
                queue.append(pred)
        # Add Lose prop similar
for d in range(1, n):
    if outcome[d] == "": outcome[d] = "Loop"
print(' '.join(outcome[1:]))
```

## Python3 code to solve the original problem
### Explanation of the solution
- **Modeling**: States `d=1` to `n-1` (dist to 1). Moves: `e = (d - s) % n`.
- **Data Structures**: `outcome1/2` (strings), `safe1/2` (int counts), `numwin1/2` (int counts).
- **Initialization**: Mark direct Wins (`d in set`), enqueue `(player, d)`. Undecided: `safe = k`, `numwin=0`.
- **Propagation**: BFS queue processes marked states. For each, generate opponent predecessors `pred_d = (pos + s) % n`. Update counts:
  - New opp "Win" → decrement pred safe; if 0 → mark pred "Lose".
  - New opp "Lose" → increment pred numwin; if 1 → mark pred "Win".
- **Post-process**: Unmarked → "Loop".
- **Output**: Map back via `n - i` for positions 2 to n.
- **Complexity**: O(n^2) time (acceptable), O(n) space.
- Matches samples; handles preferences (Loop > Lose).

```python
import sys
from collections import deque

input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()

index = 0
n = int(data[index])
index += 1

k1 = int(data[index])
index += 1
S1 = [int(data[i]) for i in range(index, index + k1)]
index += k1
S1_set = set(S1)

k2 = int(data[index])
index += 1
S2 = [int(data[i]) for i in range(index, index + k2)]
S2_set = set(S2)

outcome1 = [""] * n
outcome2 = [""] * n
safe1 = [0] * n
numwin1 = [0] * n
safe2 = [0] * n
numwin2 = [0] * n

queue = deque()

for d in range(1, n):
    if d in S1_set:
        outcome1[d] = "Win"
        queue.append((1, d))
    else:
        safe1[d] = k1
        numwin1[d] = 0
    if d in S2_set:
        outcome2[d] = "Win"
        queue.append((2, d))
    else:
        safe2[d] = k2
        numwin2[d] = 0

while queue:
    p, pos = queue.popleft()
    if p == 1:
        status = outcome1[pos]
        opp_set = S2
    else:
        status = outcome2[pos]
        opp_set = S1

    for s in opp_set:
        pred_d = (pos + s) % n
        if pred_d == 0:
            continue
        pred_player = 3 - p
        pred_outcome = outcome1 if pred_player == 1 else outcome2
        if pred_outcome[pred_d] != "":
            continue
        if status == "Win":
            if pred_player == 1:
                safe1[pred_d] -= 1
                if safe1[pred_d] == 0:
                    outcome1[pred_d] = "Lose"
                    queue.append((1, pred_d))
            else:
                safe2[pred_d] -= 1
                if safe2[pred_d] == 0:
                    outcome2[pred_d] = "Lose"
                    queue.append((2, pred_d))
        elif status == "Lose":
            if pred_player == 1:
                numwin1[pred_d] += 1
                if numwin1[pred_d] == 1:
                    outcome1[pred_d] = "Win"
                    queue.append((1, pred_d))
            else:
                numwin2[pred_d] += 1
                if numwin2[pred_d] == 1:
                    outcome2[pred_d] = "Win"
                    queue.append((2, pred_d))

for d in range(1, n):
    if outcome1[d] == "":
        outcome1[d] = "Loop"
    if outcome2[d] == "":
        outcome2[d] = "Loop"

# Output first line: Rick first, dist n-1 to 1
res1 = [outcome1[n - i] for i in range(1, n)]
print(" ".join(res1))

# Second line: Morty first
res2 = [outcome2[n - i] for i in range(1, n)]
print(" ".join(res2))
```

## Algorithms
The core concept is **maximum overlap (or maximum concurrency) in resource scheduling**. Specifically:
- Model the problem as 7 fixed time slots (discrete timeline).
- For each time slot, compute the number of groups (events) active (having a class).
- The minimum rooms required equals the **maximum** number of active groups across all slots, as this is the bottleneck (peak load). Rooms can be reused across non-overlapping slots.
- This is a special case of the **sweep line algorithm** or **histogram maximum** for interval scheduling, simplified due to fixed discrete slots (no need for sorting intervals).
- Time complexity: O(7 * n) = O(n), optimal for n ≤ 1000.

No graph coloring or assignment needed; it's purely peak-load computation.

## Tutorial
### Core Idea: Peak Load in Scheduling
In scheduling problems (e.g., rooms, platforms, servers), the minimum resources = maximum simultaneous demand.
- **Discrete case** (like this): Fixed time slots → Count active events per slot → Take max.
  - Efficient for small number of slots (e.g., 7 days).
- **Continuous case** (intervals [start, end)): Use sweep line:
  1. Collect all start/end events, sort by time (starts before ends if tie).
  2. Sweep: +1 at start, -1 at end → Track current/max active.
- Or: Sort starts/departs, two-pointers to simulate overlaps.
- **Why it works**: Resources are fungible (any room for any group in a slot); bottleneck is per-slot max.
- Edge cases: All groups idle (0 rooms), all overlap in one slot (n rooms), staggered (1 room).
- Extensions: Weighted loads, multi-resources → Max flow or greedy.

**Steps for this problem**:
1. Read n and n binary strings (schedules).
2. For each slot 0-6: Sum `int(schedule[i][slot])` over i=0 to n-1.
3. Output max sum.

**General tips**:
- Use arrays/histograms for discrete times.
- Prefix sums for cumulative if needed.
- Practice: Visualize as 7 histograms, find tallest bar.

## Example Problems
### Example 1: Minimum Platforms Required for Trains (Classic, e.g., GeeksforGeeks/InterviewBit)
**Problem**: Given arrival and departure times of n trains (1 ≤ n ≤ 10^5), find minimum platforms needed at a station so no train waits. Input: First line n, second line n arrivals (sorted? no), third line n departures.

**Solution**: Sort arrivals and departures. Use two pointers to find max overlap:
- Initialize i=0 (arr), j=0 (dep), plat=1, max_plat=1.
- While i<n and j<n:
  - If arr[i] <= dep[j]: i++, plat++ (new train arrives).
  - Else: j++, plat-- (train departs).
  - max_plat = max(max_plat, plat).
- Handles continuous time; O(n log n) sort.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
index = 0
n = int(data[index])
index += 1
arr = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(n)]
index += n
dep = [int(data[index + i]) for i in range(n)]

arr.sort()
dep.sort()
i = j = 0
plat = max_plat = 1
while i < n and j < n:
    if arr[i] <= dep[j]:
        i += 1
        plat += 1
        max_plat = max(max_plat, plat)
    else:
        j += 1
        plat -= 1
print(max_plat)
```

### Example 2: LeetCode 253. Meeting Rooms II
**Problem**: Given n intervals `[[start1,end1], [start2,end2], ...]` (0 ≤ n ≤ 10^4), return min meeting rooms needed. Intervals may overlap arbitrarily.

**Solution**: Priority queue (min-heap) for end times:
- Sort intervals by start.
- Heap stores end times of allocated rooms.
- For each interval: If heap's earliest end <= current start, pop and push new end (reuse room). Else, push new end.
- Final heap size = min rooms.
- O(n log n) time.

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import heapq
def minMeetingRooms(intervals):
    if not intervals:
        return 0
    intervals.sort(key=lambda x: x[0])
    heap = []
    for s, e in intervals:
        if heap and heap[0] <= s:
            heapq.heappop(heap)
        heapq.heappush(heap, e)
    return len(heap)

# For stdin: assume first line n, then n lines "s e"
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
intervals = []
index = 1
for i in range(n):
    s, e = int(data[index]), int(data[index+1])
    intervals.append([s, e])
    index += 2
print(minMeetingRooms(intervals))
```

### Example 3: Codeforces 598A (Tricky Sum, but adapted to scheduling; similar peak load: CF 1354C or discrete slots like AtCoder ABC)
**Problem** (Custom similar: "Class Scheduling" discrete): n=100 groups, each with 24-bit string (daily slots 1-24). Find min classrooms for one day. (Direct analog.)

**Solution**: Identical to original: Count per slot 0-23, take max. O(24*n).

**Python3 Code**:
```python
import sys
input = sys.stdin.read
data = input().split()
n = int(data[0])
schedules = data[1:1+n]
SLOTS = 24  # Assume 24 slots
max_rooms = 0
for slot in range(SLOTS):
    count = sum(int(sch[slot]) for sch in schedules)
    max_rooms = max(max_rooms, count)
print(max_rooms)
```
